Summary 0:
Transparency and investor protection: marketing communications for collective investment undertakings (UCITS)
Transparency, investor protection and investor participation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on the transparency, investor participation and participation in marketing communications of collective investment undertaking undertakings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the transparency and investor involvement in the marketing communications and information exchange of investment funds.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
alternative investment fund managers, including alternative investment funds;
UCITS management companies, including any UCITS which has not designated a UCITS management company;
euVECA managers;
EuSEF managers.
It does not apply to the following:
EU countries’ national laws, regulations and administrative provisions applicable to marketing communications.
The European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) must publish the texts on their websites in a language customary in the sphere of international finance, including their non-official summaries which would allow investors to get a broad overview of those laws and regulations.
Directive 2014/65/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 15 May 2014 on markets in financial instruments and amending Directive 2002/92/EC and Directive 2011/61/EU (OJ L 173, 12.6.2014, pp. 349-349).
The Regulation applies from 2 August 2021. It repeals Directive 2014/64/EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
 
It has applied since 2 August 2014 and had to become law in the EU countries by 2 August 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s financial markets are governed by a number of rules.
For more information, see:
European Investment Bank and European Securities & Markets Authority website
European Commission website.

Summary 1:
EU market surveillance — products subject to EU harmonisation
EU markets surveillance — goods subject to Union law
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/745 — EU market surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on market surveillance to ensure that products covered by EU law are applied correctly.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to products that are subject to the EU’s harmonisation legislation on products. It does not apply to:
products that are imported from a non-EU country;
products imported into the EU from outside the EU.
EU countries must designate their own market surveillance authorities to carry out market surveillance. They must also designate a single liaison office to assist in the cooperation between the authorities in different EU countries, as set out in Chapter VI.
The European Commission is responsible for coordinating the activities of these authorities and ensuring that they carry out their tasks effectively and effectively.
Rules and procedures for market surveillance and liaison offices
Each EU country must designate a market surveillance authority and a liaison office. These authorities are responsible for:
monitoring compliance with EU law;
assisting the Commission in the implementation of the legislation;
providing information to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Committee on the Functioning of the European Union (EFTA).
The Commission must ensure that market surveillance is carried out in accordance with the rules and procedures laid down in Regulation (EC) No 1223/2009 on the harmonisation of EU law on the importation of goods.
FROMENTRY INTO FORCE
This regulation applies from 1 January 2021. However, Articles 29, 30, 31, 32, 33 and 36 shall apply from 1.1.202101.

Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Summary 2:
Radio and television broadcasting rights — licensing
Radio and TV broadcasting rights – licensing
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/865 on licensing of broadcasting rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to improve the licensing of the broadcasting rights of collective management organisations (collective management organisations) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The directive sets out the licensing rules for the broadcasting of radio and television programmes.
It does not apply to:
radio programmes;
television programmes; and
recast services.
Licensing rules
EU countries must ensure that collective management organisation (MMO) or organisations managing broadcasting rights have the right to grant or refuse the authorisation for a retransmission of a programme.
They must also ensure that:
the MMO has the necessary financial resources to carry out its tasks effectively;
the rights are not transferred to a third party (e.g. the European Broadcasting Union (EBU) or a non-EU country’s national broadcaster;
there is no need to simplify the licensing process with regard to rights held by broadcasting organisations.
The licensing rules apply to the following categories of rights:
audio-visual broadcasting services (i.e. television programmes, radio programmes and retransmissions of radio programmes);
recasting services (radio programmes); and
retransmission services (television and radio programmes).
Collective management organisation or organisations:
may manage the rights of certain categories of users, such as those who live in an EU country other than their EU country of origin.
In the case of broadcasting organisations, the MMO may manage rights of users who live outside the EU country where the broadcasting organisation or organisation manages them. However, where more than one MMO manages rights of that category for the territory of that EU country, it shall be for the Member State for which the operator of a radio or television service seeks to clear rights for a re-transmission to decide which MMO or organisation has the right granting or refusing authorisation.
Where more than 1 MMO/EU country manages rights for the same category of user, it will be for that country to decide whether to grant, refuse or grant authorisation to the retransmitter.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It applies from 7 June 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 7 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of this directive.
For more information, see:
Radio broadcasting rights (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
  The European Commission adopts Directive (EU)* 2017/884 on the licensing and management of the rights to broadcast programmes (Radio and Television Directive).
Successive amendments to Directive (EC) No 865/2017 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 3:
Registration and revocation of domain names in the.eu top-level domain (tLDN)
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the registration and revocation procedures for domain names
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 184/2011 — registration and revoking procedures for the registration of.eu Top Level Domain Name (TLD)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules governing the registration, revocation and licensing of top level domain names (‘tLDs’) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the.eu TLD, which is one of the largest country code TLDs (ccTLDs) in Europe;
the EU countries’ national registrars (Registry and Registrars);
the European Commission.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 733/2002.
Registry
The.eu Registry is a not-for-profit organisation established in the EU and is responsible for registering and licensing top level domains. It is managed by the European Commission and is based in Luxembourg.
The registry is managed and licensed by the Commission. It has the following tasks:
register domain names;
register them;
receive and register them; and
reward them.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
It entered into force on 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission adopted delegated acts in the field of TLD regulation in order to ensure the proper functioning of the TLDN market.
For more information, see:
‘TLD regulation’ on the Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Decision (EU), Euratom of 27 June 2011 on the establishment of the registry and revocable domain names for the top level.eu.
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 4:
The European Connecting Europe Facility (CEF) 2018-2021
The European connecting Europe Facility 2018-2020
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/1046 establishing the European Connectivity Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE CEF?
This decision sets up the European Community Connectivity Facility, the EU’s digital connectivity fund.
The CEF aims to improve the free movement of persons, goods, capital and services in the European Union (EU). It also aims to contribute to the digital single market and the digital transformation of the economy and society.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision establishes the CEF for the period from 1 January 2018 to 31 December 2020.
It covers the following actions:
digital connectivity networks that are connected to the internet, which are intrinsically trans-European, which is due mainly to the functioning of the applications and services which they enable, priority for support.
EU countries’ efforts to ensure that these networks are open to all users, irrespective of their nationality or place of residence. These include:
improving the speed of the internet and the availability of information and communication technologies (ICTs) for the digital economy;
providing access to the market for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in the EU;
developing and improving the quality of the information and communications technology (ICT) infrastructure for the internet;
creating a single European market for SMEsports, e-commerce and e-mobility;
enhancing the mobility of goods and services between EU countries.
Eligible actions
The following actions are eligible for CEF funding:
telecommunications networks:
internet access for all users;
access to the single market for all SMEs;
transport networks for all consumers;
internet infrastructure for all businesses;
information and communication technology for all workers;
telematics infrastructure for enterprises;
infrastructure for the social economy.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
The funding entered into force on 1 January 2019.

Summary 5:
Removal orders for terrorist material on the internet: combating online terrorism
Removals from the online environment: combating terrorist material
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/865 on the enforcement of removal orders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the removal of terrorist material from the internet is effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
It sets out rules on how the removal order procedure works.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
the removal order is issued by the competent authority of the country in which the person concerned is a national of an EU country;
the contact points for the transmission of the order are located in the country of origin of the individual concerned.
The contact points must be established in the territory of the EU country of destination. The contact points should serve only for operational purposes.
When issuing a removal order, the authority must give due consideration to any notification of an interference with an investigative interest (deconfliction).
The competent authority must also take into account the freedom of expression, including the freedom to receive and impart information and ideas in an open and democratic society and the freedom and pluralism of the media.
If the contact point is located in a non-EU country, it must be able to transmit the order to the contact points of the countries in which it is issued.
Where a contact point in another EU country is located, the competent authorities must inform that country’s national authorities of the reason for issuing the order.
In addition to the list of contact points, EU countries must also inform the contact centres in which they are located.
National authorities must be informed of the reasons for the decision to issue the order, including:
information on the nature of the investigative interest,
the nature and duration of the investigation and
the content of the communication.
Removal order procedure
The removal order must be issued in the language of the national authorities.
A contact point must be set up in each EU country.
Contact points must also be located in countries where there is a serious threat to public order or public security.
Communication from contact points to the relevant national authorities
The national authorities must communicate the information they have received to contact the relevant contact points in other EU countries.
Information must be sent in a language that is easily understood and understandable to all users.
Directive 2014/33/EU on combating terrorism on the European Commission website
The directive sets out measures to combat terrorist content on the Internet. These measures include:
measures to prevent the dissemination of terrorist content online;
implementing measures to address infringements of Article 3(3) and (6), Article 4(2) and 7, Article 5(1), (2), (3), (5) and 6), Articles 6, 7, 10 and 11, Article 14(5), Article 15(1) and Article 17.
Implementing Measures to address the Dissemination of Terrorist CONTENT Online
The regulation sets out the measures to be taken by national authorities to tackle terrorist content uploaded to the internet. It also lays down rules on:
removal orders;
procedures to be followed to remove terrorist content from the Internet;
exchanges of information between national authorities, hosting service providers, users and users’ trust in online environment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing measures to tackle terrorism, including Directive 2014/34/EU, which aims to improve the functioning of the digital single market and to protect the rights of users and the security of the internet environment. The regulation is one of these measures.
For more information, see:
‘Fighting terrorism’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027: Horizon Europe (FSPR)
Framework programme for Research & Innovation (FPS)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2021/2027/EU on Horizon Europe
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out the framework programme for research and innovation (R&I) under Horizon Europe, the European Union’s (EU) general budget.
It aims to deliver scientific, technological, economic, environmental and societal impact in pursuit of this general objective and to maximise the added value of the EU's R&I investments through Horizon Europe.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The FSPR covers the period from 2021 to 2027.
The programme is funded by the general budget of the Union. It is divided into 4 priority areas:
science and technology (S&T)
energy and mobility (e.g. smart cities);
agriculture and rural development (e-mobility);
environmental and sustainable urban development (i.e. climate change and smart cities).
The Framework Programme covers the following priority areas.
Science and technology:
research and technological development (R & T)
The European Research Council (ERC) and the European Commission are responsible for the implementation of the programme.
E-Mobility: the programme aims to improve the mobility of people, goods and services in the EU by:
increasing mobility;
improving the efficiency of transport systems;
increasing the efficiency and effectiveness of the information society
increasing access to information and communication technologies (ICT);
enhancing the competitiveness of the labour market.
Energy and mobility: the European research and development (EU research and technological innovation (EU R&T).
Environment: the EU aims to reduce energy consumption and carbon emissions.
Smart cities: the Framework Programme supports the development of smart cities, including:
smart cities;
smart grids and smart grids;
energy efficiency and smart mobility.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
   
Directives 2021/2020/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 18 July 2021 on Horizon European Research and Innovative Programme 2021-2020 and repealing Decision No 541/2014/EU and Decision No 283/2018/EU (OJ L 193, 30.7.2021, pp. 1-8)
Commission Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 of 19 July 2018 on the financial rules applicable to Horizon Europe 2021–2027 and repealing Council Decision No 966/2012.
RELATED DOCUMENT

Summary 7:
Protecting the security of the European Union’s Galileo and Copernicus satellite systems
Protecting and maintaining Galileo and COPernicus satellites
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1139 on the security and management of European Union space systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union Agency for Space Systems (ESA).
It aims to protect and maintain the security, integrity and confidentiality of the systems and their services of the Galileo satellite system.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ESA is responsible for:
operating the Galileo space system and its services;
monitoring and monitoring the systems’ security;
providing technical assistance to the European Commission;
assisting the European Space Agency (ESA) and the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA) in the field of civil aviation safety.
It is also responsible for the European contribution to the Global Earth Observation System of Systems (GESS) and for the Copernicicus satellite system, as well as for the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change.
EU funding
ESA is funded by the EU budget. It has a budget of €2.8 billion for the period 2018-2022.
The Agency’S budget is divided between ESA and the Commission. It is responsible, in particular, for the following:
the European Union budget for the Galileo programme;
the EU funding for the GESS;
EU funds for the EU contribution to global Earth observation system of systems (Copernicus;
1.5 billion;
2.3 billion for GESA funding for Copernica.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLACE
The regulation has applied since 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Galileo’ on the ESA website.
* KEY TERMS
* Security: a system or system or service that is designed and operated to protect, monitor and manage the systems or services of a satellite system or services.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Regulation (EU: 2019/852/EU of 1 July 2019 on the establishment of the ESA and repealing Regulation (EC) No 552/2004
Successive amendments and corrections to the regulation have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 8:
Programme for the development of high performance computing and artificial intelligence (HPC) in Europe
Programmes for the Development of High Performance Computing and Artificial Intelligence (AI)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2020/2093 establishing the European Union (EU) programme for the implementation of the specific objectives of the Specific Objective 1–High Performance Computing, Artificial Intelligence and Advanced Digital Skills
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
The regulation establishes the specific objective of the EU programme ‘Specific Objective 1’ for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
The specific objectives are to:
develop high-performance computing* and advanced digital skills;
developing capacity related to artificial intelligence* and cybersecurity;
provide technical assistance to the private sector and the public sector.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The programme has 5 specific objectives:
1.
High performance computing
The objective of this programme is to improve the performance of high-performing computing systems, particularly in the field of artificial intelligence.
2.
Artificial intelligence
The objectives of this specific objective include:
advancing the use of AI in industry, services and public sector;
improving the knowledge and skills of people working in these sectors;
creating a knowledge-based economy;
encouraging the development and use of new technologies;
supporting research and innovation in the areas of cybersecurity and trust.
3.
Cybersecurity and trust
The aim of this objective is to ensure the security of personal data and the protection of the information and communication technology (ICT) infrastructure.
4.
Deployment and best use of digital capacity and interoperability.
5.
To achieve these objectives, the programme will:
make use of existing and new technologies, such as the Internet of Things (IoT) and the internet of things (i.e. smart devices, smart cities, smart grids, etc.);
encourage the deployment of new and innovative technologies and services;
ensure the interoperability of existing infrastructure;
promote the sharing of best practices and best practices between EU countries and international organisations;
make it easier for companies and organisations to operate in the EU.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a multiannual financial framework, known as the ‘MFF 2021–2027’, which aims to align its duration with that of the European Economic and Social Fund (Euratom).
For more information, see:
‘The EU’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
   
Council Regulation (EC) No 2093/2020 of 21 December 2020 establishing the Specific Objectives for the Implementation of the Programme for the Programme on the Development and Innovation of the General Goals of the Union (OJ L 183, 1.1.2019, pp. 1–8)

Summary 9:
EU SME Innovation and Entrepreneurship Fund
EU SMEs Innovation & Entrepreneurships Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/826 on the EU’s innovation and entrepreneurship fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU Innovation & entrepreneurship fund (IAPF).
It aims to support the development of innovative, high-quality products and services for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The IAPF aims to:
promote the growth and competitiveness of the EU economy;
support the development and diversification of the European SME sector;
encourage SMEs to take advantage of the opportunities offered by the EU single market;
improve the quality of life of EU citizens and businesses; and
improve access to the EU market for SMEs and consumers.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme are to promote:
the development of new technologies and innovative products, services and solutions for the EU;
the diversification and expansion of the internal market for EU businesses and consumers;
access to the European single market for European SMEs;
improving access to and diversifying the EU's economy for European consumers and businesses.
Sustainable growth and jobs
To achieve these objectives, the programme supports the following actions:
research, innovation and technological development;
creating a knowledge-based economy for EU citizens;
enhancing access to new technologies;
promoting the knowledge base of EU businesses;
raising awareness of the importance of EU SMEsports and e-commerce;
fostering innovation;
boosting the competitiveness of EU companies and consumers in the EU.
The actions must have clear EU added value.
Financing
The programme is financed by the European Commission and the European Investment Bank (EIB).
The Commission may finance up to 100 % of the eligible costs.
Eligible actions
The European Commission may, by way of derogation from Article 190 of the Financial Regulation, finance 100% of eligible costs for actions implementing the specific objective. The Commission may also finance up-to-date actions that are not covered by this regulation. Actions may also be financed by other sources, such as grants, loans, grants and loans from other EU countries.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU Innovation and entrepreneurship Fund was established under Regulation (EC) No 1025/2012 on European standardisation, amending Council Directives 89/686/EEC, 94/25/EC, 95/16/EC and 97/23/EC.
For more information, see:
European standardisation (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 826/2017 of 25 June 2017 establishing the European Innovation and entrepreneurial fund (OJ L 183, 1.7.2017, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom).
last update 14.02.2019



Summary 10:
Transfer of firearms and their essential components from government stocks to permanent civilian use
Transfer of firearm and its essential components between EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2013/13/EU on the transfer of certain firearms and certain essential components intended for civilian use (codified version)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ laws and regulations on firearms and essential components are compatible with those of the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY).
It codifies and repeals Directive 91/477/EEC.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
the transfer of a firearm or its essential component from a government stock to civilian use is authorised;
the unique marking is affixed to the firearm or the essential components at the time of transfer.
The transfer must be authorised by the competent authority of the EU country in which the transfer takes place.
Transfer procedure
The competent authority must issue a licence incorporating all the particulars referred to in the first subparagraph of the directive. The licence must be valid for a period of 5 years from the date of transfer of the firearm.
It must be renewed at least once every 5 years.
If the transfer is carried out by a non-EU country, it must be accompanied by a certificate of authenticity. The certificate must bear the unique marking of the transferring entity.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive applies from 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU’s internal market for firearms and related essential components has been subject to a number of restrictions since 1 January 2011.
For more information, see:
‘Firearm transfers’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Firearms: any of the following centre-fire semi-automatic firearms: short firearms which allow the firing of more than 21 rounds without reloading, if:
a loading device with a capacity exceeding 20 rounds is part of that firearm; or
a detachable loading device containing a maximum capacity of 11 rounds is inserted into it;
long firearms: long firearms that allow the fire of no more than 11 rounds without loading;
detachable loading devices: any device containing more than 10 rounds.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 11:
Maritime transport: harmonised reporting of goods and services — EU-wide single window
Maritime Transport: harmonized reporting of Goods and Services
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — harmonised maritime transport reporting
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to harmonise maritime transport information to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the EU’s internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ maritime transport. It does not apply to:
non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1) and Iceland;
the United Nations Code for Trade and Transport Locations (UN/LOC);
the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA);
international organisations such as the International Maritime Organization (IMO) and the World Trade Organization (WTO).
EU countries must ensure that the information they provide to the single window is accurate and up-to-date. They must also ensure that:
the data they provide are accurate and comparable;
they are reliable;
there is no duplication of information; and
they do not provide false or incomplete information.
National single windows
Each EU country must set up a maritime single window with one or more other EU countries.
The national single window must:
provide information on:
port facilities and port facilities;
port of arrival;
customships;
ship names and registration details;
ships’ names and addresses;
agent of ship at intended port of arrival.
It must also provide the following information:
ship registration details
ship name and registration number
port port of departure
number of calls made to port facilities
last ten calls at port facilities in chronological order (most recent call first):
last 10 calls made by a ship to a port facility
last five calls at ports
last 5 calls to ports
port number of ships that have made a port call
port address
port type
port country

Port port number

Port type

Port state
Port city
Port State identifier
Countries
United Nations code for trade and transport locations.
Information to be provided to the maritime national single-window
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.

Summary 12:
Road toll charges: classification of vehicles and the notification of failure to pay road fees
Road tolls: classification and notification of failures
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/865 on the classification of road tolls
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ road toll-charging authorities (Tolls) are able to detect and inform the public of any failure to comply with road toll charges.
It sets out rules for the classification, notification and information to be sent to the public.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all vehicles registered in the EU. It does not apply to:
vehicles registered in non-EU countries;
toll-charging vehicles that are not covered by the EU’s general rules on road traffic;
vehicle registration certificates issued by EU countries.
The rules apply to the following categories of vehicles:
cars, trucks and buses;
motor vehicles;
passenger cars;
taxi vehicles.
Tolls
EU countries must ensure that:
tolls are notified to the authorities of the EU country in which the vehicle is registered;
the vehicle is accompanied by an information letter;
information is sent to a contact office in the country where the vehicle was registered. The contact office may be a natural person or a public or private body.
Information letter
The information letter must contain the following information:
the name and address of the contact office;
where the vehicle with registration is registered
the number and make of the vehicle
the make and model of vehicle.
the registration certificate
the date of registration
the identity of the holder of the registration certificate.
Where the vehicle has been detected by a responsible body, the responsible body must send the information letter to the contact offices in the relevant EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
It has applied since 19 October 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 19 October 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Tolls’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Road toll: a road toll levied on a vehicle for the purpose of facilitating the movement of goods, passengers and freight.

Summary 13:
EU network of immigration liaison officers
EU network OF IMMIGRATION LANCE OFFICERS
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2014 on the establishment of a European Network of Immigration Liaison Officers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the functioning of the European Union’s (EU) network of liaison officers.
It sets up a mechanism through which EU countries, the European Commission and EU agencies can more systematically coordinate the tasks and roles of their liaison officers deployed in third countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes a European network of EU liaison officers (EUNILO).
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that the network of officers deployed to non-EU countries contributes to the implementation of the Schengen acquis.
EU countries deploy liaison officers to third countries on the basis of their competence and training. These liaison officers are responsible for:
monitoring the movement of migrants;
assisting border control authorities;
providing information and assistance to the authorities of the countries in which they are deployed;
coordinating the activities of the national authorities of those countries; and
assessing the effectiveness of the network.
The European network is managed by the Steering Board, which is composed of representatives from each EU country. It is chaired by the Chair of the Commission and comprises representatives of the EU countries and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency. It meets at least once a year. It has a budget of €500,000. It also comprises representatives from the European Parliament, the Council of Europe and the Commission.
Each EU country must designate one or more liaison officers for each of its territories.
Training and selection
EU liaison officers must be trained in accordance with national curricula. Such curricula should be non-compulsory and supplementary to national curriculas established by deploying authorities.
They must also be given the opportunity to take part in the organisation of training events organised by the Commission, the Commission or the EU agencies.
In addition, EU countries may also agree to share certain tasks among each other, based on their competence.
When a liaison officer is deployed to an EU country, he or she must have the following training:
a basic training programme;
a written training manual;
the training of the officers’ skills and knowledge;
an annual report on the training and selection of the liaison officers;
exchanges of experience and best practice;
training of the staff of the steering board.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU is committed to protecting the human rights and fundamental freedoms of all migrants, regardless of their migratory status, in full compliance with international law.
For more information, see:
‘EU’ on the European External Action Service's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Immigration liaison officer: an immigration liaison officer who is responsible for monitoring the movements of migrants in the territory of a third country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2014 of 27 June 2014 on the creation of a network of European immigration liaison Officers (recast) (OJ L 136, 28.6.2014, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 14:
Biometric identity cards for EU citizens — minimum security and format standards for identity cards
Biometric identification cards for European Union citizens – minimum security, format and security standards
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/865 on minimum security standards for national identity cards (Biometric Identity Card Regulation)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum security requirements for the issuing of identity cards in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that EU citizens can exercise their right of free movement, subject to certain limitations and conditions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU citizens who have the right to move and reside freely within the territory of the EU. It does not apply to non-EU citizens.
National identity cards must be issued by the EU country issuing them.
EU countries must ensure that:
the identity card is issued in the language of the country issuing the card;
the card bears the country code of the issuing country;
there is a minimum period of validity of 5 years and a maximum period of 10 years.
The identity card must contain, on the front side, the two-letter country code, printed in negative in a blue rectangle and encircled by 12 yellow stars.
It must contain biometric identifiers (fingerprints, iris prints, facial features and iris scans).
The card must be accompanied by a security document (fingerprint or iris scan).
the holder of the card must provide the identity card holder with a copy of the national security document.
Collection of biometric data must be kept for 10 years after the end of the 5-year validity period. The card holder must keep a record of their fingerprints and facial features.
In addition, the holder of an identity card may be required to provide a fingerprint for the purpose of verifying the authenticity of their identity card. The holder of a card must also provide a photograph of their face and a description of their name and place of residence.
When issuing a national identity card, EU countries must:
ensure that the card holder’s fingerprints are recorded and stored in a secure database;
use the same fingerprint for authentication purposes;
keep a copy for at least 5 years after that period;
notify the European Commission of any changes to the security standards and format of the cards.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve the security of EU citizens’ identity cards. These include:
introducing a minimum security standard for national identification cards;
establishing a standard format for national IDENTITY cards; and
introduced a standard security document for biometric identification.
For more information, see:
‘Biometric Identification’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* National identity card: a card issued by an EU country to a person who is a resident of another EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барканска грака  глакова   данта
Council Regulation (EEC) No 865/2019 of 1 January 2019 laying down minimum security specifications for the identity cards of the Member States issuing them (OJ L 136, 1.2.2019, pp. 1-10)

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems for security, border and migration management
Interoperation of EU Information Systems for security and border management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1861 on interoperability of the European Union information systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve interoperability between the EU’s information systems in order to improve the work of border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities.
It sets up a common information system (ESP) to facilitate the exchange of information between EU countries’ border guards and customs authorities on travel documents and travel documents.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
travel documents, such as passports and visas;
passports and visas issued by EU countries to non-EU citizens;
travel data held in an EU information system, Europol data or in Interpol databases.
EU countries must ensure that:
EU information systems are interoperable;
national authorities have access to the same information;
the information they need to carry out border control and border control operations;
EU authorities have the same access to Europol and Interpol data.
National authorities must:
use the same data for border controls and customs duties;
use a common identity repository for identification;
have access to a common travel document database.
Requests for information must be carried out in a language easily understood by all EU countries;
not be made using the same language;
be carried out using data that is stored in different databases;
such as Europol or Interpol or Eurojust;
only be performed using data related to persons or travel documents that are stored in the European information system or in the Interpol database; and
be performed using the information that is already available.
The European Commission is responsible for the development and operation of the shared BMS. It is assisted by a committee composed of representatives from each EU country and chaired by the Commission. The Commission is also assisted by the European Border and Coast Guard Agency and the Directorate-General for Migration and Integration (DGMI).
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
It entered into force on 11 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve its information system interoperability.
For more information, see:
‘EU Information System interoperability’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Travel document: a travel document issued by an EU country to a person who has entered the EU on the basis of a valid travel document. It must be accompanied by a passport, visa or travel document containing a photograph of the holder and a description of the travel document and the travel date.
Passport: a passport issued by a country for the purpose of entering the EU.
Short-stay visa: a valid visa issued by the country in which the person is staying for a limited period of time.

Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1861/2019 of 11 June 2019 on interoperable European Union Information Systems (Euratom) (OJ L 183, 11.6.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 16:
Renewable electricity: renewable energy — rules for self-consumers and joint acting renewables
Renewables: rules for renewables
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2017/24/EU on renewable electricity
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to encourage the development of renewable energy (renewable energy) in the European Union (EU).
It recasts and repeals Directive 2009/28/EC on renewable energy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to electricity generated by renewable sources such as photovoltaic (PV) and geothermal (Geothermal) energy systems.
It does not apply to:
electricity generated by nuclear power plants;
energy generated by hydroelectric plants; or
energy produced by hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs).
EU countries must ensure that renewable energy is available to all EU citizens, regardless of whether they are self-employed or not.
The rules apply to the following types of renewable electricity:
generating renewable electricity from renewable sources (i.e. renewable sources of energy that are capable of producing electricity at a useful temperature level;
producing renewable energy from sources other than those listed in the directive’s Annex I; and
producing electricity from sources that are not renewable, such as wind turbines, geothermal heat pumps, solar thermal power plants, hydroelectric heat pumps or heat pumps enabling the use of ambient or geothermal energy from water that has previously been pumped uphill.
Cooperation between renewable sources and jointly acting renewable energy consumers (e.g. wind turbines and hydroelectric power plants)
Energy generated by jointly acting renewables self-conformity with other sources
Cooperating between EU countries
Cooperative renewable energy sources must be able to meet the energy requirements of EU countries’ electricity markets. This includes:
collecting, storing and distributing renewable energy;
providing renewable energy for the generation of electricity;
sharing renewable energy with other energy-intensive sources;
making renewable energy available to other EU countries;
using renewable energy to generate electricity for the production of other energy sources.
In addition, each EU country must:
provide information on the renewable sources it considers to be renewable;
ensure that renewable sources are available to the public;
make available to consumers the information they need to make a decision on whether to participate in the renewable energy market;
take measures to reduce the carbon intensity of the electricity they generate;
encourage the development and diversification of renewable sources, including by:
encouraging the sharing of renewable resources;
developing and diversifying renewable energy markets;
reducing the cost of energy for consumers;
establishing renewable energy efficiency targets;
introducing incentives to encourage renewable energy generation;
improving the transparency of the market for renewable energy producers;
promoting renewable energy transparency.
Measures to reduce greenhouse gas emissions (GHG emissions)
In order to reduce GHG emissions, EU countries must take measures to: ensure that:
all EU countries have the same GHG emission limits for each renewable energy source;
all energy sources are eligible for GHG credits;
each EU country has to set up a national GHG-emission limit for each energy source.
Each EU country is responsible for ensuring that the GHG limits are applied to each renewable source of electricity.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
  
It applies from 1 July 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Green energy’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 17:
Radio emergency services: radio frequency spectrum policy
Radio emergency systems: radio emergency services
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 on radio emergency systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to improve the functioning of the EU’s radio emergency system. It builds on the emergency system elements already enshrined in EU law, namely a public safety answering point (PSAP) and a most appropriate PSAP.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all radio frequency (RF) bands, including the 40,5-43,5 GHz and 66-71 GHz bands. It does not apply to the radio frequency bands used by the European Union (EU), the European Atomic Energy Community (EEC) and the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA).
EU countries must ensure that:
radio emergency services are interoperable and interoperable;
radio frequencies are allocated according to the frequency bands they cover;
the frequency bands covered by the directive are allocated to the most appropriate emergency services.
National regulatory authorities must cooperate with each other where the relevant market is found to be transnational.
The European Commission must adopt multiannual radio spectrum policy programmes, where appropriate.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The radio emergency service directive is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and modernise the EU radio system.
For more information, see:
Radio Emergency Services Directive (European Commission).

Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of emissions of greenhouse gases emitted by heavy-duty vehicles (EU ETS) and non-ETS sectors (EU 2020/2020)
Monitoring & reporting of greenhouse gas emissions emitted by light-duty vehicle categories (EU EU 2020/2021)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/2022 on the monitoring and reporting by manufacturers of heavy-duty vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the EU’s greenhouse gas emission allowances (GHG emissions) trading system (the EU ETS).
It aims to reduce GHG emissions by 40 % and 30 % respectively by 2030 compared to 2005 levels.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
heavy-duty trucks, buses, coaches and light commercial vehicles;
light commercial vehicles, buses and coaches;
motor vehicles, trailers and heavy goods vehicles.
It does not apply to certain types of vehicles, such as:
trucks and buses;
cargo carriers;
trams and semi-trailers;
passenger cars;
cars and vans.
EU countries’ national authorities must:
monitor and report GHG emission allowances to the European Commission on a regular basis;
report to the Commission on the emission allowances they have issued to manufacturers;
monitor the data they have received from the European Environment Agency (EEA) on a yearly basis.
The Commission may, by means of implementing acts, determine the verification and correction measures necessary to ensure that manufacturers comply with the requirements of the regulation.
monitoring, reporting and reporting
The European Commission may also impose administrative fines on manufacturers if it finds that the data reported by the manufacturer deviates from the data recorded in the framework of Regulation (EC) No 595/2009 and, in particular, in accordance with Commission regulations.
Reporting and management
Manufacturers must ensure that their data are recorded in a publicly accessible register. The Register is managed by the European Union Environment Agency on behalf of the Commission. It is open to the public and can be accessed by the purchaser of a vehicle and the EU country of registration.
In addition, the Commission may adopt implementing acts to ensure the transparency of the data it receives from manufacturers. The Commission may decide to impose administrative sanctions on manufacturers that fail to comply with these measures.

Summary 19:
Roles and responsibilities of natural gas suppliers and competent authorities in the event of supply disruptions and crises
Roles, responsibilities, responsibilities and risks relating to the internal gas market
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — roles and responsibilities relating to natural gas supply
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the roles, responsibilities of the national authorities responsible for ensuring the proper functioning of the internal market for natural gas.
It aims to ensure that the EU’s natural gas market functions properly, particularly in the case of gas supply disruptions or crises.
KEY POINTS
The regulation:
sets out the responsibilities of all natural gas undertakings and the competent authorities;
introduces a common and national risk assessment to assess natural, technological, commercial, financial, social, political and market-related risks, andany other relevant ones.
The national authorities must:
conduct a comprehensive risk assessment every 2 years;
prepare a preventive action plan;
take the necessary measures to ensure the uninterrupted supply of gas to its protected customers;
ensure that the gas supply standard is met;
not impact negatively on the ability of any other EU country to supply gas to their protected customers.
Risk assessment
The European Commission must conduct a risk assessment at least every two years.
National authorities must ensure that:
the risk assessment is carried out in accordance with the relevant EU law and regulations;
the competent authorities prepare a common risk assessment.
the national risk assessments are based on a template set out of the relevant template;
inform the European Parliament, the European Commission, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of the results of the risk assessments.
In addition, the Commission must carry out a risk simulation to assess the impact of a gas supply disruption or crisis on the EU gas market. This simulation must be repeated at least once every two year.
EU countries’ national authorities are responsible for:
ensuring that the risk assessment and preventive action plans are prepared;
monitoring the gas market;
taking the necessary steps to ensure gas supply is uninterrupted;
providing the Commission with information on the risks and measures to be taken;
assessing the impact on the energy security of the EU.
Protected customers
The EU country responsible for protecting the rights and interests of the protected customers should:
provide the Commission and EU countries with a description of the number of consumers covered by the regulation;
identify and inform the Commission of the names of those covered by this regulation.
Protecting the rights of consumers
The Commission and the EU countries must ensure the protection of consumers’ rights, including by:
establishing a list of protected customers and their rights of access to gas supply;
designating a national authority responsible for safeguarding these rights and ensuring that it is able to provide the necessary information to the Commission, EU countries and the Commission;
developing and implementing a national emergency response plan.
Ensuring the safety of gas supplies
In order to guarantee the safety and security of supply, the regulation sets out a number of conditions, including:
protecting the interests of consumers;
promoting gas security and ensuring the efficient operation of the gas system;
protectring the security of energy supply for the EU as a whole;
implementing the EU solidarity mechanism.
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/856 sets out rules on the implementation of Regulation (EC) No 994/2010 on gas supply.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve the internal energy market in the EU, in particular to:
improve the efficiency of the energy market; and
improve energy security.
For more information, see:
Directives on energy security (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Natural gas: natural gas is a natural gas-related energy which is used to provide electricity, heating, cooling and heating to households and businesses, as well as for transport, heating and cooling.
* Energy security: a system that ensures that the supply of energy is uninterrupted throughout the supply chain.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
  Еблганска народна граба барка (OJ L 183, 1.7.2017, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) No 752/2017 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 20:
Energy efficiency of energy-saving products — EU rules on labelling and labelling (Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on energy efficiency) (European Commission).
EU rules on energy saving products — European standardisation (Commission)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/1025 — energy efficiency requirements for energy efficiency products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the labelling of energy efficient products in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the efficiency of products and reduce energy consumption.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
energy efficiency products;
energy-saving appliances;
electromagnetic compatibility products.
It does not apply to energy-related products such as heating and cooling systems.
EU countries must ensure that:
products are labelled in a language easily understood by end-users;
they are accompanied by a product information sheet.
Labels must comply with the requirements of the delegated acts adopted pursuant to Directive 2010/30/EU and Directive 96/60/EC.
Manufacturers and importers must:
make sure that the labels comply with these delegated acts;
ensure that the information sheet is clearly legible and legible;
keep a record of any changes to the label;
provide a copy of the label for market surveillance purposes;
notify the European Commission of any amendments to the regulation.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to amend the regulation if it considers that the regulation has been amended or repealed or replaced by delegated acts.
Imporporters must ensure compliance with the regulation’s requirements.
Exemptions from the regulation include:
good manufacturing practice;
the manufacture of products that are not covered by the regulation;
products that are used in the manufacture of goods that are covered by a delegated act.
Products covered by this regulation must be accompanied by:
a label in paper form complying with the relevant delegated act;
an information sheet;
a description of the energy efficiency of the product;
information on the products’ energy efficiency.
Where a supplier places a product on the market, each unit must bear an electronic label in electronic form. The electronic label must be supplemented by an information sheet, which must contain:
the energy efficiency rating and energy efficiency class of product; and
the name and address of the supplier.
Distributors must ensure the electronic label is accompanied by an electronic information sheet and that the label is clearly visible on the product.
In addition, EU countries must take steps to ensure that the electronic information sheets are easily accessible on the internet and that consumers can easily identify the products they buy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
The delegated act has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Energy efficiency’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 21:
Protecting personal data: EU rules on the processing of personal data (Regulation (EU) 2016/852)
Protecting human data: European rules on data processing
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation on the Protection of Personal Data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that personal data are protected when they are processed by companies and public authorities.
It sets out the rules for the processing and use of these personal data.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the processing, storage, use and dissemination of data. It does not apply to the storage, processing, use or dissemination of information on individuals.
EU countries must ensure that:
personal data are processed only in accordance with the law of the EU country in which the processing takes place;
the processing is carried out in a way that respects the privacy and security of the data subject;
privacy and security concerns are taken into account in the decision-making process;
data protection measures are in place to protect the personal data of individuals who are the subject of the processing.
Right of access to personal data
The data subject has the right to:
the right to access and to have their personal data processed in a manner that respects their privacy;
to have their data stored and used without undue delay;
access to information on their activities;
information on their use.
The right to erasure (’right to be forgotten’)
right to have been extended to include:
their name, address and contact details;
their contact details.
Processing and use
The processing of data must comply with the following rules:
Personal data processing must be carried out under the supervision of a data protection officer.
Data protection officer:
is appointed by the European Commission to ensure the proper functioning of the European Union’s system of data protection;
is responsible for the protection of data, including:
identifying, analysing and storing personal data;
ensuring that the data are used only for the purposes for which they are intended;
assessing the risks associated with the processing;
checking that the processing complies with the data protection requirements of EU or national law;
monitoring and assessing the impact of processing on the privacy, security and data protection of individuals;
taking the necessary measures to protect personal data, such as:
protecting the confidentiality and integrity of the information collected;
collecting, storing, using and disseminating personal data for the purpose of preventing, detecting, investigating, investigating and prosecuting crime;
identification of the person responsible for processing personal data and ensuring that the person concerned is informed of their rights and obligations;
keeping a record of all personal data that is processed, including the names of the persons who have access to it;
notifying the person who has access to the data and the reasons for their access.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that data protection officers:
have the powers and expertise necessary to carry out their tasks;
carry out the tasks in line with EU or Member State law; and
have access to all the data they collect.
Information on personal data processing:
must be kept confidential and must be made available only to authorised persons;
must not be used for purposes other than those for which it is necessary to do so;
may only be shared with third parties;
can only be used in the context of investigations, investigations, prosecutions, investigations and prosecutions.
Individuals’ right to privacy:
may not be deprived of their right to express their views, opinions or opinions on a particular issue;
have their rights to be excluded from the processing or use of their data, except in cases where they have a legitimate interest in protecting the privacy of others.
Consumers’ rights:
are entitled to access to their data and to be informed of the conditions under which it can be used.
They must be able to withdraw their consent as soon as they wish. They must also have the option to be erased from their data. This right must be as easy as to withdraw as well as to give consent.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DELEGATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 14 April 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 14 May 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to ensure a high level of protection for personal data in the EU.
For more information, see:
EU data protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Personal data: data collected by a company or public authority on the basis of a request from a user.
* Data processing: the process by which a company, public authority or individual collects, processes and uses personal data on a given person.

Commission Delegated regulation on



Summary 22:
HICP — national and regional accounts in the European Union (EU) and the European Central Bank’s assessment of price stability (2013-2020)
HICPs — national, regional and cross-border accounts
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 549/2013 on the implementation of the Harmonised Interinstitutional Consumer Price Index
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the methodology for the harmonisation of the harmonised interinstitutional consumer price index, the HICP, which is used by the European Commission to assess price stability in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It does not apply to the European Economic Area (EEA).
It applies to the EU‘s external borders, which are covered by Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 609/2013.
EU countries’ national or regional accounts
Each EU country must submit an annual report to the Commission on how it is implementing the regulation.
The report must contain the following information:
the average prices of goods and services in the country in which the country is located;
the prices of the products and services it imports and exports.
the number of goods imported and exports to the country where the country participates in the calculation of the average prices.
Annexes to the regulation provide a description of the prices of products imported into the EU and exports from the EU, as well as the prices paid by the EU countries for goods imported into and exported out of the EU to the same EU country.
Each year, the Commission publishes a report on the methodology used to calculate the average price of goods.
In addition, it publishes a summary of the data it has collected and the methodology it has applied.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts concern:
sampling and representativity;
collection and treatment of prices;
replacements and quality adjustment;
index compilation;
revisions;
special indices;
treatment of products in specific areas.
Deadlines, exchange standards and revisions
The Commission must ensure that the implementing acts comply with the following deadlines and exchange standards:
1.1.3 years from the date of entry into force of the regulation;
2.5 years from that of the implementing act;
3.6 years from 1.7 years from 2.8 years from 3.8 year from the end of the implementation act.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Directive 2013/13/EU on the harmonization of the interinstitutions’ accounts (HICCP) has applied since 1 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission and the ECB use the harmonized interinstitution price index to assess the price stability of EU countries under Article 140 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU).
For more information, see:
‘The harmonised consumer prices index’ on the European Council’ website.

Summary 23:
Importation of goods from non-EU countries to the EU: rules on the investigation and enforcement of duties
Imports of goods to the European Union (EU) — rules on investigations and enforcement
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1225/2009 on the implementation of measures to combat cross-border trade in goods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ imports of goods are subject to an effective and proportionate level of import duties.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC, No 1223/2009.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to goods imported from:
the EU’s single market economy;
the market economy third country;
third countries of the Western Balkan countries;
non-euro area countries.
Importers’ rights
EU countries must ensure that the import of goods is subject to a duty on the value added tax (VAT) calculated on the basis of the value of the goods imported. This value must be based on representative sales in the ordinary course of trade in the exporting country. It must be calculated in accordance with the customs duties applicable to imports from the importing country.
Duty:
provisional and definitive duties
Provisional duties may be imposed on imports of the product concerned manufactured by the companies referred to in the regulation.
Defensive duties may not apply to imports of products manufactured by companies listed in Annex I of the regulation, as amended by Regulation (EU, EC, EC) No 1101/2013 (see summary).
Duties
The Commission may also impose definitive duties on imports from third countries if it considers that the goods are likely to pose a threat to public health, public security, public order or the environment.
The investigation procedure
An investigation into an import is carried out by the Commission. The investigation is open to all interested parties, including the companies involved in the import. The Commission decides on the outcome of the investigation on a case-by-case basis, taking into account all relevant factors, including:
exchange of information,
the economic situation of the importing and exporting countries,
exports and exports of the products concerned,
customs procedures,
trade in goods and services between the exporting and importing countries, and
the level of protection of intellectual property rights.
If the Commission concludes that a product is likely to present a risk to public security or public order, it may impose import duties on the import or export of that product. It may also decide to impose import taxes on the export of the same product to the exporting or importing country, depending on the circumstances of the case.
Expiry of import duty
A decision to terminate the investigation may be terminated by the European Commission on the grounds that the investigation has not yielded a satisfactory result.
Reviews and refunds
An EU country may request a review or a refund of the import duties it has imposed. The review or refund must be carried out within 9 months of the conclusion of an investigation.
In addition, the Commission may review or issue a refund to the importing or exporting country in the case of a failure to comply with the conditions laid down in the investigation. The European Commission may issue a decision to suspend the importation of a product or to suspend its export for a period longer than 9 months.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2009/33/EC has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Imports from the Western Balkans’ (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Implementing Decision (EU), Euratom, Euratom and Euratom Implementing Regulation (Euratom) of 21 December 2009 on the functioning of the market-economy third country investigation procedure (Codified version) (OJ L 347, 21.12.2009, pp. 1-24)
Successive amendments to Regulation (eu) No 1323/2008 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 24:
Subsidies and countervailing measures: investigation and enforcement of EU rules on trade in agricultural products (Codified Regulation (EU) No 182/2011)
Subsidies and anti-subsidies (codified Regulation)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EC) No 184/2011 on the investigation and prosecution of subsidisation and injury caused by imports of agricultural products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Delegated Council Regulation (CFSP) No 181/2010.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to imports into the EU from non-EU countries. It does not apply to imports of goods originating in the EU.
It does not cover:
the importation of goods from the EU and imports of products originating outside the EU;
the use of domestic over imported goods; or
the export performance of goods imported from the European Union (EU).
It applies to the importation, export performance and use of imported goods by EU countries or exporters.
Importers and importers must inform the European Commission of any subsidies they have received.
The Commission may launch an investigation to determine whether these subsidies or injury have been caused by the import of goods. The investigation must cover both subsidisation* and injury. It must be launched within 60 days of receiving a request for information from an interested party. The Commission may decide to open an investigation if it considers that there has been a substantial increase in the level of subsidies and/injury caused by these subsidies.
In the event of an increase in imports, the Commission may impose provisional duties on the imports. The provisional duties must be imposed no earlier than 60 days from the initiation of the proceedings but no later than 9 months from the end of the investigation.
Where the Commission considers that an importation or export performance has been affected by a subsidisation or injury, it may impose a provisional duty on the import or export of the imported goods. It may also impose a duty on imports of the goods if there is a significant increase in export performance.
Provisional duties may be imposed on imports if there are significant increases in the export performance or use of imports.
EU countries and exporters must not seek or accept from the Commission any subsidy or injury resulting from imports of their goods. They must instead inform the Commission of the reasons for this decision.
If the Commission concludes that there is no evidence of a subsidy or an injury, provisional or final findings, affirmative or negative, may be made on the basis of the facts available.
Annex II to the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organisation (‘the WTO Agreement’) contains, inter alia, the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade 1994 (the GATT 1994) and an Agreement on Agriculture (Agreement on Agriculture).
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Summary 25:
Recovery and resilience support for EU countries experiencing severe economic and social challenges (2020-20)
Recovery & resilience support (2020 - 2023)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/2094 on the recovery and resilience of EU countries facing severe economic or social challenges
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions under which EU countries receiving financial support from the European Union are eligible for financial assistance.
It aims to help EU countries to recover from the impact of the economic and financial crisis and to strengthen their resilience to future challenges.
KEY POINTS
EU countries eligible for EU financial support
The regulation applies to all EU countries that have been affected by the crisis or are facing serious economic and/or social challenges. It does not apply to:
non-EU countries that are experiencing serious social or economic challenges;
those that have not been affected.
Beneficiaries must:
recover from the effects of the crisis;
rebuild their economies;
improve their capacity to cope with the impact from the crisis.
The measures covered by the regulation are:
economic and social measures, such as:
investment in infrastructure;
job creation;
employment policies;
education and training;
public procurement;
transport.
EU funding
The European Commission:
assists EU countries in the implementation of the regulation’s rules on the allocation of financial support;
provides the Commission with a list of measures that are eligible;
ensures that EU countries are able to draw up recovery plans and resilience plans.
FROMENTRY INTO FORCE
To qualify for financial support, EU countries must submit to the Commission an economic recovery plan that is duly reasoned and substantiated. The plan must cover:
the economic situation in the country concerned;
the state of the economy;
measures taken to improve the quality of life of the population;
economic policies and measures taken to tackle unemployment.
To be eligible for funding, countries must have:
a recovery plan;
a national ownership and a focus on relevant reforms and investments;
an investment strategy; and
a plan to strengthen the capacity of the labour market.
In addition, the Commission must assess the economic situation and the resilience of the country in terms of its ability to deal with future challenges, including:
its ability to adapt to the economic, social and fiscal situation;
its capacity to respond to future shocks;
how it is able to adapt its economic policies to the needs of its citizens.

Summary 26:
Employment policy instruments — EU-wide monitoring and evaluation
Employment policies — European Union (EU) policies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2017 on the European Union’s employment policy instruments
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ policies on employment take account of the needs of EU citizens and human rights.
It establishes a European Union employment policy instrument.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that their employment policies take into account guidelines for employment drawn up by the Council of the EU. These guidelines are based on the principles of the Employment Framework Directive (TFEU).
The regulation sets out rules on how EU countries should implement these guidelines. It also sets out the conditions under which the European Commission may grant grants to EU countries for the purpose of monitoring and evaluating the effectiveness of these policies.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the instrument. It is assisted by the Directorate-General for Employment, Social Affairs and Inclusion (DSI) and the European Employment Agency (EEA).
It is also assisted by a Joint Committee on Employment and Social Affairs.
Scope
The instrument applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to:
non-EU countries that are part of the Schengen area;
those that are not part of that area.
Beneficiaries of the regulation are:
employees of EU countries;
employers of EU workers;
people who are not EU citizens.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATOR APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCELEBRATION
It enters into force
It applies from 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality of employment policies in the EU, including:
the European Employment Strategy (see summary);
the EU Employment Policy Instrument (EEOI), which aims to improve employment policies for EU citizens by:
improving the quality and consistency of EU employment policies;
ensuring that EU citizens’ rights are taken into account;
promoting equal opportunities for all EU citizens;
encouraging EU citizens to participate in the labour market;
supporting the creation of a European employment market.
For more information, see:
EU employment policies (European Commission).

Summary 27:
Uniform rules for the carriage of passengers by rail in the European Union (COTIF)
Universal rules for carriage by rail
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 on the uniform rules for passenger transport in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down uniform rules on passenger transport within the EU.
It aims to ensure that passengers travelling by rail are treated equally and fairly.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all rail services provided under public service contracts. It does not apply to:
the carriage of goods and passengers by road;
the operation of passenger services provided by private companies;
services provided by public service undertakings.
Conditions of carriage
Passengers must be provided with:
an adequate number of places for bicycles;
a minimum number of seats for bicycles in the carriage;
adequate numbers of places in the locomotive and in the cabins;
access to the ticket vending and tour operators’ information systems;
information on the conditions and limitations of the ticketing system.
Passenger information
Train operators must provide passengers with information on:
conditions and conditions of transport;
traffic management and timetabling;
availability of services;
costs and charges;
revenue and charges.
In addition, train operators must ensure that:
passengers are informed of the conditions of travel and of the means of transport available to them;
there is no discrimination on the basis of their nationality, religion, race, colour, language, age, disability, sexual orientation or sexual orientation;
they are given access to the information they need to make a reservation;
passenger information is provided in a non-discriminatory manner and without undue delay.
Liability of the carrier
The carrier is responsible for:
operating the railway system;
providing passenger information;
carrying out the passenger information system and providing the passenger with the information necessary to make an informed decision;
ensuring that passengers have access to information on the services they are entitled to;
making sure that the information is available to all passengers;
notifying passengers of any changes to the timetabling system and the conditions for the re-routing of trains;
assisting persons with disabilities to enable them to be independently mobile.
Compliance with the COTIF rules
Rail operators must:
ensure that passengers are provided with the necessary information to make their decision-making process more efficient;
take all necessary measures to ensure the safety and security of passengers.
Train companies must also:
provide information to passengers about the conditions under which they operate;
make sure that passengers’ travel information is made available to ticket vendors, tour operators and ticket-holders;
keep passengers informed of any delays and cancellations and late running of trains.
Disputes between train companies are settled by arbitration.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
It entered into force on 23 February 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Uniform railway rules’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Passenger information system: a system that allows passengers to make informed decisions about the transport they take on board a train.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/1371 of 23 February 2019 laying down uniform uniform rules concerning passenger transport by rail (recast) (OJ L 136, 24.3.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (UE) 2019-1371 have been incorporated in to the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
