Summary 29:
European External Action Instrument (EFSD+)
European External action Instrument (EOUD)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2010 establishing the European External Action Service
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s external action instrument.
It aims to ensure consistency between the different areas of its external action and between these and its other policies, as well as to work for a high degree of cooperation in all fields of international relations.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the objectives of the instrument, which are based on the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development (SDGs) and the EU's common foreign and security policy (CFSP).
The objectives of EFSD+ are to:
contribute to the achievement of the SDGs at global level;
promote peace, stability and conflict prevention;
fight against the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMDs);
support the sustainable development of the world economy;
support sustainable development in the fields of human rights, democracy, civil society, peace, security and global challenges.
Programmes
EFSD+ comprises a number of thematic programmes. They cover actions linked to the pursuit of SDGs in the following areas:
Human Rights and Democracy;
Civil Society Organisations;
Peace, Stability and Conflict Prevention;
Global Challenges.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
The instrument entered into force on 1 July 2010.
BACKGROUND
The EU countries must ensure that the EU external action is consistent with their national policies. This means that it contributes to achieving the objectives.
For more information, see:
EU external action instruments (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) No 529/2010 of 26 June 2010 establishing the EU External Action Instruments (EFD) (OJ L 201, 3.8.2010, pp. 1-8)

Summary 30:
Reporting of breaches of EU law: protection of whistle-blowers (Directive 2018/852/EU)
Reporting breaches of Union law: protecting whistleblowers
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/884 on the protection of whistleblowers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that persons reporting to institutions, bodies, offices or agencies of the EU benefit from the same level of protection as those reporting to the European Commission.
It sets out minimum standards of protection for those who report breaches of European Union law that are harmful to the public interest.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all persons who report to the EU institutions or bodies. It does not apply to third parties, such as external reporting platform providers, external counsel, auditors, trade union representatives or employees’ representatives.
EU institutions and bodies must ensure that those who make a public disclosure of a breach are protected against any form of retaliation. This includes:
public disclosure in cases where, despite internal and external reporting, the breach remains unaddressed, for instance if the breach was not appropriately assessed or investigated, or no appropriate remedial action was taken.
Public disclosure in the event of a serious breach of the law:
the disclosure must be made by a person who is well placed to report directly to the organisational head of the organisation concerned. In smaller entities, this function could be a dual function held by a company officer well placed by a chief compliance or human resources officer, an integrity officer, a legal or privacy officer or a chief audit executive, a chief financial officer, or a member of the board of directors.
The disclosure must also be made to a third party. Such third parties could be external reporting platforms providers, outside the organisation’s internal reporting channels and procedures.
Reporting to external reporting channels: the disclosure must not affect such external channels or procedures, where they exist, but should ensure that the same minimum standards are applied throughout the EU. The directive does not affect internal reporting systems and procedures that exist.
Persons making public disclosure should qualify for protection in the case of:
a breach that is harmful to public interest;
the breach is not properly assessed and investigated;
there is no action taken to remediate the breach;
an internal or external reporting channel or procedure exists that does not adequately assess or investigate the breach.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive.
For more information, see:
‘Reporting of EU breaches of law’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* ‘Whistleblower’: an individual or group of individuals or groups of individuals who, through their reporting, expose and prevent breaches of the laws of the Union and who have a key role in exposing and preventing such breaches and in safeguarding the welfare of society.

Summary 31:
European integrated border management: EU Agency for the Protection of the Union’s external borders
European Integrated Border Management: European Integrated Border Control Agency
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2019/852 establishing the European Integrated border management Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
 
It establishes the European integrated border control agency (EFCA).
KEY POINTS
The EFCA is responsible for the management of the external borders of the European Union (EU). It works closely with the European External Action Service (EEAS) and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EEA) to ensure that EU countries’ external borders are effectively managed.
It is the first EU agency to be set up under the Schengen acquis.
The agency has a national coordination centre in each EU country. It operates 24 hours a day, 7 days a week. It has a liaison officer in each of the EU countries. It also has liaison officers in Ireland and the United Kingdom (1).
The European situational picture (ESP) is the European border management picture (EU-wide picture) which aims to improve border management in the EU. It is based on the national situational picture of EU countries and the EU-wide border situation. It covers the broadest possible geographical basis. It includes information on:
the number of people crossing the borders of each country;
the types of vehicles, goods and services that cross the borders;
traffic flows of goods and goods;
border security measures;
measures to be taken by EU countries to prevent, detect and combat illegal immigration.
EFCAs work closely with EEAAs part of its mandate, the EFCAs:
operate the national coordination centres (NCCs) and liaison officers (Liaison officers) in EU countries;
coordinate the activities of the EEA and EMSA;
provide the European Commission with information on the situation on the external border;
assist the Commission and EEAas in the implementation of EU policies and programmes.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve its internal border management. These include:
strengthening cooperation between national border control authorities;
improving cooperation between EU countries in the field of external border management;
developing a common approach to border control.
For more information, see:
EU border management (European Commission).

Summary 32:
Information and communications technology (ICT) cybersecurity — European cybersecurity certification framework
Information and communication technology cybersecurity: European cybersecurity classification framework
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on cybersecurity certification
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Cybersecurity Certification Framework (ENISA).
It aims to improve the cybersecurity of information and communication technologies (ICTs) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the certification of cybersecurity products and services in the EU.
It does not apply to:
electronic equipment and software;
electromagnetic fields;
information and communications technologies;
telecommunications networks;
internet of things (ICT) infrastructure;
mobile phones and tablets;
smart phones and smart devices;
personal data storage devices.
Objectives
To improve cybersecurity, the regulation sets out a set of objectives for ENISA, including:
to:
ensure that cybersecurity products, services and devices meet the requirements of the EU’s cybersecurity legislation;
to promote the development and use of cybersecurity-related products and solutions;
promote interoperability between EU countries’ national cybersecurity certification authorities;
encourage the exchange of best practices and best practices in the field of cybersecurity.
To achieve these objectives, ENISA:
encourages the development of a common European cybersecurity framework;
provides a common approach to cybersecurity certification standards;
ensures that EU countries apply the European cybersecurity standards.
examines the risks associated with cybersecurity;
cooperates with other EU countries;
develops a common cybersecurity framework.
Ensures that the EU cybersecurity standards are aligned with those of the United Nations Security Council and the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTYTY).
Ensure that cybersecurity certification is applied uniformly across the EU and that the standards are compatible with those adopted in the United States, Japan and the United Kingdom (1).
Establishes a cybersecurity certification body to ensure that cybersecurity product and service providers comply with ENISA requirements.
ENISA: is composed of an Executive Board, an Executive Director and an Executive Advisory Group, an Advisory Group and a National Liaison Officers Network.
The Executive Board and Advisory Group meet at least twice a year.
An ECCG that consists of representatives of national cybersecurity certifying authorities or other relevant national authorities is established to ensure the consistent application of ENISA standards. It is chaired by the Executive Director, assisted by the Advisory Group.
In addition, the Advisory Board is assisted by a national liaison officer network.
National liaison officers network
The ENISA Advisory Group assists the Management Board and the Executive Board in the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 28 June 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 28 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Cybersecurity: the ability of a system to detect, prevent and respond to cyberattacks.
* ICT: the technology used in the production, distribution, use and maintenance of information, communication and communication networks (ICT networks).
* Communication: the means of communication, exchange and exchange of information between users, including the transmission and reception of messages, and the transmission, storage and management of data.

Summary 33:
EU countries’ gross national income (GNI)
EU countries' gross national incomes (GNIs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 608/2014 on the transmission of data on Gross National Income at market prices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of national income* data by the European Commission (Eurostat) to the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (EU).
It repeals Directive 89/130/EEC, Euratom and Regulation (EC) No 1287/2003 on European statistics.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
It covers:
gross national income, gross value added* (GVA)* and gross domestic product (GDP) at market price*;
gross value added of the various institutional sectors or the various industries;
Gross value added (GVIs) of the EU’s economic and social partners;
total value added, which includes taxes and subsidies on products.
The data are transmitted to Eurostat for the purpose of:
monitoring the economic situation of EU countries;
collecting and analysing economic statistics;
checking the quality of GNI aggregates and their components;
assessing the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of the GNI data.
Quality and reliability
Eurostat is responsible for verifying the quality and reliability of the sources and methods used to compile the data, as well as for ensuring that the data are comparable, reliable and exhaustive.
To verify the quality, Eurostat must carry out a number of tasks, including:
visits to EU countries to verify that the EU countries are in compliance with ESA 2010;
verification of quality of the data;
drawing up an inventory guide in close cooperation with the expert group referred to in Article 4 of the regulation.
National authorities must ensure that:
the data are reliable, exhaustive and comparable;
data are transmitted at the same time for the preceding year and any changes made to the data for previous years;
the EU countries must notify Eurostat of any changes in the data that have been made to them;
there is no duplication or overloading of data; and
there are no restrictions on the use of statistical confidentiality.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a series of implementing measures to modernise and modernise the European statistical system. These include:
Implementing measures for the system of own resources (ESA 2010);
Implementation measures for ESA 2014 (see summary);
EU policies on the European labour market (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Gross national income: the sum of the value of goods and services produced and traded in the EU by each EU country. It can be defined in three ways:
production approach: GDP is the total value of products produced by each industrial sector or industry, plus taxes and less subsidies on those products (which are not allocated to sectors and industries).
GVA: the value added per unit of GDP.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval rules for vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units based on UK type approvals (until the end of the EU’s membership of the European Union)
EU type–type–certificate of conformity
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — EU type–certification of vehicles and systems, systems and components based on the type–approval granted by the United Kingdom
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the granting and granting of EU type‑certificates to vehicles*, systems*, components* and separate* technical units* based on EU type‐approval.
It aims to ensure that the vehicles which have been granted EU type-authorisation by the UK type–cleaning authorities can continue to be sold in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation does not apply to:
vehicles, systems*, components*, separate technical unit** or separate vehicles* that were granted after 1 January 2008;
cars and light commercial vehicles (LCVs) which were granted before 1 January 2009;
vehicle-related technical units (SCVs) that were approved after the date of entry into force of the withdrawal agreement between the EU and the EU countries.
Vehicles and systems covered by the regulation must comply with the following requirements:
the vehicle manufacturer must have submitted a request for EU type approval to the competent authority;
the manufacturer must provide the competent authorities with all the necessary documentation and information concerning the vehicle, system, component and separate unit* specifications;
technical units must have been tested and certified in accordance with the relevant EU rules.
The competent authorities must:
approve the vehicle and system;
ensure that the vehicle complies with the requirements laid down in Directive 2007/46/EC;
notify the technical service notified by the EU authority to the European Commission;
provide the technical services with the results of the tests carried out;
make available to the Commission any test reports that have been sent by the competent UK authorities.
If a vehicle or system does not meet the requirements of the regulation, the manufacturer must submit a request to the authority granting the type‑cleaning* and type‐certification* to the vehicle. The competent authority may grant a Union type‐cleaning and type-certification to the manufacturer after it has accepted the request and after the manufacturer has agreed to cover the costs that the European type‐cleaner* has incurred as a result of exercising its powers and fulfilling its obligations in respect of the vehicles.
A vehicle or vehicle‐related technical unit may only be granted a European type‑cleaner if it meets the following conditions:
it meets all the requirements set out in the regulation;
it is manufactured in the territory of an EU Member State and has been certified by a competent authority in that Member State; and
it has been tested in compliance with the applicable EU rules and regulations.
In addition, the vehicle or component must meet certain conditions, such as:
its conformity assessment;
its availability on the market;
compliance with EU rules on vehicle safety and environmental protection;
availability on the EU market of the technical units;
conformity with EU regulations on vehicle design, manufacture, testing and certification.
Where the manufacturer does not comply with these conditions, it must provide a written request for a type-cleaning or type‐clarification. The manufacturer may also submit a technical report on the condition that it meets these conditions. The technical report must include the original UK type‐clearance document, the information folder and the test reports.
When a manufacturer submits a request, the European vehicle type-clearance authority may:
grant a vehicle, systems or components a type–clean‐certificate;
accept the request for the type-clean‑certificate, including all the documentation and the information contained in the original document;
require the manufacturer to provide a technical service with the technical report, including the test results;
assist the European technical service in carrying out its tasks;
take over the tasks and responsibilities of the competent bodies in the exercise of its powers.
However, if the manufacturer fails to fulfil these obligations, it may request a new type‑clearance from the competent body.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 March 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has been a contracting party to the Treaty on European Union (Treaties) since 1 January 1958.
For more information, see:
Types of vehicles — European Union rules on type–car classification (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  The regulation on the granting, granting and issuing of type–level certificates (OJ L 136, 30.3.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019



Summary 35:
European Police Office (Europol) — rules on cooperation and cooperation with the European Union’s law enforcement agencies
European Police Officer (Euratom) — laws and regulations relating to cooperation and coordination with Europol
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 883/2013 on the establishment of a European Police Office
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and procedures for the establishment and functioning of Europol, a European Union (EU) police agency.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes Europol as a European police office (EPO). It aims to:
ensure effective communication and cooperation between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities;
raise awareness of its activities;
facilitate the exchange of information between Europol and other EU countries;
promote cooperation between national police units and Europol.
Europol is responsible for:
collecting, analysing and disseminating information on all forms of crime the prevention or combating of which are considered to be a priority by the EU;
ensuring effective communication between the relevant national police authorities.
EU countries must:
supply Europol with the information necessary for it to fulfil its objectives, including information relating to forms of organised crime, terrorism, organised crime and serious organised crime.
Ensure compliance with national law when supplying information to Europol;
provide information on sources of information, such as:
terrorism,
terrorism and
serious crimes;
serious crime;
information relating to serious crimes falling outside the scope of the objectives of the regulation.
Each EU country must ensure that its competent authorities ensure that Europol has access to all relevant information it needs to fulfil these objectives.
In addition, each EU country may, via its national unit or, subject to paragraph 5, a competent authority, supply Europol:
with the information it requires;
with information it considers necessary for the fulfilment of its objectives;
inform Europol of:
the types of crime that are a priority for the EU,
the nature of the crime and the methods of investigation;
the number of persons involved in such crimes; and
the methods and methods of investigations.
Information exchange
EU country authorities must cooperate with each other in order to ensure that the information they provide Europol is accurate and reliable. They must also ensure that they comply with the law of the EU country in which the information is supplied.
Bilateral exchanges of information
The EU country which supplies the information Europol needs must ensure it complies with the national law of that country when providing it. It must also inform the European Commission and the European Judicial Network of the countries which supply it with information.
The Commission may, in accordance with its national law, use Europol's infrastructure, for such bilateral exchanges also to cover crimes that fall outside the EU's objectives. The Commission may also decide to use the Europol infrastructure for other bilateral exchanges.
Procedures and procedures
The Europol management board is composed of representatives of EU countries and is chaired by the Director-General of the Directorate-General for Law Enforcement Cooperation (DOL). It is assisted by the Executive Director, who is appointed by the European Parliament and the Council. The Executive Director reports to the Management Board, which meets at least twice a year. The Management Board is assisted, in particular, by the Cooperation Board. The European Police Cooperation Board may issue opinions, guidelines, recommendations and best practices. It may also adopt decisions on the implementation of its work programme.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 13 June 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 13 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Police Organisation (Epol) was established on 1 May 1999. It is the successor of the Convention based on Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, which set up the European Police Organization.
For more information, see:
About Europol (European External Action Service).

Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Council Regulation (EU).
-
Directive 2009/934/JHA.
Last updated: 13.06.2009

The European Commission must assess every 6 months whether the exceptional circumstances causing the severe disturbances in Member States still exist and report to the Council.
EU countries must use financial assistance primarily in support of their national short-time work schemes or similar measures, and, where applicable, in the support of relevant health-related measures.
Beneficiary countries must:
use financial assistance mainly in support for their national health schemes or other measures;
use it in the form of loans;
notify the European Commission every six months of the measures they have taken;
ensure that the loans are repaid;
inform the European Central Bank and the European Economic and Financial Affairs Committee of the amount of the loans they have granted.

Summary 37:

EU rules governing credit default resolutions (CCPs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/23 on the rules governing the credit default settlement and resolution of credit default settlements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

KEY POINTS
Scope

Resolution authorities, resolution colleges and involvement of European supervisory authorities
The resolution authorities and resolution colleges are designated by the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the European Banking Authority (EBA).

Implementation of the regulation

CCPs comply with all the rules laid down in the regulation;
the regulation is implemented in line with the requirements of the European Financial Services Authority (EFSA);
the resolution authorities are supervised by the EBA.
The EFSAs have the power to adopt delegated acts, such as delegated acts.
In addition, the European Commission has the right to adopt guidance on the application of the regulations.

CDS* and counterparty clearing (CCD);

cash equities;
debt instruments;
equity in resolution;
short-term securities;
credit instruments.

Summary 38:
European Green Deal for the rail sector
European green deal for the railway sector
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/865 on the European Green Deal
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
It sets out the EU’s strategy for the European Union (EU).
It aims to transform the EU into a climate-neutral society by 2050.
KEY POINTS
The EU is the only transport mode that has consistently reduced its greenhouse gas emissions and CO2 emissions since 1990. This has been achieved mainly thanks to the employees who have continued working under difficult and uncertain conditions. However, the situation in rural areas and in remote and rural areas means that the networks that guarantee the provision of basic services to the population are often fewer in number and less well-developed. This is why the EU needs to modernise and modernise its rail system.
The European Green deal is a new growth strategy that aims to tackle climate change by 2050, with a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy where there are no net emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use. It also aims to ensure that the EU is a climate neutral society by 2030.
Railways are a key element of this strategy. They contribute to the growth of the EU economy and to the sustainable development of the economy. They are also a major source of jobs and economic activity, as they provide the means of transport for many of the most deprived people in the EU.
However, the EU, as well as the transport sector, which is one of the main sources of income and employment in the country. The EU. This means that it is important for the development of economic growth and jobs in the rural areas, which are often excluded from the EU budget.
In order to achieve this, it is necessary to improve the efficiency of the rail system and to reduce its carbon emissions and carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions. To this end, the European Commission has adopted the European Year for Railways (Euratom 2020).
The year 2021 will represent the first full year where the rules agreed under the Fourth Railway Package will be implemented throughout the Union, namely on the opening of the market of domestic passenger services, on reducing costs and administrative burden for railway undertakings operating across the Union and on providing the European EU Agency for Railwayways (ERA) with additional tasks that aim to lower technical barriers.
By 31 March 2021, the Commission will inform the European Parliament and the Council of its plans for the implementation of the European year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The decision applies from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
The 2020 EU Green Deal is part of the broader European growth strategy, which also includes the European 2020 Agenda for Europe (see summary) and the European Climate Action Plan.
For more information, see:
EU Green Deal (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Railways: a transport system that transports people, goods and capital between the EU and the rest of the world.
* CO2: the greenhouse gas emitted by the burning of fossil fuels in the production, transport and use of energy.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Decision 2020/884/EU of 21 December 2020 on the implementation, on behalf of the Member States, of the Union Green Deal.

Summary 39:
European Security and Defence College (ESDC) - establishing a European security and defence College (2013-2020)
European Security & Defence College - establishing an European security & defence College
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2013/189/CFSP on establishing the European Security and Defense College
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a European Security & Defense College.
It sets up a European Union (EU) training and education centre for the defence and security of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The ESDC’s main tasks are to:
provide training and training activities for EU countries’ armed forces;
facilitate the exchange of experience and best practice between EU countries and international organisations, such as the United Nations Security Council and the International Institute for Strategic Studies (IISS);
facilitating the exchange and exchange of information between the ESDC and the European Defence College;
support the activities of the European Union Institute for Security Studies (EDC) and the EUISS;
contribute to the research and development of European defence policy.
Organisation and structure
The decision sets up an ESDC organisation and a network. The ESDC is governed by a Steering Committee, which is composed of representatives of EU countries. It is chaired by the European Council of Europe (CFSP) and comprises representatives from each EU country. It also includes representatives from the European External Action Service (EUISS), the European Parliament, the Council of the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy.
The Steering committee is responsible for the organisation and administration of ESDC. It meets at least twice a year. It has a budget of €1.5 million for the 2013-2020 period. The budget for the following years is set out in the decision:
€1.2 million for 2014-2020;
€3 million for 2021-2022; and
€4 million for 2024-2025.
Budgetary principles
The budget is based on the following budgetary principles:
budgetary principles apply to the expenditure:
revenue and expenditure must be in balance;
the ESDC must be financed by the EU budget;
recipients must be informed of the amount of the expenditure;
spend must be proportionate and proportionate to the number of participants in the training activities;
participation of non-EU countries in ESDC activities must not exceed 10% of the total number of students in each year;
EU countries must ensure that ESDC participants have the opportunity to participate in training activities organised by the institution.
Exemptions
The European Council may exclude from the scope of the decision certain activities that are not covered by the budget. These include:
training and education activities undertaken by EU countries;
research and development activities undertaken in the field of defence policy;
exchanges of information and best practices;
military training and defence policy research;
training activities conducted by the IISS and other relevant international organisations;
programmes and activities organised in cooperation with other relevant actors.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 22 April 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the first EU country to establish a military training and development centre. It was established in 2008.
For more information, see:
European Defence College — ESDC (European Commission).

Council Decision 2013/190/CFPS of 22 April 2014 establishing a new European Security And Defence College and repealing Council Joint Action 2008/550/ CFSP (OJ L 112, 24.4.2013, pp. 22-24)

Summary 40:
Reused water: minimum requirements for urban water reuse
Reclaimed water: maximum requirements for Urban Water Reuse
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2019 — minimum requirements to be met when urban water is reused for agricultural irrigation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum requirements on the use of treated urban waste water from urban wastewater treatment plants to be reused for agriculture.
It aims to protect the environment and human and animal health.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
reclaimed urban water* from urban waste treatment plants;
reused urban water from agricultural irrigation;
used agricultural irrigation from treated urban wastewater from urban water treatment plants.
EU countries must ensure that:
the reclamation facility operator has a water reuse risk management plan;
the competent authority in charge of producing, supplying and using reclaimed water has an action plan to ensure that the water is safe for use;
there is a risk assessment to be carried out to assess the impact of water reuse on the environment, including the risk of substances of emerging concern.
Risk assessment and risk management
The authority responsible for the management of reclaimed water must:
ensure that the reclamation facilities comply with the minimum requirements set out in the regulation’s annexes;
establish a water management plan for the purpose of producing and supplying reclaimed water;
identify the risks to the environment;
make sure that the plan is updated every 2 years;
inform the European Commission of the key elements of risk management.
The Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts in order to adapt to technical and scientific progress in the field of water management and to take into account the key aspects of the risk management set out by the competent authorities.

It entered into force on 1 July 2019.

Summary 41:
EU Blue Card — mobility of workers
European Blue Card
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/852 on the right of citizens of the Union and their family members to move and reside freely within the territory of the Member States
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU citizens have the right to move freely between the EU and its Member States.
It sets out the conditions under which they can do so.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU citizens, regardless of their nationality. It does not apply to non-EU citizens.
EU citizens who are not nationals of an EU Member State and who have not been granted an EU Blue Card (i.e. a residence permit) are excluded from the scope of the directive. However, EU citizens who have been admitted to the EU on the grounds that they are a threat to public health, public order, public security or public order may be granted a temporary residence permit.
Applicants must submit a complete application for an EU blue card to an EU country’s competent authority. The authority decides whether to grant or reject the application on the basis of a number of grounds, including:
the applicant's nationality;
the nature and duration of their stay in the Member State;
whether the applicant is a risk to public order or public health.
The authority decides on the granting or rejecting of the application within 90 days of receiving the application.
If an employer rejects an application, it must inform the applicant of the reasons for its decision and inform the family members of the reason. The family members must be informed as soon as possible.
When an employer refuses to grant an application for a blue card, the family member must be given the opportunity to apply for a new one. If the employer decides to grant a new card, that decision must be notified to the applicant's family members.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that family members have the same rights as nationals of the country in which they are admitted.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACE INTO FORCE?
 
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
This directive is part of a package of measures designed to improve the integration of migrants into the labour market in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘EU blue card’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* EU citizen: a citizen of the EU who has been granted a permanent residence permit by the competent authority of a Member State.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Directive 2018/884/EU of 21 July 2018 on the rights of citizens and their families to move, reside and work freely within their Member States and repealing Directive 2004/38/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council (OJ L 158, 30.5.2018, pp. 75-78)
RELATED DOCUMENTS

	
Commission Implementing Decision (EU, Euratom) 2019/856 of 27 June 2019 on the implementation of Directive (EU), Euratom on the accession of the Community to the Schengen acquis and on the conditions for the granting and granting of residence permits to nationals of Member States, and repealing Directives 64/221/EEC (EEC) No 1612/68 and 68/360/EC, 72/194/EEEC, 73/148/EEU, 75/34/EU and 75/35/Eec, 90/364/EC and 90/365/EC.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?

KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision sets out the EU’s migration policy. It aims to:
contribute to the prevention, control and reduction of irregular migration;
improve cooperation between EU countries and international organisations;

preventing, detecting, investigating, investigating and prosecuting illegal immigration;
providing information and assistance to border control authorities;
monitoring and assessing irregular migration flows;
developing effective border management strategies;
coordinating and sharing information and best practices.

they:
coordinate their border management policies;
take action to prevent, investigate, investigate and prosecute illegal immigration and irregular border crossing;
cooperate with non-EU countries;
identify and share information on irregular migration movements;
participate in border control operations.

women and children at risk;
unaccompanied minors;
persons in need of humanitarian admission for legal or physical protection needs, including victims of violence or torture.

Summary 43:
Equal treatment of men and women in the workplace
Equality of treatment of women and men in the workforce
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2019/24/EU on equal treatment between men and female workers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure equal treatment of workers in the European Union (EU) when it comes to maternity and paternity leave, paternity leave and parental leave.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
each worker has the right to leave at least 5 working days per year to carers’ leave;
every worker has access to flexible working arrangements to allow them to take care of their children during their absence from the workplace;
all workers who exercise their parental leave are entitled to a payment or an allowance in accordance with the principle of equal treatment.
The directive does not apply to workers engaged in an activity in a self-employed capacity and repealing Directive 2010/18/EU (see summary).
Employers must:
ensure that workers who take maternity or paternity leave have access to the same payment or allowance as those who do not;
make sure that the same treatment is given to workers who are engaged in the same activity, irrespective of whether they are men or women;
provide workers with access to a flexible working arrangement to enable them to exercise their right to parental leave, including the possibility of working on a part-time or full-time basis;
allow workers to take paternity leave on the same terms as men and conditions as men.
Workers’ rights
have access to:
a paid leave of at least five working days;
the right to paternity leave of up to 12 weeks;
access to a paid maternity leave of 12 weeks.
the possibility of taking parental leave in ways other than on a fulltime basis.
Employer’s’ obligations
EU employers must ensure:
that employees who take leave do not lose out on the benefits they receive;
employers must provide workers with information on the conditions under which they can exercise this right;
that workers who have taken maternity leave receive a payment and an allowance for the duration of their leave. They must also be informed of the reasons for any refusal of such a request or for any postponement of such arrangements.
In addition, EU countries must take into account the social partners’ role in the implementation of the directive.
EU rules on paternity leave
Each worker must have the same rights as men when taking paternity leave. This includes:
an equal share of the work-life balance between working hours and paid leave; and
the same access to paid leave as men, regardless of whether or not they are working in an employment contract, collective agreement or practice.
Flexible working arrangements
The employer must provide flexibility in terms of working arrangements, such as:
the number of working days and working hours,
the duration of paid leave,
time worked,
working hours and working conditions,
availability of childcare and childcare leave, and
availability and duration of parental leave and leave. Employers must also ensure that workers have the option of working from home or from a temporary accommodation.
Paternity leave
The same rights apply to both men* and women when taking care of a child.
Parental leave
Every worker must be able to take parental leave on a regular basis, including:
five working days during the working year;
six working days in the following months;
a maximum of 10 weeks during the following calendar year.
If a child is under the age of 18, each worker must take at least 10 working days of paid parental leave per year. However, the maximum number of weeks of paid paternity leave must not be less than 12 weeks per calendar year for men and 10 weeks for women.
Caring responsibilities
Each employee must be given the opportunity to take a maximum of 5 paid carers' leave during the year during which he/she is working. This must be:
at least 5 days per month;
at the same time as men;
on the same pay;
in accordance with national law, collective agreements or practice, and taking account of the powers delegated to social partners.
Exemptions
In the case of children under 18, the directive provides for a maximum amount of unpaid leave of 6 weeks per year for women and 3 weeks per month for men. It does not, however, apply to men under 18.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 2 August 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve the rights of women working in the labour market.
For more information, see:
‘Equality for women’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
   
Council Directive 2019/23/EU of 22 June 2019 on the right of women to leave the workplace and to care for their



Summary 44:
Fraud and other criminal offences affecting the EU’s financial interests
Fraud
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/849 on criminal offences and sanctions relating to fraud and other crimes affecting the Union's financial interests.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It establishes minimum rules relating to the definition of fraud and sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the European Union (EU).
It aims to contribute to the efforts to fight those criminal phenomena.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
fraud affecting EU financial interests (i.e. the EU's budget) and
other criminal offences involving fraud.
It does not apply to the following offences:
any attempt to commit fraud;
any breach of the confidentiality of a contract or other agreement between the EU and a third party.
EU countries must take the necessary measures to prevent, investigate, prosecute and bring to justice those who commit these offences. These measures include:
suspension or revocation of accession to the EU;
restrictions on access to EU financial services;
suspending or revoking of the authorisation of the use of EU funds;
imposing sanctions on individuals who commit fraud affecting EU finances.
Exemptions from sanctions
The Directive does not oblige EU countries to provide for sanctions of imprisonment for the commission of criminal offences that are not of a serious nature, in cases where intent is presumed under national law. However, EU countries may impose sanctions for the following:
the commissioning of a criminal offence;
the participation in or participation in a criminal organisation;
participation in the commissioning or participation of such an offence.
any other criminal offence involving fraud, including the participation in the organisation of, or involvement in, a criminal group.
Sanctions
The EU countries must:
impose sanctions on persons who commit or participate in acts of fraud or other crimes involving fraud affecting their financial interests;
ensure that those responsible for such acts are subject to effective, proportionate, dissuasive and dissuasive sanctions.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 6 July 2016.

Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Summary 45:
E-payment fees
E-payments fees
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/13/EU on payment card fees
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that payment card interchange fees are set at an economically efficient level, taking into account the structure of domestic and cross-border payment card markets.
It sets out rules for the setting of interchange fees.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the following types of payment transactions:
transactions with commercial cards;
cash withdrawals at automatic teller machines or at the counter of a payment service provider;
payments with payment cards issued by three party payment card schemes.
EU countries must ensure that the interchange fees applicable to these types of transactions are set in a way that does not distort competition between payment card issuers and payment service providers.
In addition, the directive sets out a list of payment card acceptance services that must be provided to merchants. These services must be free of charge and must be accessible to all payment card users, irrespective of their nationality.
The European Commission must publish a report on the implementation of the directive every 2 years on the basis of the information it receives from EU countries. This report must include information on:
the interchange fees and scheme fees that are applicable to each category and brand of payment cards; and
the amount of the merchant service charges, interchange fees, scheme fees and payment application fees applicable.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
It has applied since 20 April 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 20 April 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU’s payment card market has been fragmented and fragmented for several reasons.
For more information, see:
‘E-Payments’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Interchange fee: the interchange fee charged by a payment card issuer to a merchant to enable it to process payment transactions.
MAIN DOCUMENT
                 Directive 2015/14/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 19 April 2015 on the payment card fee structure and business rules for electronic payments (OJoint implementation of Directive 2014/24/EU and Directive 2014-2020/EU).
Sectors of the Directive
Payment card fees: interchange fees charged to merchants for the processing of transactions involving payment cards.
Co-badging and choice of payment brand or payment application: the agreement between a payment company and a payment application provider to accept payment cards from a merchant.
Amendments to Directive 2015/15/EU
Amending Directive (EU, Euratom) 2016/852 amends Directive (EC) No 552/EU to:
introduce a new type of interchange fee for payment card transactions;
set out the list of card acceptance service providers that must provide payment card services to merchants;
establish a common set of fees for card acceptance;
ensure that interchange fees for payment cards are calculated according to the volume of transactions that are processed by the payment company;
make it easier for payment companies to set interchange fees based on the number of transactions they process per day.

Summary 47:
Data collection and transmission of data on transport traffic
Data collection, transmission and dissemination of information on transportation traffic
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the transmission of transport traffic data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the transmission and collection of statistics on the movement of goods and passengers in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must send the European Commission (Eurostat) a report containing such information and data as it may request in order to verify the quality of the data transmitted.
Eurostat transmits the data to Eurostat, the EU’s statistical office, which then forwards it to the European Economic and Social Committee (EASC).
The Commission then transmits these data to the EASC, which forwards them to the Council of the EU.
Each EU country must send Eurostat a report on the data it receives. The Commission then forwards the report to the Commission.
The data transmitted must be sent in the form of an annual report. The data must include:
the year in which the data were transmitted;
the type of transport and the number of movements of goods, passengers and vehicles involved;
a description of the types of vehicles involved and the types and quantities of goods transported.
Reporting country
Each year, Eurostat sends the Commission a report with the information it receives from each EU country. The report must contain the following information:
a list of the vehicles involved in the data transmission,
the number of vehicles and the type of passengers and goods transported;
number of vehicles transporting the data.
the total number of persons and goods transiting the data;
total number of goods transited the data, and
the volume of goods moved by vehicle.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLYEAR IN ENTRY INTO FORCE?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts. These delegated acts are necessary to ensure the proper functioning of the statistical system.
For more information, see:
‘Transport data’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Vessel traffic: the total number and quantity of goods moving across the EU by road, rail, inland waterway, inland waterways and inland waterways.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 December 2013 on the submission of transport data (codified version) (OJ L 127, 1.2.2013, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
 
  The European Commission adopted Regulation (EC) No 652/2014 on the exchange of information between the European Statistical System (ESS) and the European Transport Statistics System (ETS).

Statistics on railway transport by rail
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 on the transmission of railway statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the collection and transmission of statistics on the movement of passengers by rail in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the rules for the transmission and analysis of railway passenger and freight statistics.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
passenger-kilometres (passenger kilometres);
transport of goods by rail;
traffic by rail.
EU countries must transmit the statistics to Eurostat, the European Statistical System for Railways (Eurostat).
Transmission of data
The data must be collected in the form of the following:
the number of passengers carried by rail and the number of goods transported;
the type of transport;
types of transport.
Data collection
Each EU country must send Eurostat a report on the data it receives every 2 years.
The report must include the following information:
total number of passenger kilometres carried;
number of freight movements;
type of transport and type of freight;
percentage of freight carried.
Each year, Eurostat must send a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the results of the data collected.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACE APPLY?
From 1 January 2017.
It applies from 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
It repeals and repeals Directive (EC, Euratom) No 91/2003.
For more information, see:
Railway statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
Passenger-km: a unit of measure representing the distance of one passenger by rail over a distance of 1 kilometre.
Train passenger: a person travelling by rail from one railway station to another.
Rail freight: a shipment of goods moving by rail between two railway stations.

Summary 49:
Exemption from the visa requirement for biometric passports
Exemption of biometric passport holders
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 539/2001 on the exemption from visa requirement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for granting the EU’s biometric visa exemption to non-EU citizens who are not nationals of the EU countries.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC), which had to become law in the European Union (EU) countries by 2001.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
non-EU nationals who have a valid biometric identity card (i.e. a passport issued by the EU country in which they are resident;
visas issued by EU countries’ consulates;
travel documents issued by third countries;
biometric passports issued by special administrative regions of the countries of the People's Republic of China, Hong Kong SAR, Macao SAR, Taiwan and Hong Kong.
Exemptions from visa requirements
EU countries may grant the exemption if:
the holder of a biometric identification card is not a national of an EU country;
the applicant has a valid travel document issued by a country other than the EU;
there is no reason to believe that the holder of the biometric card will not be able to return to the EU.
The exemption does not apply to holders of passports issued in the EU, unless:
there are grounds for doubt about the validity of the card; or
the person concerned has been convicted of a criminal offence; or is suspected of committing a crime.
There are exceptions to the above, such as:
for the protection of the health or safety of the person concerned
the protection of personal data
the security of the personal data of the individual concerned.
Authorisation to travel
The European Commission must adopt a delegated act setting out the criteria for the granting the visa exemption. The Commission must consult experts designated by each EU country on the basis of the principles laid down in the Interinstitutional Agreement of 13 April 2016 on Better Law-Making.
Delegated acts
The Commission adopts the delegated act within 90 days of receiving the notification of the existence of visa-related circumstances. It must amend the annexes to the regulation, taking into account the available resources in the consulates of the Member States and shall amend Annex II accordingly.
Implementing act
The implementing act must be adopted by the Commission within one month of:
receiving the notification;
being made aware of the information referred to in paragraph 3;
presenting a report on the report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee on Foreign Affairs and Security Policy.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It enters into force on 1 January 2016.

Summary 50:
European Union Agency for Criminal Justice Cooperation (Eurojust)
European Union agency for criminal justice cooperation (EUROJUST)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 establishing the European Union Agency on Criminal Justice cooperation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the Eurojust, a European Union (EU) agency for the prevention and investigation of criminal offences.
It sets out the tasks and objectives of Eurojust.
KEY POINTS
Scope
Eurojust’s competence covers criminal offences related to the criminal offences listed in Annex I of the regulation. It also covers:
criminal offences committed in order to procure the means of committing the serious crimes listed in Annex I;
custodial offences;
reciprocal access to justice;
the prevention, investigation and prosecution of serious crimes.
The regulation does not apply to:
illegal immigration;
illegal drug trafficking;
trafficking in human beings;
human trafficking of weapons of mass destruction.
Objectives and tasks
The European Union agency has a number of objectives and tasks, including:
promoting cooperation between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities in the fight against organised crime;
encouraging the exchange of information and best practice in criminal matters;
providing information and assistance to the judicial authorities of EU countries;
ensuring the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms;
facilitating cooperation between national judicial authorities;
exchanges of information between national authorities and Eurojust staff;
participating in investigations and prosecutions;
monitoring and sharing of information on criminal offences committed by EU countries and between national countries.
EurOJurisdictional cooperation
The EU countries must cooperate with Eurojust in the following areas:
collecting, analysing and disseminating information on serious crimes;
cooperating with the European Judicial Network for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms (ENHRF);
exchanging information and advice on criminal matters.
EU countries must also cooperate with each other on:
the protection of the rights of victims and victims’ families;
preventing, investigating and prosecuting serious crimes committed in their territory;
protecting human rights, fundamental freedoms and the fundamental freedoms of EU citizens;
establishing a European Criminal Justice Network (ECJN).
The ECJN is responsible for:
coordinating and sharing information and expertise with the national authorities of each EU country;
assisting the European Commission in the implementation of its tasks;
advising and advising the European Parliament and the Council of the EU on Eurojust;
developing and implementing Eurojust's strategic guidance.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The Eurojust statute was amended by Council Decisions 2003/659/JHA (3) and 2009/426/JH (4).
For more information, see:
European Justice Network — Eurojust (European Commission).

Summary 51:
Fighting money laundering: criminal law
Fighting cash laundering: combating money laundering
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/849 on combating the financing of terrorism
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat money laundering by means of criminal law, enabling more efficient and swifter cross-border cooperation between EU countries’ authorities.
It sets out rules on the freezing and confiscation of instrumentalities and proceeds of crime in criminal matters.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
money laundering;
the financing of terrorist activities; and
the funding of organised crime.
EU countries must cooperate in criminal proceedings regarding money laundering. They must ensure that information is exchanged in an effective and timely manner in accordance with national law and the existing EU legal framework.
In criminal proceedings, EU countries must assist each other in the widest possible way and ensure that the information exchanged between them is accurate, reliable and timely.
National authorities must:
ensure that:
the information they provide is accurate and reliable;
information is shared with the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAFT) and the European Judicial Network on money laundering and terrorist financing;
they cooperate with the national authorities of non-EU countries in order to prevent, detect, investigate and prosecute money laundering offences.
They must also:
notify OLAF and the OLAFT of any information they receive on suspected money laundering activities;
take the necessary measures to detect and prosecute those suspected of money laundering; and prosecute the individuals suspected of involvement in such activities.
report to OLAF the information they have provided to them.

Summary 52:
EU control system for ensuring compliance with the rules of the common fisheries policy for bluefin tuna (ICCAT Recommendation)
EU control systems for enforcing the rules on the conservation of bluefin trout
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 establishing a Union control system to ensure compliance with rules of EU fisheries policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) control system that ensures that the EU’s fisheries policy (CFP) is implemented in line with the International Convention for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to fishing in the eastern Atlantic and the Mediterranean. It does not apply to tuna caught in the Black Sea.
EU countries must ensure that:
fisheries are not caught in excess of the quota;
fishing vessels and traps are caught within the limits set out in the regulation;
captures and transfers of fish are carried out in accordance with the catch and transfer procedures laid down in Annex II of the regulation.
Catch and transfer of fish
Each year, each EU country must send a list of designated ports to the International Commission for the Control of Pollution from the Fishing of Bluefin Tunas, the ICCAT Secretariat, which transmits this information to the Commission.
The Commission then transmits the list to the European Commission, which then forwards it to the EU countries’ fisheries control authorities. The Commission then forwards the information to ICCAT.
If a vessel or trap is found to be in breach of the rules, it must inform the EU country of its flag and submit a report to the Council of the reasons for the infringement. The report must include:
the number and average weight of fish caught and transferred;
the name and registration number of the vessel and the number and weight of the catch;
a description of the type of fishing gear and equipment used;
details of the number of catches and transfers made.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLY?
From 15 February 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
The ICCAT recommends that EU countries:
ensure the conservation and sustainable management of the bluefin species;
promote the sustainable management and sustainable use of the fish they catch.
For more information, see:
Bluefin tuna conservation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bluefin tuna: a species of tuna that is threatened with extinction due to overfishing.
* Polluter: an individual or group of individuals who are involved in the commercial fishing of fish.

Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1021/2009 of 20 November 2009 establishing a system for enforcing rules of Union fisheries policy.

Summary 53:
Ensuring fair competition in the EU’s maritime industry: injurious pricing of goods
Enesco rules
Ensco rules ensuring fair competition within the European Union (EU).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on injurious prices of goods in the maritime industry
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
It aims to ensure fair competition between EU countries’ customs authorities in the import and export of goods.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 385/96.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to imports of goods into the EU from non-EU countries. It does not apply to:
non-EU vessels that are built or built in the European Economic Area (EEA);
ships built in non-EEA countries;
ships constructed or built outside the EEA.
EU countries must ensure that:
the import of goods to the EU is free of charge;
the customs authorities do not deny loading and unloading rights to vessels subject to the denial of loading and loading rights.
Customs authorities must:
ensure that the vessel is registered in an EU country that has the right to unload and unload goods;
not deny loading or unload to vessels which are not registered in the country in which the vessel was built;
allow the vessel to be loaded or unloaded if the vessel has been delivered before the extended delivery date.
If a vessel is not registered, the customs authorities must inform the shipbuilder of the reasons for its extension of delivery. The shipbuilder must prove that the extension of the delivery date was for normal commercial reasons and not to avoid the application of this regulation.
Shipbuilders must also prove that they have the right of unloading or unloading of the vessel.
Exemptions from the regulation
The EU countries may exclude from the scope of the regulation:
ships which are built in an EEA country other than that of the EU country where they are registered. Such vessels are not subject to this regulation, unless they are:
built in a country where there is a duty on the shipbuilding company to ensure that they comply with this regulation;
built by a shipbuilder that is a member of the Union industry;
made by a company that is not part of that EU industry.
Implementation
The European Commission must adopt implementing measures to ensure the proper functioning of the system. These measures include:
monitoring and reporting of infringements;
implementing measures to prevent, investigate and settle infringements.
The Commission must also:
take the necessary measures to protect the interests of the shipping industry, including:
identifying infringements of competition law;
ensuring that vessels are registered and unregistered;
establishing a register of vessels that have been extended delivery dates;
developing a list of ships that have not been delivered.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Injunctive pricing’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 December 2013 on injudicious pricing of products in the marine industry (codified version) (OJ L 136, 31.12.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 54:
Cableway safety: EU conformity assessment and conformity assessment
CABLEWAY safety: Europe-wide standards
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on European standards for cableway safety
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU-wide rules on the European standardisation of safety components and subsystems of cableway installations.
It aims to improve the safety of cableways in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
cableway installations, such as funicular railways, aerial ropeways (cable cars, gondolas, chairlifts) and drag lifts, as well as cable cars and cable cars used for the transport of people, goods, capital and capital goods;
the components of these installations, including: subsystems* and safety components*;
cabling systems used for cable cars;
bicyclists’ equipment;
motor vehicles;
trailers used for road transport.
Manufacturers, importers and distributors must ensure that their products comply with the essential requirements set out in the regulation.
They must also ensure that:
their products are designed and constructed in such a way as to ensure that they meet the essential safety requirements;
they have a CE conformity assessment* marking and EU declaration of conformity.
EU countries must notify the European Commission and the Member States’ competent authorities of the products they consider to be in compliance with these essential requirements within 30 days of receiving a CE marking or EU declaration.
Importers must inform the Commission of the results of the conformity assessment.
The Commission and Member States must take all necessary measures to ensure the safe operation of the cableway installation.
If an EU country considers that an installation does not comply with essential requirements, it must issue a new authorisation for entry into service. This authorisation must be valid for 30 years from the date of its issue.
In the event that important characteristics, subsystems or safety components of existing cableways undergo modifications for which an authorisation is required by the Member State concerned, such modifications and their repercussions on the installation as a whole must be notified to the competent authority of the country in which the installation is located.
A notified body must submit a report to the Commission every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation’s essential requirements. This report must include information on:
the number of installations covered by the regulation;
a description of the risks to the safety and health of users;
how the installation complies with the necessary safety requirements.
Where a notified body considers that a cableway does not meet essential requirements for the safety or health of its users, it may withdraw its authorisation and issue a notification to the EU country in question. This notification must be accompanied by a copy of the quality system approval decision(s) issued by the notified body.
Annex II to the regulation sets out the responsibilities of manufacturers and importers of products. It also sets out a list of the EU countries’ authorities responsible for the monitoring and control of the installation. The Commission must take the necessary measures in the event of non-compliance.

The consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for persons with disabilities
Assistance of people with disabilities — European Union (EU) accessibility rules
Assistive devices and services
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/65/EU on accessibility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the accessibility of products, services and devices for persons who have disabilities.
It sets out minimum requirements to ensure that these products are compatible with assistive technologies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
products and services designed and manufactured for the use of persons who are blind, visually impaired, partially sighted, partially or totally sighted or with other disabilities;
electronic devices, including mobile phones, computers, tablets and peripherals;
accessibility of the information and communication technologies (ICTs) used in these products;
information and communication services used by persons who use these products.
Products and services must comply with the following requirements:
the product must meet the accessibility requirements laid down in Annex I of the directive.
The product must be designed and constructed in such a way that it meets the requirements of the European Parliament and of the Council of Europe’s Committee on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (CRPD).
it must be compatible with:
assistive technologies*;
visual aids* and tactile relief format or sound in addition to a text warning so that blind persons can perceive it.
it should be able to handle real-time text conversations so that persons with hearing or visual impairments can exchange information in an interactive way;
people who are hard of hearing and/or visually impaired can communicate in a non-verbal communication mode;
mobile phones and tablets should be capable of handling real time text conversations;
telecommunications services should provide information in interactive ways.
In addition, the directive sets out the requirements for:
support services:
where available, support services (help desks, call centres, technical support, relay services, training services and training services) must provide information on accessibility of the product and its compatibility with accessibility technologies, in accessible modes of communication.
EU countries’ national authorities:
must ensure that the products comply with these requirements;
make sure that the information they provide is accessible to persons who require it;
ensure that the product is compatible with the accessibility technologies used in the products they sell.
Manufacturers must ensure that their products meet these requirements.
They must also ensure that:
they comply with other EU legislation on accessibility, such as Regulation (EC) No 261/2004 on the universal design of assistive devices (see summary);
they provide technical specifications for products that meet the requirements set out in Annex II to the directive;
they ensure that products that do not meet these specifications are available for sale in the EU and that they can be easily traced back to the manufacturer.
If a product does not meet all the requirements, it must be replaced by a product that does.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 28 June 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 28 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the accessibility directive. The report concludes that the directive has had a positive impact on the development of the market for accessible products in the European Union.
For more information, see:
‘Accessible products’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Accessibility: the ability of a person with a disability to use a product or a service without discrimination on the basis of their disability.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  The accessibility directive (Directive 14.6.2014/EU)
1.
Accessibility (OJ L 136, 28.5.2014, pp. 1-8)

Summary 56:
Supply chain due diligence: traceability of minerals and metals — European Union rules
Supply Chain due diligence : traceability and traceability in the supply chain
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on supply chain due-dealing of minerals
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s supply chain* is traceable.
It sets out the rules for the implementation of supply chain traceability.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all metals and minerals imported into the EU from non-EU countries. It does not apply to gold, diamonds, platinum, precious metals and precious metals.
EU importers must ensure that:
they carry out their own supply chain checks and audits of their own products;
they ensure that they carry out independent third-party checks on their supply chain.
They must also carry out checks on importers’ own suppliers.
In addition, they must carry out audits of the suppliers’ supply chain to check that they have carried out due diligence correctly.
The European Commission must review the regulation every 3 years to assess the functioning and effectiveness of the regulation.
Importers must:
ensure that their own checks are carried out by independent third parties;
carry out audits on their own suppliers;
check that the importers have complied with the relevant EU rules;
report to the Commission on the results of their checks.
If an importers fail to comply with this regulation, they may be subject to sanctions, such as a ban on trade in certain metals or minerals.
Ex-post checks on EU importers
EU countries must ensure their importers carry out an ex-post check on their imports of certain metals, minerals and minerals. They must keep records of such checks for at least 5 years.
Recognition of due diligence schemes
The Commission must recognise supply chain-based due-diplomatic and supply-chain due diligence* schemes. These schemes must be established by the EU countries’ competent authorities and must be publicly available on the internet.
When an EU country does not recognise a supply chain scheme, it must inform the European Commission of the reasons for its decision. The Commission must then decide whether to grant the scheme recognition. If the Commission considers that the scheme does not comply with the regulation, it may decide to withdraw its recognition.
Review
The EU must review this regulation every three years. It must also review it every 5 years to ensure its effectiveness.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
This regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The supply chain tracing of minerals is a key element in the fight against conflict and illegal exploitation of minerals. It is also essential for the sustainable development of the world economy.
For more information, see:
‘The Supply Chain Traceability of Minerals’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 57:
Rules governing the destruction or disposal otherwise of goods by customs authorities
Rules governing:
Destruction or disposal other of goods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2009 — rules governing the disposal or destruction of customs goods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the destruction, disposal or disposal of customs-related goods by the customs authorities.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1013/97.
KEY POINTS
   
The regulation lays down the rules governing: destruction, disposing of goods and the disposal of non-originating goods. It does not apply to goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that:
goods are destroyed or disposed of in accordance with the law of the EU country in which they were imported;
the goods are disposed of according to the rules laid down in the regulation’s annexes;
customs authorities must inform the European Commission of the goods they destroy or dispose of.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to determine the cases referred to in point (d) of Article 102(1) of the regulation, in order to determine whether the goods are exempted from notification of the customs debt.
In addition, the regulation sets out the conditions for temporary storage of goods for the purpose of temporary processing.
Special provisions relating to non-origin goods
The EU goods may be entered, stored, moved, used, processed, processed or consumed in a free zone.
A temporary storage facility may be set up in an EU country, provided that it meets certain conditions.
Temporary storage facilities must comply with the following conditions:
temporary storage must be located in an area free from customs duties;
they must be free of charge to the EU customs authorities;
there must be no charge for the temporary storage.
There must be a minimum number of temporary storage facilities for each EU country.
An authorised economic operator for customs simplifications must be deemed to comply with this obligation, insofar as his or her records are appropriate for the purposes of the operation of the facility.
Importers’ rights and obligations
The customs authorities must notify the EU countries’ authorities of:
the name and address of the consignee,
the date on which the goods were brought into the territory of the country in question and
the amount of customs duty they have paid on the goods.
Customs authorities may refuse to notify the customs authority of a customs debt if they consider that the goods do not meet the conditions laid down by the regulation.
Exceptions
The European Commission may decide to exempt from notification certain goods which are not subject to customs duties.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 June 2009.
BACKGROUND
The original regulation was adopted in 1997 and had to become law in the European Union countries by 1 June 1997.
For more information, see:
‘Destruction and disposal of goods’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Destruction: the destruction of goods in the event that they are found to be in breach of customs rules.
