Summary 58:
EU rules on intellectual property infringement
EU rules governing intellectual property infringements
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1383/2003 on the infringement of intellectual property rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that intellectual property (IP) rights are protected when goods are imported into the EU from outside the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the import, export or importation of goods that are considered to be infringing.
It does not apply to:
the manufacture, importation or export of goods for commercial purposes;
the marketing of goods which are intended to be used in a way that infringe an intellectual property right (i.e. that is contrary to the law of the EU’s general principles of freedom of expression, creativity and innovation);
the sale of goods intended for use in a commercial context.
Importers’ obligations
EU countries must ensure that the importation, export and import of goods is accompanied by a formal notification to the European Commission.
Customs authorities must:
notify the EU countries’ competent customs authorities of the import of infringing goods;
ensure that the customs authorities have all the necessary information to determine whether the goods are infringing;
take the necessary measures to prevent, investigate and prosecute infringements.
The customs authorities must also:
suspend the release of or detain the goods if they suspect that they are infringing the intellectual property* rights;
make a decision to suspend the release or detention of the goods.
take measures to prosecute the infringements if they consider that these infringements are likely to lead to serious damage to the rights of the right-holders, users or groups of producers.
In addition, EU countries must take measures to protect the interests of consumers and to ensure the protection of their health and safety.
Expiry of goods
The European Commission must inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the reasons for suspending or detaining the goods they consider to infringe intellectual property.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLACE?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2003.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat intellectual property theft.
For more information, see:
‘Defending intellectual property’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Regulation (EU) No 1303/2003 of 21 June 2003 on the infringement of intellectual Property Rights (OJ L 183, 1.7.2003, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom). This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 59:
EU customs programme for cooperation in the field of customs
Union customs programme: 2021-2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/2027 establishing the Union Customs programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the Union customs programme (‘Customs programme’) for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries’ customs authorities in the area of customs.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme aims to:
promote cooperation between customs authorities;
improve the efficiency and effectiveness of customs authorities’ work;
contribute to the prevention and control of cross-border trade in goods and services;
provide technical assistance to customs authorities.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme include:
improving the effectiveness and efficiency of customs procedures;
promoting customs cooperation;
encouraging the exchange of information and best practice;
developing customs information systems;
enhancing customs information and exchange of best practice.
Implementation and forms of EU funding
The regulation sets out the following forms of funding:
EU funding for the implementation of the work programme:
the European Commission’s general budget;
the general budget of the European Union Agency for Customs Cooperation (EUAC);
the General Fund for Economic and Social Affairs (EUASA) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
The EUAC and the EUASA are the main sources of funding for implementing the programme.
The European Commission is responsible for the overall budget for the programme, while the EUA is the main source of funding.
EU work programme
The work programme is implemented by the Commission. The work programme covers a number of objectives, including:
monitoring, monitoring, evaluation, verification, verification and control;
exchange of information on customs procedures and best practices;
assessment of the impact of customs legislation on the economy and the environment;
development of a customs information system;
research and development of customs information technology (CIT) systems.
Exchange of best practices
The Commission will continue to provide for the possibility of involving external experts within the meaning of Article 238 of the Financial Regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 11 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
The customs programme is part of a package of measures designed to promote cooperation between the EU countries in order to improve the efficiency of their customs procedures.
For more information, see:
‘EU Customs Programme’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Customs: a customs procedure or system for the prevention, control and enforcement of goods or services imported into the EU.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1165/2019 of 11 March 2019 establishing the Customs programme for Cooperation in the Field of Customs (OJ L 31, 11.3.2019, pp. 1-8)

Equal Opportunities and Access to the Labour Market: EUTS
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 on the implementation of the Pillar of social innovation and Employment of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It aims to improve the quality of life of workers in the EU by promoting equal opportunities and equal opportunities for workers.
It sets out the EU’s approach to the social inclusion of women and men.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision applies to all EU countries’ public employment services and social partners. It does not apply to the European Social Fund (ESF+) or the European Pillar of Employment and Social Innovation.
EUTS aims to:
promote equal opportunities;
improve working conditions;
ensure fair working conditions and social protection and inclusion;
encourage the participation of women in decision-making processes;
increase participation of men and women in labour market activities.
Funding and methods of implementation
EU funding
The European Union (EU) finances the Pillar by means of grants and co-financing from the general budget of the EU.
The main sources of funding are:
the general budget;
the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF); and
the EU social partners’ contributions to the Pillar.
Co-funded priority or programme priorities or programmes.
Specific priority or programmes
The EU funding is divided into 3 categories:
1.priority or programme:
a priority programme for social inclusion.
2.programmes:
social inclusion: a priority programme, which aims to promote equal opportunities, including:
equal opportunities for women;
access to labour market;
fair working conditions.
3.programme:
recreational and vocational training.
4.employment services:
e.g. social protection.
5.education.
6.training.
7.access to work.
8.employment mobility.
9.employment rights.
EU countries must ensure that:
women and men have equal opportunities to equal opportunities in terms of access to employment;
women have access to equal pay and equal working conditions, irrespective of their place of residence;
people with disabilities have equal access to education and training;
children have the same opportunities as men, regardless of their age.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 July 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND

provides information on its activities to the other parties;

is allowed to present its views on policy proposals to the Commission;

contributes effectively to the evidence base on which policy proposals are made.

Summary 66:
Suspension of budgetary surveillance in the euro area
Sanctions against Member States that deviate significantly from the medium-term budgetary objective or the appropriate adjustment path towards that objective and fail to correct the deviation
Sanctioning of Member States whose deficit below the 3 % of Gross Domestic Product (GDP) reference value and fails to correct their deviation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2011 on the imposition of sanctions in the preventive and corrective parts of the Stability and Growth Pact
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the sanctions applicable to Member States which deviate from the 3% of gross domestic product reference value* (GDDP) in the budgetary surveillance (SGP) part of the European Union (TFEU).
It aims to ensure that Member States’ budgetary surveillance is more effective and to ensure the proper functioning of the economic and monetary union (EMU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all Member States with a deficit below 3 % GDP. It does not apply to:
non-euro area Member States;
euro area countries that have a deficit above 3 %;
EU countries with a budget deficit below 2 % of GDP.
It does not cover:
the European Commission’s budgetary surveillance programme;
the budgetary surveillance of the EU countries’ balance of payments.
Suspensions
Member States must notify the European Commission of the amount of interest and non-interest-bearing deposits that they have lodged with the European Central Bank (ECBanks) and the fines they have imposed on them.
The Commission must publish a report on the application of the regulation every 5 years.
Fines
The amount of the interest-bearing deposit and of the fines must be set in such a way as to ensure a fair graduation of sanctions and to provide sufficient incentives for EU countries whose currency is the euro to comply with the fiscal framework of the Union. The Commission may, on grounds of exceptional economic circumstances or following a reasoned request by the Member State concerned addressed to the Commission within 10 days of adoption of the Council decision under Article 126(6) TFEU referred to in paragraph 1, recommend that the Council reduce the amount or cancel it.
If a Member State does not lodge a non-Interest-Banking Deposit with the Commission, the fine must be converted into a fine.
A Member State must inform the Commission as soon as possible of any misrepresentations it has made to the European Parliament, the Council or the European Economic and Financial Committee (EEC). The Commission must conduct all investigations necessary to establish the existence of the misrepresentations and to determine the extent to which they were made.
In addition, the Commission must report to the EEC on the results of its investigations and on the sanctions it has imposed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 14 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the SGP.
For more information, see:
‘Sustainable growth and jobs’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Fiscal surveillance: the process by which the Commission assesses whether a country deviates significantly from its medium- or long-term budget objective or adjustment path.
* Budget surveillance: this is the process in which the EU monitors the budgetary position of a country in terms of its economic and financial situation.

Summary 67:
Statistical data and metadata for the European Union (EU)
Statistical information and data collection
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 on statistical data and data transmission
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the transmission of statistical data to the European Commission (Eurostat) of the EU’s statistics.
It aims to improve the quality and comparability of EU-wide statistical data.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ national statistics. It does not apply to EU countries that are not members of the European Economic Area (EEA).
It applies to:
EU countries that do not participate in the EU statistical system;
non-EU countries which do not have a national statistical system.
The data are transmitted by Eurostat to Eurostat, which then transmits them to the Commission.
Eurostat must ensure that the data and the metadata are accurate and comparable to those of other EU countries. It must also ensure that there is no distortion of the quality of the statistical data or of the programme of the metadata.
Programme of the statistics and metadata
Each EU country must adopt a programme of data and of metadata to be transmitted to fulfil the requirements of this regulation. The programme must include:
a programme for the geographical level;
programme for the national level; and
programmes for the regional and local levels.
Data collection and transmission must be carried out in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EU Statistical Code of Practice (EU Statistical Code of Procedure).
The European Commission must:
ensure that Eurostat has access to all the data it receives;
establish a programme to transmit the statistics it receives.
Each year, Eurostat must send Eurostat a report on the programme it has adopted to the Council and the European Parliament. This report must include information on:
the programme Eurostat adopts;
the methodology, definitions and programme of statistics it uses;
data quality;
a description of the methodology and programme;
information on the metadata it transmits;
how the programme is transmitted;
measures to ensure comparability between the programme and the data;
methodology and programme to ensure the accuracy of the data transmitted.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘EU statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Statistical data: data on the population of an EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2006 of 21 December 2006 on statistical information and statistical data transmission (OJ L 283, 24.12.2006, pp. 1-8)

Summary 69:
European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS) — rules on the processing of personal data (2018-2021)
European Data Processing Supervisor (EU) 2018-2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001 establishing an independent authority responsible for ensuring that the fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons, and in particular their right to data protection, with respect to processing of their personal data, are respected by the European Union institutions, bodies, offices and agencies.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes an independent European Union authority, referred to as the European Data Protection Supervisory Supervisor.
KEY POINTS
The European Union institution, bodies and agencies responsible for processing personal data are subject to the responsibility of the European data protection supervisor. The supervisor is responsible for:
ensuring that the protection of the rights of the natural persons whose data are processed by the EU institutions and bodies;
protecting the privacy and security of the personal data of the individuals concerned;
collecting, analysing and analysing the data of EU citizens and ensuring that their rights are respected.
The supervisor’s tasks include:
monitoring the processing and storage of data;
assisting the European Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) in the field of data protection;
identifying and investigating data security breaches;
providing the Commission with information on data breaches.
In addition, the supervisor:
assists the Commission in its work on data security and privacy matters;
ensures that data protection is protected;
coverses the information they collect;
coordinates with the Commission and the EDPB;
prepares and disseminates information to the public on data protection issues;
examines the data protection policies and procedures of EU institutions;
reviews and evaluates data protection policy and procedures;
is responsible for the implementation of EU data protection rules and procedures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLANTS APPLY?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The establishment of an independent EU authority to ensure that the rights, freedoms and data protection are respected is one of the objectives of the Lisbon Treaty.
For more information, see:
‘The EU’ website.
* KEY TERMS
* European data Protection Supervisor: an independent body responsible for protecting the rights and liberties of human beings.
ACT

Summary 70:
EU’s own resources for the period 2021-2027
EU‘s own funds for the periods 2021-27
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/852/EU on the EU’ s own resources
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
 
It sets up a system of EU own resources to cover annual appropriations for payments to the European Commission.
It aims to contribute to the development of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the rules and procedures for the collection, management and use of the EU's own resources.
EU countries' own resources are used to cover the annual appropriations to the Commission's general budget (MFF) and the general budget of the Member States.
The amount of own resources allocated to the EU must not exceed 1.40 % of the sum of all Member States' gross national income (GNI).
The Commission may call on a maximum amount that the Commission may annually call from a Member State to cover its own resources, which must be at least 1.5 % of each Member State's GNI. The Commission may also borrow from the European Investment Bank (EIB) or from the International Monetary Fund (IMF).
Each year, the Commission must draw up a list of EU countries’ own resources that it can call on. The list is published in the Official Journal.
Each Member State must provide the Commission with a report on how it intends to use the own resources it receives from the EU countries. The report must be sent to the Council every 2 years.
Every 2 years, the Council, the European Parliament and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) assesses the results of the own resource collection. The results of these surveys are then forwarded to the EEC and the Commission, which then forwards the results to the other Member States and the EEA.
In addition, each year the Commission is required to report on the progress it has made in terms of reducing greenhouse gas emissions (GHG emissions) and carbon dioxide emissions (CO2 emissions) by 3,8 kilograms multiplied by the population in 2017.
A carbon border adjustment mechanism and a digital levy must be put in place by 1 January 2023.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
It applies from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to reduce GHG emissions. These include:
a carbon tax;
a digital levy.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union’ website.
* KEY TERMS
* CO2 emissions: greenhouse gases emitted from the burning of fossil fuels.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 71:
Fuel efficiency of heavy-duty vehicle tyres — labelling and information
Fuel efficiency and information of heavy‐duty vehicle tyre manufacturers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/865 on the labelling of tyres with respect to fuel efficiency and other essential parameters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to improve the fuel efficiency* and environmental performance of light-duty vehicles (LVs) and to encourage end-users to purchase tyres with lower external rolling noise by providing them with harmonised information on the tyre’s characteristics.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the requirements for the EU Member States to ensure that tyres with a wet grip index (WG) of at least 0.3 kg/litre are labelled on their vehicles. It does not apply to:
re-treaded tyres;
new tyres; and
new vehicles with fewer than 4 wheels.
The regulations cover:
the wet grip class and measured value of the candidate tyre;
the tyre class and the measured value;
external rolling noise classes and measured values.
EU Member States must ensure that tyre manufacturers, importers and distributors provide the public with the information they need to make informed purchasing decisions.
End-users must:
identify the tyres they wish to buy;
make sure that tyres are labelled in a language easily understood by the public;
assess the tyres’ fuel efficiency;
check that tyres comply with the requirements laid down in the regulation;
ensure that tyres meet the requirements set out of the regulation.
use tyres that meet the criteria set out in Annex II to the regulation and that they comply with Annex III.
Exchange of information
Member States must also exchange information with the European Commission on:
roadworthiness;
environmental performance;
road noise;
traffic noise.
In addition, Member States are required to provide the Commission with:
a list of tyre manufacturers and importers;
a description of the tyres to be sold in the Member States;
information on the tyres available for sale in the European Union (EU).
The Commission must also provide the European Parliament, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions with a list of tyres that comply with this regulation. The Commission may adopt delegated acts to supplement the regulation in the light of scientific and technical progress.
Importers must ensure the following information is available to the public:
inform the Commission of the type of tyre they intend to sell;
instruct the public of the characteristics of the tyre and of the class of tyre to which it applies;
provide the Commission, the Commission and the European Transport Safety Agency with information on tyre characteristics and the tyre class;
with the Commission providing information on tyres that do not comply with Regulation (EC) No 1222/2009 on fuel efficiency.
Consumers must also be informed of the following:
how tyres are classified according to their wet grip characteristics;
how they are rated on the basis of their performance in the test cycle;
where tyres do not conform to the specifications laid down by the Commission;
if a tyre does not conform with the specifications, the manufacturer must inform the end-user of the reason for this.
Motor vehicles must be equipped with a tyre information sheet to enable them to choose the tyre they want to buy. The information sheet must include:
type of tyre, class and measurement value,
wet grip characteristics,
class of tyre and class of motor vehicle, and
the class of vehicle, type of vehicle and type of roadworthiness.
Manufacturers must also inform the Commission on the type and number of vehicles to which tyres are to be fitted, including:
vehicle type;
type and size of vehicle;
number of wheels;
distance to be driven;
duration of driving;
length of driving time;
total number of kilometres driven.
Directive 2014/40/EU on energy efficiency
The directive sets out a number of energy efficiency targets for vehicles, such as:
combating CO2 emissions from motor vehicles;
reducing greenhouse gas emissions from energy-intensive sectors;
increasing energy efficiency from transport;
improving the efficiency of energy-efficient vehicles.
It also sets out rules on the energy efficiency of vehicles and their trailers.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the regulations.
For more information, see:
‘Fuel efficiency’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* External rolling noise: the noise emitted by tyres during road traffic.
* Roadworthiness: the ability of a vehicle to withstand the impact of road traffic on its aerodynamic performance.

Summary 72:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/856 on the implementation of the Customs Control Instrument
Im Implementing Regulation(EU) 2020/955 on the implementing of the customs control instrument
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2019 — implementing the Customs Controls Instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that goods entering and exiting the EU’s external borders are subject to risk-based controls in accordance with the EU Customs Code.
It sets up a European Union instrument to improve the effectiveness of customs controls.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to goods entering the customs territory of the EU. It does not apply to goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries.
Implementation
The instrument is implemented through work programmes, which are funded by the European Union (EU).
The work programmes are designed to:
improve the effectiveness and efficiency of EU customs controls;
increase the efficiency and effectiveness of EU border controls; and
ensure the consistency of customs procedures.
EU funding
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the work programmes. The work programme is funded by EU funding from the EU budget. The total amount of EU funding for each work programme must not exceed the total eligible costs of the action. The amount of the work programme may not exceed €5 million.
The Commission may also make grants available to EU countries for the purpose of:
improving the efficiency of customs control procedures;
ensuring the consistency and effectiveness;
enhancing the quality of customs data;
collecting and analysing data on goods entering or leaving the EU;
providing information on goods.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
It entered into force on 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
  The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve customs controls, including:
the introduction of a risk assessment system for goods entering its external borders;
the creation of a European Customs Information System (ECIS);
the establishment of a customs information system.
For more information, see:
‘Risk-based customs controls’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 73:
Electricity market: energy efficiency and competition
Electricity markets: electricity efficiency and competitiveness
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/943 on electricity market efficiency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the efficiency of the electricity market in the European Union (EU).
It recasts and repeals Directives 94/22/EC, 98/70/EC and 2009/31/EC on electricity markets.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all electricity markets in the EU. It does not apply to:
the transmission system operators (TSOs);
distribution system operators;
electricity producers;
market participants.
It does not cover:
transmission system operators.
EU countries must ensure that:
TSOs are independent of the transmission system operator;
they operate in a non-discriminatory manner;
there is no discrimination on the basis of market share;
the system operators are independent from transmission system users.
There is a system operator’s responsibility for:
operating the electricity transmission system;
providing services to consumers;
transmitting electricity to the grid;
monitoring and analysing the performance of the system operators’ systems;
ensuring the reliability of the grid.
providers of electricity must be able to participate in the market in a fair and transparent manner.
The system operators must:
provide consumers with information on the conditions under which they can switch to other electricity markets;
participate in market participants’ decision-making;
inform consumers of the conditions and conditions for switching, switching, demand response and other services;
operate in a transparent manner; and
operationalise the system operator.
operators must ensure they:
have the necessary financial resources to meet their obligations;
take the necessary measures to ensure the safety of consumers’ electricity;
carry out market surveillance;
have sufficient resources to monitor and analyse the market.
In addition, they must ensure:
they:
monitor and analyse market participants;
cooperate with market participants and market participants to ensure that the market participants are informed of market conditions;
allow consumers to switch to different electricity markets depending on their needs;
make available to consumers the information they need to switch from one market to another.
They must also:
ensure that consumers can switch between electricity markets without any discrimination;
notify the European Commission of any changes in market conditions that could affect their electricity consumption;
promote competition in electricity markets by:
establishing a single market operator for electricity markets, and
setting up a single system operator for transmission systems.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision No 593/2018/EU has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
   
For more information, see:
Energy efficiency (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Transmission system operator: a company responsible for the transmission, distribution and distribution of electricity.
* Market participant: a market participant whose main activity is to provide electricity to consumers.

Summary 74:
Joint Undertaking for the development of advanced supercomputers and quantum computers (BDVA) (until 2020) (European Commission)
Joint Subsidiary for the Development of Advanced Supercomputing and Quantum Computers (DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the establishment of a Joint Undertaking to develop advanced supercomputer and quantum computer systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a joint undertaking to support research and innovation in the fields of data, artificial intelligence and robotics.
It sets up the European Data, AI and Robotics (DAIRO) joint underwriting body. It can pool resources from the EU, Member States and third countries associated to Horizon Europe, the Digital Europe Programme or the Connecting Europe Facility and the private sector.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
research and innovation (R&D) and the creation of high-performance computing and quantum computing (HPC) systems;
connecting Europe facilities (connecting the EU’s external borders, such as the European Maritime and Fisheries Agency (EMSA), the European Space Agency (ESA) and European Research Council (ERC);
the European Partnerships (European Partnerships) with private and/or public sector partners (e.g. the European Innovation and Innovation Fund, the European Social Fund, Horizon Europe and the European Investment Bank for Innovation and Entrepreneurship (EIF).
the creation of a joint supercomputing centre (Joint supercomputer centre).
supercomputers, quantum computers and HPCs are to be used for research, innovation and entrepreneurship.
Funding
The EU funding for the joint under this regulation comes from the Horizon Europe programme and from the European Union (EU).
The European Commission and each participating country provide financial support to the joint undertaking.
The joint undertaking must:
provide financial support for direct actions, i.e. research, development and technological demonstration projects;
contribute to the implementation of EU projects and initiatives in the areas covered by Horizon Europe;
make available to the public and private sectors the information and communication technologies (ICTs) they need;
ensure that projects are implemented in a cost-effective way, and that the results are shared with the public, private sector and other interested parties.
EU funding
To achieve these objectives, the EU contributes to the development and implementation of HPC systems by:
raising awareness of the potential of the HPC sector;
providing technical support for research and development projects in this field;
encouraging the use of the high-performing HPC system to develop innovative products and services;
supporting the development, testing and deployment of high performance computing and QPC systems in the field of innovation;
developing and deploying HPC-related technologies in the area of entrepreneurship and innovation.
Dates of entry into force
The Joint Undergoing entered into force on 1 January 2019. It will continue to operate until 31 December 2020.

Summary 75:
Climate change: EU 2020-2040 climate action — EU 2020–2040 targets and objectives
Climate change — EU 2040–202040 EU climate action – EU2020–2030 objectives
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/2040 on the EU’s climate action
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU meets its climate action objectives by 2020.
It sets out the objectives and measures to achieve these objectives.
KEY POINTS
The objectives of the regulation are to:
contribute to reducing greenhouse gas emissions (GHG emissions) in line with the Paris Agreement (the ‘Paris Agreement’);
reduce the risk of climate change to 2°C above pre-industrial levels by 2020;
ensure that the economy and society adapt to climate change.
The objective is to reduce GHG emissions to 2 °C by 2020 compared to 1990 levels.
To achieve this objective, the EU must:
redouble its efforts to reduce its GHG emission levels to 1.5 % below 1990 levels;
increase the efficiency of its energy system;
improve the resilience and adaptability of the economy against climate change;
take the necessary measures to protect the environment and human health and the economy from the effects of climate risks.
EU 2020–2020 climate action targets
The EU‘s 2020–40 climate targets are set out in the regulation’. These include:
the EU 2020 greenhouse gas emission reduction target of 2 % compared with 1990 levels and the EU 2030 greenhouse gas reduction target;
carbon dioxide (CO2) reduction target (carbon dioxide) of 0.8 % compared to 2010 levels; and
the goal of a 2 % reduction in global warming by 2050 compared to 2005 levels. These targets are based on an indicative EU greenhouse gas budget, which must be revised every 6 years. The Commission must also publish the methodology underlying the projected indicative EU GHG budget.
In addition, the regulation sets out:
a climate-neutrality objective for the EU by 2040;
the objective to achieve the EU's climate neutrality by 2050;
a long-term goal to reduce the EU emissions of GHG by 20 % from 1990 levels compared to 2030 levels. The objective is based on the best available scientific knowledge and on the basis of the best possible scientific knowledge.
Climate-neutralisation objective
The Commission must set out a climate neutralisation objective by 2050. This objective is achieved by:
increasing energy efficiency;
reducing emissions of CO2 from all sources of energy;
improving the efficiency and effectiveness of the energy sector;
developing a climate-resilient, adaptive and resilient economy.
Coherence and consistency of EU policies
EU countries must ensure that their national energy and climate plans are aligned with the objectives set out by the regulation. They must also ensure that they are coherent and consistent in all relevant legislation and policies, and that they provide the necessary information to the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of the objectives. This information must be made available to the public and to the private sector.
National authorities must ensure the transparency of their national policies and measures and that the public can access them.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2022.
BACKGROUND
The European Union is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle climate change and to delivering on the agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change.
For more information, see:
‘Climate action’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 78:
European Institute of Innovation and Technology (EIT) — strategy for the period from 2021 to 2027 (SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2021/695 establishing the European Institute of Innovation and Technology
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE STRATEGIC INNOVATION AGENDA?
The decision sets out the priority fields and the strategy of the European institute of innovation and technology for the seven-year period 2021-2027.
The strategy establishes the EIT’s key actions and its outreach activities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EIT is responsible for:
research and innovation;
exchange of best practices and best practices;
promotion and dissemination of best practice;
international cooperation and global outreach activities;
development of innovative and entrepreneurial skills of MSCA fellows and ERC grantees at all stages of their career.
Objectives

promote European innovation and technological development;
contribute to the development of European creativity, cultural and linguistic diversity, European identity and cohesion, European employment, economic resilience and smart growth;
ensure the sustainable development of Europe as a global actor.
To achieve these objectives, the strategy must take account of the following:

In addition, it must:

.

creativity, cultural heritage, design and design;
environmental aspects (e.g. water, energy, transport, agriculture, forestry, water management, etc.).
Cultural and creative sectors

to promote European creativity and cultural heritage;
to contribute to the creation of a more dynamic, dynamic and dynamic European economy.

exchanges of experience and best practice, including:
participation of the public and private sectors in the activities carried out by the institute;
visits and exchanges of scientists, engineers, technicians and other experts;
cooperation with international organisations, such as universities, universities and the World Economic Forum (WEF);
international exchanges of information, including on:
science, technology and innovation policy;
research, innovation and development (R & D);
research on the environment and climate change;
investment in sustainable development.
Monitoring and evaluation
The report and monitoring of the KICs’ operational performance, including their administrative expenditure and their results, will be carried out in close cooperation with Horizon Europe common corporate services within the Commission.

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011 — definitions, descriptions, presentation and protection on the classification, presentation*, labelling, geographical indications and marketing of certain aromatic-wine products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU), as a whole, does not lose out on market share by ensuring that products marketed under the name ‘Aromatised Wine’ or ‘Oromatisation’ are protected in the EU.
It sets out the rules applicable to the definition and description of these products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
products marketed under a geographical indication (GIs);
appliances;
wine-based drinks;
influents;
food-based products;
sugars;
alcohol-based beverages.
Protection
Products covered by the regulation must comply with the following conditions:
the product must be produced in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation’s Annex I;
the products must be labelled in a language easily understood by the general public;
they must be accompanied by a description of the production processes and methods of analysis;
there must be a link between the geographical indication and the final product.
the information to be provided in the product specification must be provided by the manufacturer or importer between the product and the geographical area of origin.
The European Commission may adopt implementing acts to ensure the protection of products covered by this regulation.
Labelling
The Commission must ensure that:
a product is labelled according to its geographical indication;
it is accompanied by an indication of the place of origin of origin;
a description of its production process and methods;
an indication of its sugar content;
invert sugar is expressed as invert sugar.
If the Commission considers that a product does not meet the conditions set out in Annex II, it may decide to exclude it from protection. The Commission may, by means of implementing acts, adopt all necessary measures related to this decision, including checks and verification to be carried out by the Member States, including the submission of trans-border applications, including testing.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Directive 2011/13/EU applies from 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Protected wines’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 81:
EU’s macro-financial assistance to Ukraine (2018-2022)
EU‘s macro‐financial assistance To Ukraine (2010-2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/2022 on the European Union's macro‑financial assistance for Ukraine
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a financial assistance programme for Ukraine.
It aims to strengthen the economic resilience of Ukraine, supplementing resources made available under the International Monetary Fund (IMF) programme.
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the terms and conditions for the assistance.
The assistance will be provided in the form of loans. The loans will have a maximum average maturity of 15 years. They will be repaid by the European Commission, which will provide the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Agricultural Fund (EAF) with the relevant documents in due time.
In order to ensure uniform conditions, implementing powers are conferred on the Commission. These include the power to carry out checks, for the Court of Auditors and for the European Public Prosecutor's Office to exercise their competences. The Commission will regularly monitor the progress in attaining the objectives of the assistance and report to the European Parliament and the Council of the EU on the results and efficiency achieved.
EU financial assistance
The financial assistance will cover the period from 1 January 2018 to 31 December 2020. The first instalment of the loan was disbursed to Ukraine for an amount of €2.8 billion. The release of the second instaliment will not, in principle, take place earlier than three months after the release.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
From 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been providing financial assistance to the Ukraine since 2014.

‘Ukraine’ on the website of the European External Action Service (EEAS).

Fishing of swordfish in the Mediterranean: EU rules for the conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT Convention)
Fisheries of the Mediterranean
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2018 on the EU’s common fisheries policy for swordfish
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU rules on the conservation and management of the swordfish caught by European Union (EU) fishing vessels.
It aims to ensure that swordfish are not caught illegally.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels registered in the EU. It does not apply to:
fishing vessels registered outside the EU;
ships registered in a non-EU country;
seaworthiness vessels;
any other fishing vessel registered in another EU country.
EU countries’ annual fishing plans
Each EU country must submit an annual fishing plan to the European Commission by 30 April, 30 July and 30 October of each year and by 30 January of the following year. The Commission then forwards this information to the ICCAT Secretariat, which then forwards it to the EU countries.
Each year, EU countries must submit a report to the Commission on the implementation of the regulation. This report must include:
the number of vessels registered and the number of catches;
the total number of fish caught;
a description of the fishing gear used;
details of the number and type of catches.

Summary 85:
Convention on the conservation and management of Straddling Fish Stocks and Highly Migratory Fish (CMSF) (OJ L 286, 29.10.2008, pp. 1-2)
Convention of the United Nations Convention on the Conservation and Management of Stradling Fish (CSF)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2016/852 on the Convention relating to the Conservation, Management and Restriction of Fishing in the Ogean Ocean
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE CONVENTION?
The decision aims to ensure the conservation, management and restriction of fishing in the overlap area of the International Treaty on the Law of the Sea (ICTY).
The ICTY covers the part of the Pacific Ocean bounded by the following lines: the 50°S parallel from its intersection with meridian 150°W until the intersection of meridian 130°W, and the 4°W parallel from the intersection with Meridian 150.
The convention aims to:
promote the sustainable use of the marine environment;
ensure the sustainable management of highly migratory fish stocks;
improve the protection of marine ecosystems.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels flying the flag of the European Union (EU).
Purse seine vessels flying EU flag are prohibited from fishing in:
the overlap area;
the 4°S area; and
the 50°W area.
EU countries must notify the International Association for the Conservation of Tropical Tunas (IATTC) and the World Conservation Council (WCPFC) prior to fishing in these areas. The IATTC Secretariat forwards the information to the Commission by 30 June.
Before fishing in this area, flag EU countries must inform the WCPFC and the ICTC under which of the two conservation and measures adopted by those organisations the vessels flying their flag shall operate when fishing in that overlap area. The Commission then forwards that information to its Secretariat, which forwards it to the International Commission for the Protection of Marine Living Resources (ICPHR).
If a country considers that a vessel flying its flag is fishing in an area covered by the ICTY, it must suspend its fishing activities in that area until the end of the closure period for that fishing activity.
In the event that a whale shark is deliberately encircled in the purse seine net, the master of the vessel must ensure that all reasonable steps are taken to ensure its safe release; and report the incident to the EU country, including the number of individuals concerned, details of how and why the encirclement happened, where it occurred, and an assessment of the life status of the animal(s) on release (including whether any were released alive but subsequently died). The vessel’s refrigeration equipment in the sealed well must be rendered inoperative.
If silky sharks are unintentionally caught and frozen as part of a purse-seine vessel operation, and if the governmental authorities are present at the point of landing, the whole silky shark must be surrendered to them.
Fishing on data buoys
Data buoys must be used only for the purpose of collecting, analysing and disseminating information on marine life. They must not be used for commercial purposes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 30 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
  The ICTY is the most important fishery in the world and is the subject of a number of international agreements.
For more information, see:
Straddling fish stocks (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Ogeanic: a group of fish that migrate between two or more countries.
* Highly migratory: fish that are migratory from one country to another.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Decision (EU, Euratom) No 852/2016 of 30 May 2016 on the convention relating to conservation, Management, Restriction on Fishing (Ogilean Ocean).
Successive amendments to Council Decision 2008/572/EU have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 86:
Common Understanding between the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission on Delegated Acts (COUNCIL OF LAWMAIN DOCUMENT
Common Understanding
SUMMARY OF:
Interinstitutional Agreement on Better Law-Making
Decision (EU) 2016/852 on the common understanding between the EU institutions on delegated acts
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement establishes a common framework for the adoption of delegated acts by the European Commission.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Delegated acts
The delegated acts are acts adopted by the Commission in order to improve the efficiency of the EU’s internal market. They aim to:
improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the internal market;
ensure that EU countries’ laws and regulations comply with the principles of subsidiarity and proportionality, as well as the fundamental rights of citizens;
promote competition and innovation;
encourage the exchange of information and best practice in the field of law-making.
Scope and general principles
The European Commission adopts delegated acts on the basis of a common approach to EU law. It consults experts designated by each EU country on the content and scope of the delegated acts. The Commission then adopts the delegated act on its own initiative.
Duties of the Commission
The Commission:
is empowered to adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 1 of the agreement;
is responsible for the implementation of the acts adopted under this agreement.
the Commission: must:
adopt delegated acts with the consent of all EU countries;
adopts the acts in line with the general principles of EU law;
prepares an impact assessment for each delegated act;
assesses the impact of a delegated act and its consequences on the economy and society;
has the power to amend delegated acts if it considers that they do not meet the objectives of the common approach.
It must consult EU countries and the European Court of Auditors on the results of the impact assessment. It must also consult the European Judicial Committee on the Law-making of EU countries.
In addition, the Commission must consult the Council of Europe, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions.
EU countries:
have the right to decide whether the Commission should adopt delegated act.
If the Commission decides to adopt a delegated acts, it must consult experts designated in each EU Member State.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Decisions (EU and EU countries) have applied since 13 April 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Better law making’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Delegation act: an act adopted on the initiative of a Member State for the purpose of improving the efficiency or effectiveness of EU legislation.
* Impact assessment: an assessment of the effect of a proposed act on the economic, social or cultural life of an EU country.
ACT
Council Decision (EC) No 852/2016 of 13.4.2016 on the Common Understanding between European Parliament and Council of 13 March 2016 on the European Council of 21 March 2016 establishing a common understanding on delegated Acts (OJ L 136, 13.3.2016, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
 
  The European Commission adopted Decision (EU, EU) 2016-852 of 13 April 2015 on the adoption, on behalf the European Community, of a Common Understanding on Delegate Acts (EC, EC, EC and EU) (Official Journal C 202, 14.5.2016)

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2011 on the procedure for citizens’ initiatives for implementing the Common European Union (COP)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the registration and submission of statements of support to the competent authorities of EU countries’ competent authorities.
It aims to improve the transparency and effectiveness of the European Union's (EU) internal market for goods, services and capital.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
The rules on the registration of a proposed citizens' initiative are laid down in Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1082/2013.
Organisers must register with the competent authority of each EU country. They must provide a statement of support form with the following information:
the name and address of the organisers;
the number of signatories;
a description of the initiative and the reasons for its creation;
details of the organisation and the number of signatures.
EU countries must verify that the organisers have obtained the necessary personal data for the purpose of verifying the statements.
They must also ensure that the information they provide is accurate and that they are not using personal data to commit fraud.
they must inform the European Commission of any misuse of the personal data they have received.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that their competent authorities have the necessary information to carry out the verification of the statements they receive.

It has applied since 1 January 2011.

Summary 88:
Pesticides: EU statistics
Pesticide statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the placing on the market and use of pesticides
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a common framework for the systematic production of European Union (EU) statistics on pesticides which are plant protection products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all pesticides on the EU market. It covers:
plant protection products* intended for use on agricultural crops;
pesticides intended to be used on non-agricultural crops, such as fruit trees, vegetables and fruit trees;
products intended for the production, storage, transport, storage and disposal of agricultural products intended for agricultural use.
It does not apply to:
products designed for use in the food chain;
chemical pesticides intended for industrial use;
organic pesticides intended to protect crops against pests.
EU statistics
EU countries must submit a report to the European Commission every 3 years on the statistics they have collected. The first report must be submitted by 31 December 2016.
The Commission must publish the results of the first report within 3 years of the date of entry into force of the regulation. It must also publish a report every 5 years after the end of the 3-year reporting period.
Sectors covered by the regulation
The report must include information on:
the number of pesticides used;
the quantities of substances used,
the types of pesticides placed on the European market and the number of products placed on it;
in the EU countries’ national lists of pesticides on sale.
Use of pesticides in agriculture
The EU countries must ensure that pesticides are used only in accordance with the EU’s rules on the use of chemicals in agriculture, i.e. that they are not applied to crops other than those covered by EU rules.
In addition, the report must also cover:
information on the types and quantities of chemicals used in agriculture;
information about the types of products on sale and the quantities of pesticides that are placed on them;
measures to reduce the risk of adverse reactions to pesticides;
monitoring of pesticide residues;
data on pesticide residues in food and feed;
reports on the number and quantity of pesticides sold and used in the EU.

Summary 89:
Plant protection products — harmonised EU rules on active substances, safeners, synergists, co-formulants and adjuvants (Plant Protection Regulation (PPR) — harmonisation of the EU’s rules on plant protection products (Plants Protection Regulation)
Plant production has a very important place in the European Union (EU). This regulation aims to ensure that EU countries’ laws and regulations are compatible with the principles of plant protection.
ACT
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 14 June 2008 on harmonised rules on the approval of active substances*, active substances**, safeners*, synergists*, co-forms* and adjuants*.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It harmonises EU rules for the approval, recognition and use of substances* intended for plant protection*.
It sets out the requirements and conditions for the granting of EU-wide authorisations to active substances*.
The regulation applies to the following substances:
active substances*;
safeners* and synergists**.
KEY POINTS
Active substances
EU countries must ensure that:
the substances covered by the regulation meet the following criteria:
they are of value for the protection of plant health and the environment;
they do not endanger human health or the environment.
The European Commission must assess the application for approval of an active substance and decide on its application within 6 months of receiving it.
If an application concerns the same plant protection product and the same uses as contained in the dossier, the EU country must decide on the application at the latest 6 months after receiving the dossier.
An EU countries may decide on an application for the same active substance if:
it concerns a different use;
it is not of value to the EU as a whole;
the economic interest of the applicant is limited.
Where an EU country decides that an application does not meet the criteria for the active substance, it must decide whether to grant the application.
In this case, the Commission may adopt a harmonised procedure for the assessment of the differences between EU countries' national rules.
Safeners
The Commission may decide to adopt harmonised methods for assessing the safety and environmental performance of safeners.
Co-formulas
If the Commission considers that a synergist is of value only to the plant protection market, it may decide that it does not constitute a safety or environmental risk.
Advertising
The EU country which receives the application must ensure the safety of the product and ensure that its advertising is technically justifiable. It must also ensure that the information it provides on the safety, environmental and health aspects of the products it approves is accurate.
Manufacturers and importers
The manufacturer of the active substances and the importer of the safety or the environmental components must provide the European Commission with a statement of their intention to use the product. They must also provide the Commission with an opportunity to submit comments.
Labelling of products
The product must be labelled in a language easily understood by end-users.
References
Act

Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Summary 90:
Implementing Regulation (EU) No 596/2013 on the establishment of the Interim Management Instrument (IMI)
Introducing Interim Monitoring Instrument (IME)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 550/2013 — the establishment and functioning of the International Monitoring Instrument
WHAT IS THE AIMING OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the International Management Instrument, an electronic tool provided by the European Commission to facilitate administrative cooperation between EU countries’ competent authorities and between competent authorities of the EU countries and the Commission.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
It does not cover:
data protection;
data security;
privacy;
information exchange with third countries;
access to data.
Implementation of the regulation
EU countries must:
ensure that the IMI is used only by the competent authorities;
provide IMI users with access to their own data processing activities performed by an IMI user under their authority;
make sure that data subjects can exercise their rights in accordance with IMI’s rules on data security.
Each EU country must ensure that IMI complies with the data security rules adopted by the Commission pursuant to Article 22 of Directive 2001/45/2001 on the protection of personal data. It must also ensure that data subject rights are respected.
The IMI must be used only for the purpose of:
the processing of data;
the protection and management of data
the exchange of information
data
the management of information.
Information to data subjects and transparency
information on the rights of data subjects.
IMI users must be provided with:
a description of their rights and responsibilities;
an overview of their data processing;
a list of the data they have access to and the rights they have to exercise.
Annexes to the regulation set out the responsibilities of IMI coordinators. These coordinators are responsible for:
coordinating the IMIs;
ensuring that the information they provide is accurate and up-to-date;
assisting the Commission in implementing IMI;
monitoring the functioning of IMaintaining the confidentiality and integrity of data. They must ensure the confidentiality of data and ensure that the data subjects’ rights are exercised.
They must also:
assist the Commission with the implementation of the rules of procedure for data protection and data security, as well as with the management of the information it receives;
take the necessary measures to ensure the security of the personal data they collect;
cooperate with other EU countries in the field of data protection.
In addition, they must: ensure that:
relevant information is made available to the relevant authorities of EU countries, and to the Commission;
implement IMI in line with the requirements of the European Data Protection Regulation (EDR);
monitor the security and privacy of data, and ensure the data are protected;
encourage the exchange of experience and best practice on data protection between the Commission and the relevant national authorities.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Interim management instrument: a computerised electronic tool designed to facilitate the administrative coordination between the European Union and its Member States.
* Data processing: the collection, processing, storage and transmission of data on the basis of a standardised set of rules and procedures.

GFCM Agreement on the sustainable management of the Mediterranean and Black Sea fisheries
Guided fisheries management — Mediterranean and the Black Sea
Guidance for the implementation of the European Union’s GFCM agreement on sustainable fishing in the Mediterranean & Black Sea (2011-2020)

The decision concludes the agreement with Bulgaria, Greece, Spain, France, Italy, Cyprus, Malta, Romania and Slovenia.

fishing gear

Fishing gear:
minimum mesh size

The Commission may adopt implementing acts as regards detailed rules for the format and transmission of such reports.
Reporting of statistical matrices
The European Commission must submit a report to the Council every 2 years on the results of the annual reporting of statistics on the use of fishing gear in the marine environment.

Summary 92:
Uniform patent protection within the EU
Universal patent protection: European patent with unitary effect
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the creation of a European Patent with Unitary Effect
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that patents granted by the European Patent Office (EPO) have the same legal effect throughout the EU.
It sets out the conditions for granting a European patent.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any patent granted in the EU by the EPO. It does not apply to patents granted in non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that the patent has the same effect in all EU countries, irrespective of the country in which it was granted.
The EPO decides whether a patent has unitary or non-unitary effect in each EU country on the basis of a request from the patent proprietor. This decision is binding on the EU countries and on the European Commission.
Eligible EU countries
A patent granted by an EU country to a third party in another EU country may benefit from unitary patent protection if it is:
the result of a joint application for patent protection by the EU country granting the patent;
the product or service covered by the patent is identical to a patent granted to the third party; or
the third party’s invention is similar to that of the first party.
Exemptions
The European patent does not conferring patent protection on third parties does not prevent third parties from committing acts against which the patent provides protection. However, it does confer on the proprietor the right to prevent any third party from committing the acts against the patent. This right may be exercised in the case of infringement of the patent, provided that the infringements do not result in a loss of revenue for the patent holder.
In addition, the European patent may confer on its proprietor:
a right to prohibit the infringement of third parties’ acts;
an obligation to compensate third parties for any loss of income resulting from infringement of their rights;
a duty to inform third parties of any infringements of the rights conferred on it.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCELEABLE FORCE
 
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Unitary patent: a patent that is granted to a person or company for a specific purpose.
* Non-uniform patent: any patent that applies to a product, service or service that is identical or similar to another patent granted for the same purpose by the same person or service in the same EU country.

SUMMARY OF:

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

Summary 94:
Exercise of the EU’s rights under international trade agreements
Exemption of the European Union's rights in the area of trade in services
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the exercise of the Union's trade-related rights under International Trade Agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU is able to exercise its rights under multilateral trade agreements to safeguard its economic interests.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
trade in services:
the import, export and transit of goods and services between the EU and non-EU countries;
the supply of services to the EU;
services provided by the private sector to the public sector.
Exercise
The European Commission will review the scope of Article 5 of the regulation with a view to covering additional commercial policy measures in the field of services as soon as the conditions for ensuring the workability and effectiveness of such measures are present. The Commission will promptly transmit to the Parliament and to the Council draft implementing acts that it submits to the committee of EU countries. The committee will take the input received into account.
The Commission will then submit these implementing acts to the European Parliament and the Council for adoption by the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC).
Exemptions from the regulation
The EU may suspend or withdraw certain concessions established in the tariff schedules of WTO Member States. The suspension of these concessions must be decided in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 8(2) of the Regulation (GATT). The suspension must be notified to the Committee of Member States, which will decide on the suspension of the concessions.
Implementing acts
In the case of the suspension or withdrawal of a concession, the Commission must notify the committee, which is composed of representatives of the Member States and chaired by the Commission, of the measures it intends to take. It will then transmit the draft implementing act to the Commission for adoption.

Summary 95:
Agreement on the stabilisation of the EU market for bananas
Agreement establishing a stabilisation mechanism
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the conclusion of the Agreement establishing a stability mechanism for bananas (Bananas Agreement)
Decision (EU) 2017/852 — conclusion, on behalf of the European Union, of the agreement establishing a stable mechanism for banana imports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE AGREEMENT?
The regulation establishes a stabilising mechanism for the import of bananas into the EU from non-EU countries.
The agreement establishes a mechanism to ensure that the EU’s imports of bananas do not adversely affect the situation of the market for the agricultural products covered by this regulation.
It sets out the conditions under which the Commission may adopt definitive safeguard measures.
KEY POINTS
Stabilisation measures
The Commission must take into account:
the impact of the imports on the situation in the EU for bananas;
the level of imports and the impact of those measures on the market.
Imposition of safeguard measures
Implementing measures must be based on the following:
a risk assessment of the import and export situation;
an analysis of the potential impact on the supply and demand for bananas and on the competitiveness of the sector;
a review of the conditions for the implementation of the measures; and
an evaluation of the risks to the supply of bananas.
If the Commission considers that the import or export situation does not meet the conditions laid down in the annex to the regulation, it must take the necessary measures to prevent or reduce the risk of a significant increase in imports.
In the case of an increase in the import, the Commission must notify all interested parties of any such extension and explain the reasons therefor. The Commission must also notify the Association Committee for examination of the case.
Where the Commission does not consider that the facts as finally established show that the conditions set out in Article 2(1) are met, it may refer the matter to the Committee for Examination in accordance with Article 116 of the Agreements.
Actions taken by the Commission
If an import is likely to have an adverse effect on the production or export of bananas, the European Commission must:
take measures to limit or eliminate the risk to the production and export of the products concerned;
implement definitive safeguard* measures;
inform the Committee of all the relevant parties, including the Commission, of any measures taken to prevent, reduce or eliminate imports of the goods concerned.
Information sharing
The EU must ensure that:
all information which is relevant and is not confidential within the meaning of Article 12 of the Regulation is disseminated;
information which is confidential is kept in a non-confidential file, which it must manage and through which all information which it considers to be relevant and not confidential shall be disseminated.
This information must be made available to the Commission as soon as the necessary technical framework is in place, ensuring that it can manage and disseminate all information.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the agreement.
For more information, see:
Bananas and the EU (European Commission).

Summary 97:
EU border surveillance: operational rules for border surveillance (EU-wide border surveillance)
EU - border surveillance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 515/2014 on the implementation of the European Union’s external border surveillance system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the operation of the EU-wide network of border surveillance units.
It aims to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ external borders. It does not apply to the Schengen area.
Participating units
Each participating unit must have at least one person with basic first aid training.
Each unit must be equipped with:
a computerised surveillance system;
an operational plan;
basic first-aid training;
a radio frequency identification (RFID) system.
The operational plan must contain the contact details of those competent national authorities, which must take appropriate follow-up measures.
Coordination
The European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCA) is responsible for coordinating the activities of the participating units. It is assisted by the International Coordination Centre (ICC) and the International Maritime Organization (IMO).
Cooperation
The participating units must:
cooperate with each other and with the IFCO;
share information on the situation of persons on board vessels;
collect and report information about vessel transiting the contiguous zone;
report information about the situation on board to the IMC;
assist in the exchange of information between the ICCA and the IBCA;
coordinate with the Interpol and the United Nations High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (UNHCR).
The IFCA coordinates with the International Police and Border Control Centre (UNICEF) and with other international organisations, such as the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY).
In addition, each participating unit:
is responsible for:
monitoring the movement of persons and goods across the external borders;
carrying out border surveillance operations;
ensuring the protection of the lives and safety of persons crossing the border;
providing information on persons in urgent need of medical assistance;
taking other measures to ensure the safety of the people on board the vessel;
reporting information to the international coordination centre and the UNICEF.
In the event of a vessel being detained or seized, the competent national authority must inform the participating unit.
If a vessel is transiting a contiguous zone, it must report information on that vessel to the ICTY, including:
the nationality of the person on board;
the identity of the vessel and the nationality of its crew;
any imminent risk to their lives or whether there is an urgent need for medical assistance.
Where a stateless vessel crosses the border, the relevant national authorities must immediately inform the ITCA of the country in which the vessel transits.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 18 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a border surveillance strategy to prevent and combat illegal border crossings.
For more information, see:
‘Border surveillance’ on the European Commission's website.

EU resettlement priorities

the resettlement of people in urgent need;
the relocation of persons who are at risk of becoming a threat to public security or public order.
2.
In addition, the EU countries are required to take into account:
a number of refugees who are likely to be displaced by natural or legal means of escape; and
the number of people likely to become a danger to public order or public security.
3.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLYOUR ENTRY INTO FORCE?

—
