Summary 100:
The European Union Fund for the Protection of People and Goods from Transnational Threats (EUFPS)
The EUFPS
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 establishing the European Union fund for the protection of people and goods from transnational threats
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the ‘European Union Fund’, a multiannual financial instrument designed to support transnational actions and actions of particular interest to the EU.
It aims to protect the EU’s citizens and goods against increasingly transnational risks and to support the work carried out by Member States’ competent authorities.
KEY POINTS
The European Commission has adopted the regulation on the basis of a proposal from the European Parliament and the Council.
The purpose of the instrument is to:
provide financial support to actions and initiatives of the Member States that are transnational in nature (‘transnational actions’) concerning the general, specific and operational objectives of the Union;
support the work of the competent authorities of Member States in the field of security and border management;
contribute to the prevention, control and eradication of transnational crime and terrorism.
Eligible actions
The regulation sets out:
the objectives, eligible actions and strategic priorities for financial support under the Instrument;
the general framework for the implementation of the eligible actions; and
the resources made available under the instrument from 1 January 2014 to 31 December 2020 and their distribution.
Funding
The instrument is funded by the EU budget, which is divided into 3 categories:
EU actions for transnational action:
‘EU actions,’;
’EU actions of special interest to EU citizens’ and goods.
EU countries’ actions:
national actions for which the Commission has identified as being transnational;
actions for which EU countries have identified as having special interest, but which are not transnational.
Awards
The Commission decides on the number of actions that are eligible for funding under the EU Fund and the amount of resources to be made available to them. The amount of the funding is calculated according to the total number of EU citizens, goods and services that are covered by the Fund.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Instrument entered into force on 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
The ‘EU Fund for protection of persons and goods’ is one of three instruments established by the European Commission. The other two instruments are the European Instrument for Democracy and Human Rights (EIDH) and the Instrument contributing to Stability and Peace.
For more information, see:
European Union funds (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
Transnational actions: actions or actions of which the Union is a party.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2014 of 25 November 2014 establishing the “European Union fund” (OJ L 31, 31.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
   
 
Commission proposal for a proposal to establish a European Union financial instrument (EU-2020/2020) (COM(2020) 697 final, 30.3.2020)
Proposal for a Regulation establishing a new European Union Financial Instrument (EU 2020/2020).
last update 14.02.2021



Summary 101:
Cooperation between the EU and non-EU countries on sustainable fishing
Cooperation among the EU’s fisheries management organisations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on cooperation between the European Union and non–EU countries in the field of fisheries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for cooperation between EU countries to tackle non-sustainable fishing*.
It aims to improve the sustainable management of fisheries in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the fishing of fish stocks belonging to the European Community (now the EU).
It does not apply to:
the fishing of non-target species (e.g. sharks, porpoises, skates, swordfish, porbeagle sharks, sea turtles, sea scallops);
the fishery management of stocks of fish belonging to non–target species;
the management of the fishery of the fisheries of countries which have no competence for the stock in question.
It applies to fishing in the waters of the EU, including:
those of the Baltic Sea and the Black Sea;
those waters which are part of the exclusive economic zone (EEZ);
those which are not part of an EEZ.
Rules and general principles for implementing measures
EU countries must ensure that measures adopted under this regulation are based on objective criteria and are equitable, cost-effective and compatible with international law, in particular with the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organisation for Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (WTO).
. They must also establish general conditions for the adoption of such measures.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that measures are proportionate to the impact of the measures adopted;
establish general conditions to ensure that the measures are not disproportionate to their effect;
take the necessary measures to protect the marine environment and the environment;
notify the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of any measures taken in this field.
The Commission may adopt implementing measures to prevent, restrict or restrict fishing in certain areas of the fishing sector. It must inform the EU countries and the EU Parliament and Council of the reasons for its decision-making.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 of 1 July 2019 on cooperation within the framework of Regional Fisheries Management Organisations (RFMOs) (OJ L 136, 1.7.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2018/952 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements with non-EU countries
Bilateral investments with third countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the conclusion and entry into force of bilateral investment agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the conditions under which EU countries are empowered to amend or conclude bilateral investments agreements with third country after 9 January 2013.
It aims to ensure that these agreements are compatible with the EU’s common commercial policy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to bilateral investments concluded between 1 December 2009 and the end of 2013. It does not apply to agreements concluded between EU countries between 1 January 2013 and 9 January 2014.
The purpose of the regulation is to:
ensure consistency with EU investment policy and compatibility with EU law;
provide a legal basis for EU countries’ decisions on the signing and conclusion of such agreements;
set out the conditions for authorising EU countries to conclude such agreements.
Notification to the Commission
EU countries must notify the European Commission within 90 days of receiving a request for an authorisation to conclude a bilateral investment agreement with a third country. The notification must include a copy of those agreements. The Commission may request further information from the EU country concerned.
If the Commission decides to authorise an EU country to conclude an investment agreement, it must consult the country concerned on the basis of clearly defined criteria. It may also require the country to include or remove from such negotiations and prospective agreements any clauses where necessary to ensure consistency with the Union investment policy or compatibility with Union law.
Where the EU law is incompatible with that EU law, the Commission may require the Member State concerned to include the clause where necessary in the agreement.
In the event that the Commission considers that such clauses are incompatible, it may decide to withdraw the authorisation. The Member State must inform the Commission of any subsequent changes to the status of the agreement and notify it of the reasons for the decision.
Advisory procedure
The Commission may adopt an advisory procedure for the adoption of authorisations pursuant to Articles 9, 11 and 12 of this regulation. The advisory procedure must take no longer than 90 days. It must also include a review of the need for the continued application of that Chapter.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 9 January 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 9 January 2015.

Summary 103:
EU common commercial policy: temporary suspension of customs duties on certain products originating in an EBA beneficiary country (2012-2020)
EU common Commercial Policy: Temporary suspension of certain goods originating in a beneficiary country
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated acts on the suspension of Customs Tariff duties
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DELEGATED ACTS?
The delegated acts suspending customs duties are part of the European Union’s (EU) Common Commercial Policy (CCP) which aims to promote trade in goods between the EU and the European Economic Area (EEA).
KEY POINTS
The ECP suspends certain customs duties originating in beneficiary countries.
The temporary suspension applies to the following products:
goods originating in the EU from non-EBA beneficiary countries, except those originating in countries benefiting from the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) or the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFF) and originating in non-EEA beneficiary countries;
services originating in EBA beneficiaries countries, but originating in EU beneficiary countries (e.g. agricultural products);
services imported into the EU by non-EU beneficiary countries from countries that benefit from the EBF or the EMFF.
In addition, the EPCP suspends the import duties on the following goods originating from beneficiary countries:
foodstuffs originating from outside the EBA;
equipment originating from an EBF beneficiary country;
electrical equipment originating from a non-EMF beneficiary country.
Exemptions from the list of products originating from EBF beneficiaries.
Imports originating from the following beneficiary countries are excluded from the scope of the temporary suspension:
telecommunications equipment, including mobile phones, tablets, televisions, computers and mobile phones;
telecom equipment, peripherals and peripherals, including satellite television and satellite radio equipment;
vehicles, including motor vehicles, mobile phones and satellite televisions;
transport equipment, such as aircraft, boats, aircraft, helicopters, helicopters and fixed-wing aircraft;
medical devices, including medical devices and medical devices;
tourism equipment and services originating from EU beneficiaries;
food and agricultural products originating outside the EU;
other agricultural products.
Temporary suspension of import duties:
the suspension of the import duty on all products that are listed in Chapters 1 to 97 of the Combined Nomenclature;
suspension of customs duty on the import of certain agricultural products, except for those in Chapter 93;
temporary withdrawal of customs tariff duties on all imports originating in beneficiaries’ countries. The period of temporary withdrawal may not exceed 6 months.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Delegated Acts entered into force on 20 November 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the Common Commercial policy (CCP).
The CCP is based on the principles and objectives set out in Article 21 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU).
For more information, see:
Common commercial policy (European Commission).

Summary 104:
EU action to combat the illicit manufacture and trafficking of firearms, their parts and components and ammunition (UN Firearms Protocol)
EU action against the illicit manufacturing and trafficking in firearms, its parts, components, ammunition and ammunition
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2008 on the prevention and control of the illicit production, import, export and transit of firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent, combat and eradicate the illegal manufacturing, import and export of firearms.
It sets out rules on the authorisation, procedures and controls of firearms and their parts.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the manufacture, importation, export, transit and temporary exports of firearms;
the import and transit measures for lawful purposes;
parts and components intended for use in the manufacture, export or import of firearms or their parts, their components or ammunition.
Authorisation and controls
EU countries must:
ensure that firearms are not imported into the EU without authorisation;
ban the export of arms and ammunition to non-EU countries;
implement measures to prevent and combat the illegal manufacture and import of these items;
monitor and control the movements of these goods.
Importers and importers must ensure that they comply with the export control measures.
Exports and imports must be authorised and controlled in accordance with the law of the EU country of destination.
The export controls must be effective, proportionate, dissuasive and proportionate and dissuasive. They must be proportionate to the legitimate needs of the intended recipient.
Member States must take the necessary measures to ensure that these measures are proportionate. They should also be dissuasive to those who are involved in the illicit trade.
In addition, Member States should ensure that:
they do not allow the import or export of weapons and ammunition with a view to their illicit manufacture or export;
they prohibit the import of arms or ammunition intended for the manufacture or importation of such weapons or ammunition, as well as their parts or components.
They must also ensure that the export controls are effective and proportionately dissuasive in order to prevent the illicit trafficking of these arms and munitions.
Each Member State must inform the European Commission of the laws, regulations and administrative provisions adopted in implementation of this regulation, including the measures adopted in relation to the import and transit measures. It must also inform the Commission of any measures adopted by the Member States to prevent or combat the trafficking of such arms and/or ammunition. The Commission must also report to the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union on the implementation of the regulation every 5 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 3 July 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
The United Nations Firearms Protocol, the purpose of which is to promote, facilitate and strengthen cooperation among parties in preventing, combating and eradicating illicit manufacturing, importing, exporting and transiting firearms and ammunition, entered into force on 3 July 2005.
For more information, see:
The UN Firearms Protocol (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cartel: a group of countries that have signed, or are parties to, the United Nations Convention on the Suppliers of Arms and Ammunition (UNCFSP).
* Firearms: a class of firearms that are intended for military use.
* Parts and components: parts and parts intended to be used in the manufacturing, export (or import) of firearms (parts or components).

Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 105:
EUROSUR — situational awareness and reaction capability at the external borders of the EU countries
EurOSUR – operational framework
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the operational framework of the European Union’s external borders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework to improve the situational awareness, detection, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contributing to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ external borders (‘external borders’). It does not apply to the Schengen area.
The European Union Agency for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms (EurosUR) is responsible for the operational management of the EUROSUR framework. It is the main source of information and coordination between the national coordination centres of EU countries and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCA).
The EU Agency and the Member States must ensure that procedures are in place to monitor the technical and operational functioning of the framework and to ensure that it meets the objectives of the regulation.
EU countries may exchange information and cooperate with one or several neighbouring third countries. They must also ensure that they respect fundamental rights, including the principle of non-refoulement.
National coordination centres
Each EU country must establish a national coordination centre to coordinate with each other, directly and in near-real-time the situational picture of neighbouring external border sections relating to:
incidents and other significant events;
tactical risk analysis reports as contained in the events layer;
the analysis layer.
All incidents are shared with the Agency.
In addition, the Agency and Member States may share information and cooperation with the national border control authorities of neighbouring countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 2 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
Eursur is the EU agency for the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms. It works closely with the Border Control Agency (BACA) to improve situational awareness at the EU's external borders.
For more information, see:
External borders (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* External borders: the territory of an EU country where there is a risk of illegal immigration.
* Border control: measures taken to prevent, detect, combat and combat illegal immigration, including border control measures.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 2 November 2013 establishing the Operational Framework for the European External Borders of the Union (EUROSSUR) (OJ L 136, 2.12.2013, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
  The European Commission adopts Regulation (EU, Europol) 2018/852 on the implementation of the operational and technical framework for the enforcement of the common border control policy of the Community.

Commission Regulation (EEC, Eurostat) 2017/856 of 26 November 2017 on the coordination of the external border control system of the Council of Europe.

Summary 106:
Eurodac system for fingerprint searches: non-contractual liability of EU countries in connection with the operation of the Eurodac fingerprint system
EurodAC system for fingerprints
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 604/2013 laying down specific rules for the operation and maintenance of the European Union’s fingerprint system for searches
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules governing the operation, maintenance and management of the EU-wide fingerprint system (EuroDac) for searches for criminal offences.
It repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 2725/2000.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to searches carried out by EU countries’ designated authorities for law enforcement purposes. It does not apply to searches conducted by the European Border and Coast Guard (BERG) or the police of non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that the searches are carried out in cooperation with the other EU countries concerned.
Requests for fingerprint data are sent to the EuroDac Central System, which is responsible for processing and storing the data.
The Central System:
processes all requests for data relating to persons referred to in Article 9, 10, 11 and 12 of the regulation;
responds to all requests within 24 hours.
Searches must be carried out on the basis of the following criteria:
identification of the person concerned;
fingerprints must be taken as soon as possible;
the person concerned must be informed of the purpose of the search;
a description of the location of the fingerprint data; and
the results of the searches must be transmitted to the Member State of origin.
If the results of a search are not possible to identify the person who was searched, they must be stored in the Central System only for the purposes of Article 28.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
From 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EuroDAC system is one of the main tools used to identify criminal offenders in the EU. It was set up in 1999.
For more information, see:
‘EuroDAC’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 608/2013 of 21 December 2013 laying down the specific rules concerning the operation (recast) and maintenance (renewal) of the eurodac European Union fingerprint system.
Successive amendments and corrections to Regulation (Euratom) have been incorporated in to the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 107:
Designation of origin of traditional products — EU rules on the protection of traditional foods and products
Designation and labelling of traditional food and products — European rules on their protection
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1257/1999 — designating and classifying traditional products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the protection and classification of products with a European Union (EU) designation of origin*.
It aims to ensure that products from the EU’s eastern and central regions and mountain areas are protected from trade barriers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following products:
traditional products originating in a specific place, region or, in exceptional cases, a country;
products whose quality or characteristics are essentially or exclusively due to a particular geographical environment with its inherent natural and human factors;
the production steps of which all take place in the defined geographical area.
Traditional products
Products covered by the regulation include:
foods originating in the eastern or central regions of the EU (e.g. mountain areas);
traditional specialities guaranteed by EU countries;
traditional foodstuffs guaranteed by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts to extend the scope of the regulation to include products from non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that the products listed in the regulation comply with the following conditions:
the products are not only produced in the EU, but that they are also originate from outside the EU;
they are not subject to any restrictions on their trade.
they do not have to be labelled as ‘traditional products’.
The Commission may, in the case of certain types of chocolate as confectionery products, exclude them from the regulation if they are:
made in a non-European country,
produced in an EU country other than the EU country in which they originate;
originating in a place, country or region other than that in which the products originate.
Exemptions
The EU countries may exclude certain products, such as:
chocolate and derived products;
prepared meals;
beer;
beverages made from plant extracts;
pasta;
pestilents and animal breeds;
wine and spirits;
salt.
Trade marks
The following trade marks are excluded from the definition of ‘designation and classification’:
trade marks registered in breach of the first subparagraph of this regulation:
any trade mark which is used in connection with a product which is:
designated as a product originating in, or derived from, a specific geographical area; or
any name which identifies a product as originating from a specific area, region, or country.
In addition, the Commission may exclude trade marks which are used to identify products which are not traditional products.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2000. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Traditional products: products which originate in, originate from, or derive from, an area or a country, and have the following characteristics:
quality or characteristics;
origin;
location;
production steps.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Implementing Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 658/1999 of 21 December 1999 on the designating, classifying and classing traditional products (OJ L 127, 1.1.2000, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Eurat) No 758/99 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Safety of oilship passengers and cargo

Summary 109:
European Banking Supervisory Authority (ESA)
European BankingSupervisory Authority
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1092/2010 — European Banking Supervision and Supervisory Mechanism
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European banking supervisory authority.
It aims to ensure that European banking supervisors (ESAs) have a single set of rules to ensure the sound operation of the EU’s financial system.
KEY POINTS
The European Banking Authority (EBA) plays a leading role in promoting transparency, simplicity and fairness in the market for consumer financial products or services across the internal market, including by:
collecting, analysing and reporting on consumer trends;
reviewing and coordinating financial literacy and education initiatives by the competent authorities;
developing training standards for the industry;
contributing to the development of common disclosure rules.
The EBA is responsible for:
monitoring and assessing systemic risk and responding to warnings and recommendations by the European Securities and Markets Authority and the European System for Risk Management Board (ESRB);
providing advice and guidance to the European Central Bank (ESB) and the Financial Stability Board (FSB) on systemic risk;
assessing and advising the European Commission on the prudential supervision of financial institutions;
preparing a work programme for the following year.
In addition, the EBA and the ESRB are responsible for monitoring and assessing the systemic risk of the whole or part of the financial system of the European Union (EU).
.
EBA and ESAs are subject to the same rules as national authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
 
It has applied since 30 June 2010.
BACKGROUND
   
The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and modernise European banking supervision.
For more information, see:
European banking supervision (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 110:
Freedom of movement of workers
Freedom of freedom of movement
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2011 on freedom of movements of workers in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It aims to ensure that workers who wish to move to another EU country can do so without any discrimination.
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (Euratom) No 852/2008.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all workers pursuing an activity in the territory of an EU country and any employer pursuing the activity of an activity outside the EU.
The directive applies to:
any national of a Member State pursuing an employment activity in another Member State;
any employer pursuing an undertaking in the territories of an individual Member State.
EU countries must ensure that:
all workers wishing to move may exchange their applications for and offers of employment, and may conclude and perform contracts of employment in accordance with the provisions in force laid down by law, regulation or administrative action, without discrimination resulting therefrom.
Employers must also inform workers of the conditions of living and working conditions.
Workers must be regularly informed of living, working conditions and of any discrimination resulting from their decision to move.
Nationals of the Member States concerned must forward to the Commission the list, drawn up by common accord, of services referred to in paragraph 1 and the Commission publishes such list for information, and any amendment thereto, in the Official Journal of the following the Commission’s publication of the list.
.
Any national of an employer pursuing a business activity in an activity within an EU Member State may exchange information on these conditions with an employer from the Member State in which the activity is taking place.
Exchange of information
The Commission publishes a list of the services covered by the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
The European Union (EU) has been working towards the creation of a single market for workers’ movement within the EU since 2004.
For more information, see:
Movement of workers (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Employment: any employment contract or offer of employment.
* Employer: any person who has the right to employ a person who is a national of one Member State and who is pursuing the activities of an undertaking outside that Member State under the conditions laid down in the regulation and who does not have the same rights as a national.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (CE) No 550/2011 of 21 June 2011 on freedom to move within the European Community

Summary 111:
Protocol on the destruction of hazardous substances
Protocol against the destruction and re-use of dangerous substances
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on the implementation of the Protocol on the Destruction and Re-Use of Hazardous Substances
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) and its Member States fulfil their obligations under the Kyoto Protocol.
It recasts and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 1082/2008, which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2009.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the placing on the market of controlled substances for essential laboratory and analytical uses, and processes in which controlled substances may be used as process agents. It does not apply to the production, maintenance or servicing, including refilling, of products and equipment or in other processes.
Trade with a State not party to the protocol and a territory not covered by the protocol may not be authorised for a period which extends beyond 31 December 2019.
The European Commission is empowered to align Annexes to this regulation with decisions of the parties, in particular with those concerning approved destruction methods, conditions for placing on market of the substances and processes for their use.
Exemptions from the regulation
A number of exemptions are granted to certain substances, such as those listed in the annexes to the regulation. These include:
the use of substances or new substances in the production or maintenance of products or equipment;
the utilisation of new substances;
in the manufacture, maintenance, repair or refilling of products;
use of substances and equipment in the manufacture or repair of products.
Annexes to Annex I and II of the regulation provide for the exemption of certain substances from the list of substances which are subject to the prohibition of their re-exportation to non-EU countries.
In addition, the regulation provides for an exemption for certain substances listed in Annex III of the protocol.
Importers of these substances must inform the European Commission of the reasons for their exclusion from the EU’s list of banned substances and the conditions for their placing on its market.
EU countries must also inform the Commission of any measures taken by them to prevent the re-export of the controlled substances.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The Kyoto Protocol is the cornerstone of the EU strategy to combat the proliferation of dangerous chemicals. It aims to protect human health, the environment and the economy.
For more information, see:
‘The Kyoto protocol’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Hazardous substance: a substance that can be dangerous to human health and the environment.
* Process agent: a process agent which can be used to process a substance to produce a substance.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
    барканска ганка (Recast) (OJ L 183, 1.1.2010, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, No 553/2009 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

EU Agency for Research and Innovation (R&I) — rules on the organisation and functioning of the Agency for Science and Technology (S&T)
EU Agency FOR RESEARCH AND INSPECTION (R & I)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and responsibilities of the European Union Agency (EU) for Research, Innovation and Informatics (R and I).
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 1210/90.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Scientific and Technological Research (DGSR) and the European Research Council (ERC).
The agency is responsible for:
research and innovation;
the management of the R&I;
administration of the agency;

all staff matters.
The R & I Director-General is responsible:

initiatives

an Executive Director who is appointed by the Board of Directors;
an Administrative Director, who is responsible to the Board for the implementation of decisions taken by the management Board.

Summary 113:
Cooperation and partnership with enterprises in the field of the environment: EU environmental action programme
Cooperating and partnership between enterprises and NGOs
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2009/852/EC on the European Union’s cooperation and partnership programme for the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
 
It establishes a European Union (EU) environment action programme (EAP) which aims to improve the environmental performance of enterprises and small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs).
It aims to encourage the improvement of collaboration and partnership among enterprises in order to achieve the objectives of the EAP.
KEY POINTS
Cooperation
The EAP aims to:
promote cooperation and partnerships between EU countries’ authorities and SMEs;
improve the environmental management of enterprises;
encourage the participation of small organisations in environmental management schemes;
ensure that EU countries take the necessary measures to ensure that enterprises have access to information and support funds specially adapted to their needs.
EU countries must ensure that organisations get access to the information and assistance they need regarding legal requirements relating to the environment in that EU country.
Organisations must:
have a sound legal basis for their activities;
be based on sound environmental management principles;
take the necessary steps to protect the environment;
have the necessary financial resources to implement their activities.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of this EAP, including:
monitoring the implementation;
assessing the impact of the programme;
providing information to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Commission and the European Environment Agency (EEA) on the environmental impact of projects;
developing a common approach to environmental management.
Promotion of participation of enterprises
Member States must take measures to encourage participation of SMEs in EAP activities. They must ensure they have the necessary resources and support to implement the programme. These resources include human resources and specialised skills, organisational infrastructure, technology and financial resources.
In addition, Member States must promote the participation and involvement of small enterprises by:
ensuring that reasonable registration fees encourage their participation;
promoting technical assistance measures.
Participation of environmental verifiers and environmental assessment bodies
Member countries must establish a competent body to carry out environmental assessment and assessment of organisations’ activities. The competent body may be national, regional or local.
Accreditation and licensing bodies
The competent body must ensure the organisation has the necessary accreditation and licence to carry its activities out. The organisation must submit its application for accreditation or licence to the competent body, which then evaluates the application and issues a certificate of validity. The accreditation is valid for 5 years.
Annex II of the decision sets out the criteria for the selection of the accreditation body and the criteria to be followed for the licensing body.
A European Commission report on the evaluation of the bodies’ work concludes that the bodies have performed a good job in their tasks and that they should continue to do so.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
European Union environmental action programmes (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Decision (EC) No 852/2009 of 21 December 2009 on the Community Environment Action Programme for the Environment (OJ L 283, 24.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Decision (EOP) No852 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 114:
Rules on the definition, description, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks (Regulation (EEC) No 1576/89)
Rules on:
the definition, describing, presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications of certain spirit drinks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the definition and description of spirits and their labelling.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EU) No 552/89.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to spirit drinks produced by flavouring ethyl alcohol of agricultural origin or grain spirit and/or grain distillate with juniper (Juniperus communis L. or Juniperus oxicedrus L.) berries. It does not apply to spirits produced by adding alcohol to alcohol.
Labelling
A spirit drink must bear the sales denomination ‘spirit drink’. It must also bear the name ‘wine’ or ‘spirits’ in the format set out in the regulation’s annexes.
The label must contain the following information:
a description of the spirit drink;
the name of the geographical indication;
an indication of the country of origin;
a list of the countries where the spirit drinks are produced.
Directories for the marketing of spirits
Directives for the production and presentation of spirits are laid down in Annex II of the regulation.
Distributors must ensure that the label of a spirit drink bears the label ‘sparkling wine’, ‘vodka’ and ‘pomace’ as indicated in the category of spirit drink to which it belongs.
Where there has been addition of alcohol, as defined in Annex I to the regulation, the label must state the percentage by volume of pure alcohol it represents in the total pure alcohol content by volume in the mixture of the mixture. This percentage must not exceed 0.5%.
Directive 2013/13/EU amended the regulation to:
introduce a new category of spirits for the purpose of marketing;
limit the number of categories of spirits that can be marketed under the label to 14;
ban the use of the term ‘premium’ for spirits other than those listed in the annexes;
establish a European Commission list of spirits to be marketed in the European Union (EU).
The Commission must publish a report every 5 years on the implementation of these rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to modernise and modernise the rules on the marketing and presentation, as well as on the protection of certain geographical indications.
For more information, see:
‘Directives on the labelling, marketing and promotion of spirits’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 115:
Safeguarding civil aviation: common rules for safeguarding passengers and goods against unlawful interference with civil aircraft
Safer civil aviation : common rules to safeguard passengers, goods and infrastructure
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 849/2004 — common rules on safeguarding civil aircraft against acts of unlawful interference
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common European Union (EU) rules for the safeguarding of persons and goods travelling by air within the EU.
It aims to protect the security of the EU’s aviation infrastructure by establishing common rules.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the carriage of cargo and mail by air between EU countries. It does not apply to international flights.
EU countries must:
ensure that:
cargo and mail originating from non-EU countries are carried out in accordance with the rules and standards laid down by the European Union Aviation Safety Agency (EASA);
airport security measures are applied at all EU airports;
airside security controls are in place to ensure the safety of passengers and items carried on board aircraft;
the security of airside and airside parts of security restricted areas are maintained.
Airport security controls must be:
more stringent than those applied by national authorities;
more frequent inspections of cargo or mail;
detailed in implementing acts.
Cargo or mail originating outside the EU may only be carried out if:
the consignor originates in an EU country;
known consignors meet common security rules and procedures sufficient to allow carriage of goods on any aircraft; or mail on any EU aircraft; and
the cargo is transferred to a known consignee.
The rules and measures laid down in the regulation apply to:
transport cargo and transfer mail originating in non-European countries;
transportation of mail between non-EC countries; or between EU and non-EEA countries.
In addition, EU countries must apply:
rules and standards for the screening of persons other than passengers;
rules for the security screening of items carried by air;
measures to protect airside security;
common security rules for airside areas;
requirements for security screening at airports.
Implementing acts
EU governments must ensure that the following implementing acts are adopted:
a national implementing act must be drawn up and published in the Official Journal;
an implementing act may be adopted by the Commission with the prior authorisation of the European Parliament and of the Council;
a report must be prepared on the implementation of the implementing act.
A report must also be prepared for the European Commission on the application of the regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 20 November 2004.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Safer air transport’ on the EASA's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 802/2004 of 11 October 2004 on common rules concerning safeguarding Civil Aviation (OJ L 158, 30.4.2004, pp. 1-4)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Eurat) No802/2004 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 117:
EU military operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFORCE IN THE MARYLAND: EUNAFOR MED IRINI
EUMAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 of 31 March 2020 on a European Union military operation on the Mediterranean.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DECISION DO?
It authorises the European Union (EU) to contribute to the implementation of the United Nations arms embargo on Libya with aerial, satellite and maritime assets.
It sets up a military operation, known as EUAVFOR MEDINI, to carry out inspections of vessels bound to or from Libya where there are reasonable grounds to believe that such vessels are carrying arms or related materiel in violation of the UN arms embargo.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision authorises EUAMERICA’s military operation to:
operate on the high seas off the coast of Libya, in accordance with the relevant UN Security Council Resolution (UNSCR) 2292 (2016) on the arms embargo;
carry out inspections on the Libyan coast of the Mediterranean, in compliance with the UNSCR 2292 and as required;
contribute to the enforcement of the EU arms embargo with aerial and satellite assets;
conduct training and capacity building and training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy;
ensure the proper functioning of the International Maritime Organization (IMO) and other relevant international organisations;
monitor and monitor the activities of the maritime sector in the area of operation.
EUAMERISA is responsible for:
monitoring and monitoring the movement of goods and persons in and out of Libya;
collecting and storing personal data concerning persons involved in the carriage of prohibited items;
assisting the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY) and the Organisation for the Prohibition of Chemical Weapons (OPCW) in their investigations into the illicit trade in arms;
providing technical advice and assistance to the Libyan authorities and the international community in the field of maritime security.
Role of the European External Action Service (EEAS)
The EAS is responsible:
for providing technical assistance and technical advice to the EUEMISA;
for monitoring and advising the European Commission and the Council of Europe on the operation;
informing the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Committee on Foreign Affairs and Security Policy of the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
1.1.2020
It has applied since 1 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a member of the OPCW.
For more information, see:
European Union military action in Libya (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* EUAMERS: EU Member States’ armed forces.
* IMO: the international organisation responsible for enforcing the UN Arms embargo against the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMDs).
* NATO: the military arm of the NATO military alliance, which includes the United States, France, Germany, Italy, Spain, the United Kingdom and the United Arab Emirates.

Summary 118:
Document fraud: exchange of information on authentic documents and methods of falsification between EU countries (FADO system)
Document fraud : sharing of information about authentic and false documents
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated act (EU) 2019/852 on the exchange of data on authentic and falsified documents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It sets up the European Union Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation’s (Europol) FADO (Fraud Analysis and Analysis of Documents Online) system.
It aims to improve the information exchange between national authorities in order to combat document fraud.
KEY POINTS
EU countries’ national authorities must exchange information on:
authentic documents;
known methods of forgery;
the security features of authentic documents; and
statistics on detected false documents.
National authorities must ensure that the information they share is accurate and up-to-date. They must also share information on the techniques used to commit forgery and on the methods used to detect false documents, as well as statistics on the number of false documents that have been detected.
Fraudulent documents must be reported to Europol, which then forwards the information to the European Commission, which forwards it to the national authorities of each EU country.
The Commission must:
use the information it receives to:
identify authentic documents, including images, and specimens thereof, and their security features;
assess the authenticity of counterfeit documents and the method of their falsification;
make recommendations on how to prevent them from being made available to other EU countries;
ensure that authentic documents are not made available for use in non-EU countries.
collect information on false documents and their methods.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The directive is part of a package of measures designed to combat the threat posed by document fraud in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Document fraud’ on Europol's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Document fraud: the falsification of documents by means of deception, falsification or deception of the identity of a person or of a legal entity.

Summary 119:
Proportionality of access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions: EU rules on access to and pursuit of certain professions
Proportality of the access to certain professions: European rules on accessibility to and the pursuit thereof
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2005/36/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the regulation of certain regulated professions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that access to regulated professions is not restricted.
It sets out rules on the proportionality of these rules.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all regulated professions. It does not apply to professions that are not covered by the Charter of Fundamental Rights (‘the Charter’).
It does not cover:
the professions that have been regulated directly by the European Union (EU) or to professions regulated indirectly by giving a particular professional body the power to regulate.
EU countries may restrict access to a regulated profession if they consider it to be contrary to public policy or to the interests of the public.
The regulation must be proportionate to the number of people who are involved in the profession. It must not be disproportionate to the total number of persons involved in that profession.
Ex ante assessment of new measures and monitoring
Member States must carry out proportionality assessments in an objective and independent manner. They must communicate the results of these assessments to the European Commission, launching a ‘mutual evaluation process’. The Commission must facilitate the exchange of information between the results and monitor the implementation of the measures.
Procedure
The Commission must ensure that the measures are implemented in line with the principles of proportionality.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYEARLY ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
 
  The Charter of fundamental rights guarantees the freedom to choose an occupation, as well as freedom to conduct a business.
For more information, see:
‘The Charter of Rights’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Regulation of a profession: a regulation that restricts access to or pursuit of that profession by a Member State.
* Directive: a law that restricts the access of a person to a particular profession, in order to protect the public interest.
ACT
 			
Council Directive 2005/35/EC
1.1.2005
-
OJ L 136 of 1.2.2005.
RELATED ACTS
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/13/EU of 25 November 2014 on the application of Directive 2005:35/EU on the assessment of the measure to be taken by Member States concerning the regulation and implementation of certain professional bodies (OJ C 202 of 25.11.2014).

Summary 120:
Management of fish stocks in the North Sea and Baltic Sea (2016/0238/COD) and the Baltic Sea: restoring fish stocks to sustainable levels of fish yield (MSY)
Management of fishes in the north sea and Baltic sea (2016-2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 0238/2016 on the management of fisheries and fisheries management in Union waters of the north Sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s fisheries and aquaculture policy is based on science-based management plans to restore fish stocks, in the shortest time feasible, at least to levels that can produce maximum sustainable yield of fish.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the North Sea (North Sea)
the Baltic Sea
the Black Sea (Baltic Sea).
It does not apply to certain stocks of fish (e.g. the Black Sea, Black Sea and Black Sea) which are not covered by this regulation.
It applies to all stocks of species to which the landing obligation applies under Articles 15 and 16 of Regulation (EC) No 1380/2013.
Management plans
EU countries must draw up and implement management plans for fish stocks that are subject to a landing obligation. These must be based on the best available scientific advice and must be consistent with the objectives set out in the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs). They must also include:
fishing opportunities;
target species;
fisheries;
measures to reduce overfishing, illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing and destructive fishing practices.
The EU countries must ensure that fishing opportunities are open to all species of fish, regardless of their size.
In addition, EU countries are required to take the necessary measures to ensure the long-term sustainable yield (FMSY*) of the fish stocks covered by the regulation. This is achieved by:
ensuring that fish stocks are managed according to the best scientific advice available;
establishing a monitoring system;
developing a management plan for each stock of fish;
setting fishing opportunities for each species.
Fisheries must be open to fish of all sizes, irrespective of their weight, size, age, sex and geographical distribution.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
It entered into force on 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
The North Sea is one of the most important fisheries in the European Union (EU). It is also the largest source of fishing revenue for the EU.
For more information, see:
North Sea fisheries (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Fishing opportunities: fishing opportunities that are open for all fish species, including those that live at or near the bottom of the water column.
* Target species: those that have a high yield of Maximum Sustainable Yield of Fishy Mass.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 2185/2016 of 21 May 2016 on the implementation of fisheries management and fisheries control in Union territories of the North sea (OJ L 136, 22.5.2016, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2016/2185/EU have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 121:
Convention on the Law of the Sea — EU rules on the conservation and management of the living resources of the sea
Convention of the Seas
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 on the Conservation and Management of the Living Resources of the Ocean
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and procedures that EU countries must follow to ensure that the sustainable management and conservation of the marine life of the oceans.
It is part of the EU’s common fisheries policy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels registered in the EU and to the fishing grounds of EU countries’ ports.
EU countries must report to the European Commission on the catches of bottom fishing species from the preceding month in accordance with the rules laid down in the annexes to the regulation.
The Commission must report the data to the Commission within 15 days of the end of each month.
Fisheries must:
report the total number of catches of the species covered by the regulation on the basis of the points of contact and designated ports;
report on the number of vessels involved;
record the total amount (weight or volume) of remaining marine resources not recorded under point (j), (k) or (l) discarded, split to the lowest known taxon;
make a list of species of concern;
collect biological sampling of the parts of the catch;
ensure that bycatch mitigation measures are used to reduce the impact of bycatch on other species of marine life.

Summary 122:
Exemptions from the obligation to disclose cash to the Financial Action Task Force (FATF) and the European Union’s anti-money laundering and combating terrorism (OLAF) system
Exemptations from the obligations to disclose unaccompanied cash to EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005 on the obligation of EU countries to disclose unauthorised cash
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the obligation on EU countries’ authorities to disclose to the United Nations’ Office for the Prohibition of Illicit Financing of Financial Crime (FATSF) any cash they believe to be the proceeds of illicit activities.
It aims to prevent the reintroduction of illicit proceeds into the economy and the diversion of money to finance illicit activities, and to protect the functioning of the internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to cash:
unaccompanied cash;
cash used as highly-liquid stores of value:
coins with a gold content of at least 90 %;
bullion such as bars, nuggets or clumps of gold containing at least 99.5 %.
Unaccompanied cash must be disclosed to the International Monetary Fund (IMF).
EU countries must also disclose the following:
the names and addresses of any consignments, receptacles or means of transport which may contain unaccompanied cash, as well as the names, addresses, addresses and telephone numbers of the persons or entities involved in the illicit activities;
the name and address of the person or entity responsible for these transactions.
the number and postal address where the cash is found.
The competent authorities have the power to carry out controls on any consignment of cash which may carry unaccompanied cash.
Exchange of information between competent authorities and with the European Commission
The Commission must exchange information between the competent authorities of the EU countries and the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY).
Exchanges of information must be carried out in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EU) 2016/679 (see summary) on the exchange of information.
Information must be kept for a minimum of 5 years. The personal data must be erased upon the expiry of that period.
In addition, the Commission must:
ensure that personal data are not used for the purposes of personal data collection, processing, communication and other processing;
keep a record of the personal data it collects and transmits to the European Central Bank (ECB).
The European Commission must ensure that the information it receives is accurate, reliable and up-to-date, and that it is used only for the purpose of preventing, detecting, investigating and prosecuting criminal activity.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2005.
BACKGROUND
The United Nations Office for Economic and Financial Affairs (UNIFORM) has been working closely with the EU to combat the illicit financing of terrorism and the illicit diversion of funds to finance it.
For more information, see:
‘The fight against terrorism’ on the UNIFORm’ website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 89/2005 of 27 June 2005 on the obligations of Member States to disclose the unauthorized cash to certain international organisations and bodies (OJ L 136, 29.6.2005, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 89/05 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS

See consolidated version.

Summary 123:
Ensuring energy security and climate neutrality: national energy and climate plans (2016-2021)
Ensustainable energy and climatic policies
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/2021 on energy security, climate neutrality and energy efficiency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s energy policies are aligned with the objectives of the Paris Agreement on climate change.
It sets out a national energy strategy for the EU.
KEY POINTS
The energy strategy must cover five dimensions: energy security; the internal energy market; energy efficiency; decarbonisation; and research, innovation and competitiveness.
The national energy strategies must:
contribute to:
energy security;
energy efficiency;
climate neutrality;
research and innovation;
decarbonisation.
Each EU country must set up an integrated national energy plan for the period 2016-2020. This must include:
a national energy efficiency strategy;
a long-term renovation strategy to support the renovation of the national stock of residential and non-residential buildings, both public and private;
measures to stimulate cost-effective deep renovation and policies and actions to target the worst performing segments of the building stock;
targeting energy efficiency potentials of gas and electricity infrastructure.
EU countries must also take measures to develop measures to utilise the potential of gas, electricity infrastructure, including:
developing measures to optimise energy efficiency and renewable energy resources;
ensuring the efficient use of natural gas;
encouraging research and innovation in the energy sector;
promoting the use of renewable energy sources;
providing incentives for the development of energy efficiency-efficient technologies;
supporting the development and deployment of smart grids and smart grids.
National plans must also include a national climate action plan.
A national energy action plan must be drawn up for each EU country by 31 December each year. It must include a description of the measures to be taken to achieve the objectives set out in the national plan. It should also cover:
the energy security of the EU;
the sustainable use of energy;
environmental objectives;
cost-effective energy efficiency.
Annex I to the directive sets out the objectives for the national energy plans. It also sets out how the national plans are to be implemented.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive entered into force on 4 November 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 4 November 2017.
BACKGROUND
The Paris Agreement was ratified by the EU on 5 October 2016. It aims to reduce greenhouse gas emissions by 40 % by 2020 compared to 1990 levels.
For more information, see:
‘The Energy Union’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Energy security: the EU's energy policy is based on the energy efficiency of its energy system. It is designed to protect and improve the efficiency of the energy system and to reduce the impact of climate change on the economy and the environment.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU, Euratom) No 1082/2016 of 4 November 2015 on energy and Climate neutrality and on energy efficiency (OJ L 127, 4.11.2016, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
   
 
Directives 98/70/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 October 1998 on energy policy and repealing Council Directive 98/60/EC (Official Journal L 347, 28.10.1998)
Communication from the Commission to European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the implementation of the Energy Union (COM(2016) 696 final, 4 December 2016)

Summary 124:
Chemical control measures — EU countries’ stockpiles of banned substances
Chemicals control measures: EU countries' stockpiles
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1907/2006 concerning the registration, Evaluation, Authorisation and Restriction of Chemicals
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect human health and the environment against the adverse effects of chemicals on human health.
It recasts and repeals Regulations (EC, EC, EC) No 793/93 and (EU) No 1488/94 on chemicals.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to substances that are banned in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to chemicals that are used for industrial purposes.
EU countries must:
monitor the use and management of notified stockpiles;
ensure that they are not used for the manufacture, import, export, importation, export or transfer of dangerous chemicals;
monitor and control the use of chemicals in their territory;
ban the manufacture and export of chemicals that could pose a risk to human health or the environment.
Exemptions from control measures
Certain substances are excluded from the regulation’s scope. These include:
tetrabromodiphenyl ether (Tetradioxin) and hexabromocyclodecane;
chlorinated biphenyls (Dioxin);
chlorophenyls and hexachlorohexanes;
nitrates;
dioxins;
polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs);
nitrosamines.
The European Union countries must ensure that the quantities of these substances are not exceeded by more than 0.01 % by weight.
Monitoring and management
The EU country responsible for monitoring and managing the stockpiles must inform the European Chemicals Agency (REACH).
REACH:
is responsible for the monitoring, evaluation, authorisation and restriction of chemicals and for the control of their use;
is assisted by the European Monitoring Centre for Hazardous Substances (EMC) and the European Chemical Safety Authority (ECSAA);
is funded by the EU budget;
has the power to adopt delegated acts and authorisations.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY INTO FORCE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 18 December 2006. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 18 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Chemicals’ on the European Commission's website.

Council Regulation (EC), EC, Euratom

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products: monitoring and reporting on their production and consumption
Single-used plastics: monitoring, reporting and reporting
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/865 on the monitoring, evaluation and reporting of plastic products placed on the market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce the use of single-use plastics in the European Union (EU). It aims to prevent and tackle marine litter.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to plastic products which are not designed for re-use or cost-effective recycling. It does not apply to food intended for infants and young children, food for special medical purposes or total diet replacement for weight control.
EU countries must monitor and report on the reduction measures taken and report to the European Commission on progress made to achieve the binding quantitative Union targets for consumption reduction.
The Commission must assess the progress made by the EU countries to achieve these targets every 2 years. It must also assess the impact of these measures on the environment.
It must establish a list of products that are banned from the EU market and the measures they must be taken to reduce their use.
In order to comply with the first subparagraph of this directive, each EU country must monitor the products listed in Part A of the Annex to the directive and take the necessary measures to ensure that they comply with its requirements. The first reporting period will be the calendar year 2022, with the exception of points (e) and (f) of the first reporting to the Commission for which will be reported in the first. The data and information must be provided in the format established by the Commission in accordance with the format set out in the annex.
Implementation of the directive
Each EU country is responsible for ensuring that the measures taken by its producers and placing products on its market conform with the directive’s requirements.
Extended producer responsibility schemes
Each country must allow the producers established in another EU country to appoint a legal or natural person established on its territory as an authorised representative for the purposes of fulfilling the obligations of a producer related to an extended producer responsibility scheme. The representative must be established in a transparent way between the actors concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision No 552/2013/EU has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle plastic pollution. These include:
the ban on the manufacture, import, export, importation, export and importation of plastic packaging and packaging waste;
measures to reduce plastic consumption;
the reduction of the amount of plastic used in food and food-related products.
For more information, see:
‘Single use plastic’ on the EU's website.

Summary 126:
The European Union’s Office for the Protection of the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (OLP) (codified Regulation (EU) 2016/852)
The European United Nations Office for Justice and Home Affairs (OJH)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852/2016 on the coordination of the procedures for the registration of transfer applications for the protection of the rights of persons with disabilities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU), as a whole, has a single register for the transfer applications it receives.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 182/2011, which had to become law in the EU countries by 23 March 2016.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all transfers between the EU and non-EU countries. It does not apply to:
transfers of goods and services between EU countries;
reciprocal transfers;
transport of persons;
transfer of property;
the transfer of assets.
Objectives
The purpose of the regulation is to establish a register of transfers.
Applicants must submit an application for a transfer of their rights to the Office. The application must be accompanied by a declaration of division of the applicant and a description of the reasons for the application.
The applicant must submit the application in a language other than English, French, German, Italian or Spanish. The annexes to the regulation must be translated into the official language of the EU country of origin.
If the application, the applicant must provide the following the procedure laid down in the annexes:
the reasons for their application,
the conditions of the transfer and
the reason for the decision to transfer the rights.
Annexes must contain information on the conditions of registration of a transfer and the conditions applicable to the implementation of the implementing acts.
Where a transfer is not registered, the Office must notify the applicant of the deficiencies that have been identified. The applicant must also provide the reasons why the transfer has not been registered. If the applicant fails to comply with these conditions, it must inform the applicant.
Appeals
An applicant may lodge an appeal with the Office of the European Parliament and the Council. The Office must decide on the appeal within 30 days of receiving the appeal. The decision must be unanimous. The losing party must bear the costs for one opposing party only and, where applicable, one representative only.
A Board of Appeal of the Office may decide on an appeal. It is composed of one representative from each EU country and may be chaired by the Executive Director. The Executive Director is assisted by a committee within the meaning of the Regulation.
Implementation of procedures
The Office adopts implementing acts to ensure the proper functioning of the register and the proper implementation of its procedures. It also adopts delegated acts to adopt delegated acts. The implementing acts are published in the Official Journal of the Union.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 23 March 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to modernise and modernise its financial rules. These include:
Delegated acts on the registration and implementation of transfers of rights and transfer applications;
Regulations on the establishment of a register and implementing acts for the register;
Directive 2014/13/EU on the harmonisation of the rules on the register of transfer application procedures; and
Council Directive 2014/14/EU establishing a register for transfer applications.
For more information, see:
European Union Office for Law Enforcement and Justice (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/12/EU of 23 March 2014 on the codification and repealing Regulation (Euratom) no 182/11/2011 of the Council and of the Court of Justice of the United Nations (Codified version).
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Council Directive (European Parliament and Council) No 13/2014 establishing a registry for transfer application Declarations of Transfer Applicants for the Registry of Transfer Applies for the Registrations of Transfer of Right and Transfer Applying for the Register of the Transfer Appendices for the Registrar of the Register.
Council Regulation (Euroatom) 2015/856 of 23 November 2015 establishing a Registry of Transfers and Implementing Acts on the Registration and Implementation of the Registration Applies to Transfer Applications for the Transposition of Rights, and repealing Council Directives 2014/15/EU, 2014/16/EU and 2014/17/EU.
