Summary 127:
Accessible formats for the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats of certain works and other protected subject matter (Directive on copyright and related rights)
Accessible format for the Making and Dispute Resolution
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the making, distribution and dissemination in accessible format of certain protected subject matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) does not infringe the copyright or related rights of persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled.
It requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations to copyright, related rights when making and distributing accessible formats, and for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the making and distribution of accessible format copies of works and related subject matter;
the exchange of these copies between authorised entities established in an EU country and those established in a non-EU country;
accessibility of these accessible formats to persons who can read or write in Braille, large print, adapted e-books, audio books and radio broadcasts.
Exceptions and limitations
Contracting parties must:
provide a list of the works covered by the exceptions and limitations of copyright, including those relating to the making or dissemination of accessible formats;
make available to the public or to authorised entities in the EU country the accessible formats they wish to make available;
ensure that these formats are made available to those who can hear, see or read them.
Authorised entities must ensure that they:
make accessible formats available to people who cannot read, see, write or hear in print;
transport the accessible format to the non-profit organisations established in the country in which they intend to make it available to them;
carry out the distribution, communication and making available of these formats to the general public.
Distribution, communication or making available to public
Distributing, communication, making available the public accessible formats may only be carried out on a nonprofit basis by authorised entities establishing in a Member State.
Cross-border exchanges
The EU country where an authorised entity is established has the right to make and distribute accessible formats in the territory of the other EU country. However, this right may not be transferred to a third country without the consent of that country’s competent authorities.
Accessibility of the public
The European Commission must ensure:
that the distribution of these accessibility formats is carried out in accordance with the ‘non-commercial objective of the Marrakesh Treaty’;
that access to these formats is made free of charge to persons with disabilities.
The Commission must inform the public of the conditions under which access to accessible formats can be made available.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2013/552/EU has applied since 1 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Accessibility and copyright’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Implementing Decision 2013/533/EU of 21 June 2013 on the Making, Distribution and Dissemination of Accessible Format Copies of Certain Works and Related Rights (OJ L 136, 21.6.2013, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Directive 2013/525/EU have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
last update 14.02.2016



Summary 128:
Coordinated investigation and enforcement of infringements with a Union dimension (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Coordination of investigations and enforcement mechanisms for widespread infringements
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2017/20/EU on coordinated investigations and cooperation between the Member States to tackle widespread infringement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve cooperation between Member States’ authorities in the field of consumer protection.
It sets up a single liaison office in each Member State responsible for coordinating the investigation, enforcement and monitoring activities of the competent authorities, other public authorities and designated bodies, in relation to infringements covered by this regulation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all infringements that are likely to have a significant impact on the EU’s economic, social or cultural life. It does not apply to:
illegal trade in goods and services;
unlawful trade in intellectual property rights;
non-contractual agreements between companies and individuals;
illegal transfer of intellectual property.
EU countries must:
cooperate with each other and the European Commission in the following areas:
collecting, classifying and exchanging data on consumer complaints;
developing and disseminating information and communication tools;
ensuring the development of sector-specific networks of officials involved in the application of this regulation;
establishing standards, methodologies and guidelines concerning the application.
In addition, Member States must inform the Commission of their activities in these areas. They must also inform each other of their own activities in the areas covered by the regulation, including:
the training of their officials;
the collection, classification and exchange of data on consumers’ complaints and information on infringements;
development of sector specific networks of public officials; and
the development of information-communication tools.
Cooperation
Member States must cooperate with the Commission in order to achieve the objective of the regulation:
to investigate, investigate and enforce infringements involving more than one Member State;
to identify infringements which have a European Union dimension.
To this end, they should:
ensure that the Commission has access to the information it needs;
coordinate their investigations and enforcements with other Member States;
take the necessary measures to ensure that infringements are detected and punished.
The Commission may also:
make requests for enforcement measures;
make recommendations to Member States concerning the enforcement measures to be taken;
provide information to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Committee on the Functioning of the European Union (CFEU) on the enforcement of infringement.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve coordination between the EU countries’ law enforcement authorities to tackle infringements. It complements Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 on the fight against fraud (see summary).
For more information, see:
EU action to tackle fraud (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Fraud: an illegal act that is likely to result in significant economic or social damage to the EU.
* Unlawful transfer: an act that has a significant effect on the economy and social life of a Member State.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2017/19/EU of 25 June 2017 on coordinated investigation and cooperation among the Member State authorities to combat widespread infringement with a European dimension (OJ L 136, 1.7.2017, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  EU Implementing Decision (EU, Euratom) 2017/22/EC of 27 June 2017 establishing a European Commission liaison office to coordinate the coordination of the activities of Member States' competent authorities to address infringements affecting the EU's economy and society.

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices: EU rules on their safety and health
In vitro diagnostics: European standards
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/865 on the safety and effectiveness of in vitro diagnostic devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to ensure that in vitro diagnostic medical devices (i.e. in vitro medical devices) are safe and effective.
It sets out rules for the design and manufacture of these devices.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all devices that can be used to diagnose, monitor and treat human diseases. It does not apply to medical devices intended for use in the laboratory.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
devices are designed and manufactured in such a way that they are safe, effective and effective;
they comply with the safety requirements laid down in the directive’s Annex II and Annex III;
there is a high level of protection of human health and the environment;
the devices are tested and certified in accordance with the European Medicines Agency (EMA) safety standards.
There is a risk assessment procedure to assess the safety of the devices. Manufacturers are responsible for ensuring that the devices comply with this assessment procedure.
Importers and distributors must cooperate with the competent authorities.
EU countries must:
ensure that devices meet the requirements set out in Annex IV and Annex V of the directive;
take the necessary measures to prevent, detect and investigate any risks to human health arising from the use of their products;
inform the European Commission of any changes to the devices that may be made to their design or manufacture.
The Commission is responsible for assessing the risks posed by the devices and taking appropriate action to eliminate them.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It applies from 1 January 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
 Health and safety of human beings
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve the safety, effectiveness and environmental aspects of the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘In vitro Diagnostics’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* In vitro medical device: a device that tests the health and environmental potential of human cells, tissues, organs, tissues and organs.

Summary 130:
Conformity assessment procedures for appliances and fittings
Consolidated rules on the labelling and labelling of electrical and electronic appliances
Concise rules for the making available on the European Union market
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2014 on the conformity assessment procedures applicable to appliances and fitting
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the manufacture, placing on the EU market and putting into service of electrical appliances* and electronic fittings.
It repeals Directive 2009/142/EC on conformity assessment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all electrical appliances, including:
electromagnetic compatibility (EM compatibility),
electrical compatibility (electromechanical compatibility) and
electronic compatibility (e.g. electromagnetic compatibility).
It does not apply to:
automotive appliances;
electro-electrical appliances, such as refrigerators, freezers, air conditioners, washing machines, compressors, boilers, dishwashers, washer-dryers, dryers and dryers;
gas appliances and boilers.
Importers must ensure that the appliance or fitting they place on the market bears the manufacturer’s name and address on the packaging. This includes cases where the importer would have to open the packaging to put his name or her name on the appliance.
The manufacturer must indicate a single point at which the manufacturer can be contacted. The manufacturer must also indicate the address of the manufacturer and the number of points of contact for the importers.
EU notified bodies must apply the following conformity assessment procedure:
a sampling system with a probability of acceptance of 95% with a non-conformity percentage of between 0,5% and 1%,
a limit quality of 5% with between 5% and 10%.
The notified bodies apply a quality assurance system with the following characteristics:
the manufacturer must apply a sampling system;
a level of quality corresponding to 95 %, with a percentage of 0.5%, with a limit of 5%.
An audit must include an assessment visit to the manufacturer's premises.
Manufacturers must provide the notified bodies with the information and documentation necessary to carry out conformity assessments. They must communicate any changes thereof within 6 months after the announcement of the envisaged changes.
If a manufacturer fails to comply with the regulation, the notified body must inform the European Commission of the reasons for the non-compliance and inform the manufacturer of the measures taken to rectify the problem.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts on the harmonisation of technical standards in the field of industrial products. These include:
Directive 2009/141/EC establishing a European Commission Directorate-General for Harmonisation of Technical Standards (now known as the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work);
Regulations (EC) No 765/2008 and (EU, Euratom) No 182/2011 on the quality assurance and conformity assessment of products entering the Union market.
For more information, see:
‘Electromagnetic Compatibility’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Electrical appliance: any electrical appliance or electronic device intended for use in a household or commercial environment.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/1025/EU of 21 December 2014 on the application of Regulation (EU), No 552/2014 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 25 October 2014 on conformity assessments applicable to certain electrical appliances (OJ L 136, 21.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 131:
CIT cash-in-transit (CIT) services: security and cost-effective transport of euro cash in the euro area
CITs cash-inspectors
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2010/33/EU on cash in transit
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the security of euro-denominated cash in circulation in the European Union (EU).
It sets out rules on the security and costs of cash in transport.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
cash in transit: cash handlers, banks, large retail sector and other professional cash handlers;
national central banks, national central bank branches or CIT cash centres.
It does not apply to cash services provided by national central banks.
EU countries must ensure that cash handlers have access to the best price and/or service and that they can take advantage of the cash services of the nearest central bank (NCB) branch, even if it is located in another EU country.
National central banks and CITs must:
provide the cash handlers with the best prices and/services;
take advantage of cash services offered by the NCB;
use the services of a CIT centre located in the country of origin or the nearest NCB branch.
In addition, they must provide the CIT company with:
the best price;
the most up-to-date and reliable service;
a high level of security;
access to the NCBs’ cash services.
Security measures
the CIT security staff must be:
highly trained and equipped with the necessary equipment and equipment;
prepared to deal with all types of emergencies;
capable of carrying out their tasks effectively;
committed to the safety and security of the euro cash;
cooperated with the national authorities of the EU country of transit.
Exemptions from security measures
National authorities may:
suspend or restrict the transport of cash if they consider that it poses a risk to national security, public order, public health or the environment;
ensure that the security measures are in place to prevent, detect and neutralise counterfeit euro notes;
allow cash handlers to carry out their duties in a safe manner;
notify national authorities if they believe that a counterfeit euro note is likely to be found in a container;
require that the container be accompanied by a certificate of authenticity.
The EU country in which the cash is to be transported must also ensure that it is accompanied by:
a declaration of authenticity;
an inventory of the banknotes to be carried out;
security measures to be put in place for the safe handling of counterfeit euro coins;
identification of the container’s contents;
measures to be taken in the event of a breach of security.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision (EU) 2020/852 has applied since 1 July 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Cash in transit’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* CIT: a company that carries out the cash in transposition of euro notes in the EU.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  Игородна баркова галка  года
Council Directive 2010/34/EU of 25 June 2010 on cash-intra-euro transport (OJ L 136, 1.7.2010, pp. 1-8)

Summary 133:
EU rules on the issuing of euro coins and collector coins (Regulation (EC) No 974/98 on the introduction of the euro and cent) (OJ C 273 of 16.9.2012)
EU rules governing the issuing and circulation of euro and euro cent coins and collecting of euro collector coins
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EU) No 1074/2012 on the issuance and circulation rules for euro coins
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for issuing and circulating euro coins.
It aims to ensure that euro coins are legal tender throughout the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to coins denominated in euro, euro cent and cent and complying with the denominations and technical specifications laid down by the Council. It does not apply to coins issued by the European Central Bank (ECB).
Issuance of circulation coins
EU countries must issue euro coins at face value. These coins must be legal tender in the issuing EU country.
Collector coins
Collectors’ coins must have the status of legal tender only in the country in which they are minted. They must not be used as a means of payment in any EU country other than the issuing country’s national currency.
The European Commission must conduct an impact assessment on the continued issuance of 1- and 2-cent coins. The Commission may decide whether to issue commemorative coins with a common design collectively issued by all EU countries whose currency is the euro. The decision to issue such coins is taken by Council.
EU Member States must take all appropriate measures to discourage the use of collector coins as payment.
Exemptions from the regulation
The Commission may exempt from the obligation to issue collector coins if they are:
issued by a Member State that is not an issuing Member State;
not issued by an EU country that is a member of the European Economic Area (EEA);
issued in circulation by a non-EU Member State which is a contracting Member State (non-ECB Member State).
Exclusions from the scope of the regulation:
collector coins are exempt from circulation rules when they are issued in the territory of an EU Member State other than that of the issuing State.
Member States must ensure that they do not use collector coins for the purposes of payment of goods and services other than those for which they have the legal tender status;
collectors must not use them as payment for goods or services that are not legal tender;
the Commission may, however, decide to exempt collector coins from the circulation rules if it considers that they are not being used for the purpose of payment by the issuing Member States.
In addition, Member States may exclude from circulation circulation coins that are issued by a State that has not been a contracting State, such as Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1) from the list of Member States that are issuing euro coins, provided that they comply with the rules laid down in the regulation.
Implementing Regulation
The implementing regulation sets out rules for the issuance of euro circulation coins. It sets out:
the volume of coins to be issued and put into circulation;
conformity with the requirements of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU;
circulation coins must comply with certain technical specifications;
exemptions must be limited to 1-euro coins, 1-second coins, 2-second and 3-second coin coins, and 1-cent coin coins;
consultation must take place between the European Commission and the national central banks of the participating Member States on the issue and circulation limits.
Conformity must also be ensured with regard to the issue of commemorative euro coins: the Commission must determine whether these should be issued collectively or individually by the Member States, and whether they should be made available to the general public.
Consultation and impact assessment
In order to ensure the proper functioning of the single currency, the Commission will conduct a communication to the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of this regulation. It will also present a report on the impact on the economy and the financial system of the decision-making process in the event of a change in the composition of the monetary base.

European Social Fund (ESF) — Youth Employment Initiative (YEI) (2013-2020)
European Social Funds (ESFs) — Young Employment Initiative(2013 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the European Social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s youth employment initiative (YEI).
It sets out specific rules for the funding of youth employment initiatives (YEIs).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the ESF's objectives and the types of activities that may be funded under the YEI.
The objectives of the programme are to:
promote the participation of young people in the labour market;
improve the quality of education and training for young people;
encourage young people to take part in economic and social mobility;
support the development of young workers’ skills and employability;
increase participation of women in labour market activities;
contribute to the creation of a more equal and inclusive labour market.
Programming and implementation
The programme may take one or more of the following forms:
a dedicated operational programme or a dedicated priority axis within an operational programme;
a part of a priority axis;
an integrated multi-fund operational programme.
It may also support actions to combat early school leaving.
In addition, the programme may support transnational cooperation actions, such as:
supporting young people not in employment, education or training (NEET), who are unemployed or inactive;
promoting the social inclusion of young persons not in labour markets;
improving access to employment, training and training;
providing information and communication measures to young people.
Transnational cooperation
EU countries with a single operational programme supported by ESFinance may exceptionally choose not to support certain actions, in duly justified cases and taking account of the principle of proportionality.
Evaluation
The European Commission must assess the results of the implementation of the regulation every 3 years.

It entered into force on 1 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
The ESF is one of the European Union's (EU's) social investment instruments. Its main tasks are to support the social cohesion of the EU and to contribute to the sustainable development of the economy.

‘Youth Employment Initiative’ on European Commission's website.

Summary 135:
Population and vital events: Eurostat’s annual population projections and data on population and on vital events (reference period)
Population and Vital events: European Union statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 — Eurostat's annual population and vital event projections
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection, transmission and dissemination of statistics on the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that the European Commission (Eurostat) is able to assess the long-term sustainability and quality of public finances on the basis of population projections produced by Eurostat.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
EU countries must provide Eurostat with data on the total population at national level at the reference time, within 8 months of the end of the reference year. The data must cover the following variables:
live births by sex, month of occurrence, live birth-order, mother’�s age, birth year of birth, country of birth and country of citizenship;
deaths by age, sex, year of residence, region of residence.
Eurostat must use the same population definition that they use for the data referred to in paragraph 1.
Vital events:
the number of deaths that occurred during the reference period. The reference period for vital events must be the calendar year in which the events occurred.
The Commission must provide the EU countries with the data on their vital events that occurred between 1 January and 31 August of the year in question.
Data on population
The data cover:
total population of EU countries;
total deaths of EU citizens;
the total number of births of EU residents;
number of deaths in EU countries of EU nationals of EU citizenship.

Summary 137:
Fisheries policy — Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) (2018-2022)
Fishers' policy — common fisheries policy (CPP) (2014-2021)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/2022 on the Common Fisheries Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision establishes the common fisheries programme, known as the CFP.
The regulation sets out the rules for the implementation of the common fishery policy.
KEY POINTS
The CFP is the EU’s main fisheries policy. It aims to:
improve fishing and aquaculture capacity;
contribute to the sustainable development of the fishing sector;
promote the sustainable management of fisheries;
ensure the sustainable use of the resources available to the European Union (EU).
The main objectives of the programme are:
promoting sustainable fishing;
encouraging the development of sustainable fisheries; and
improving the management of the fisheries sector.
To achieve these objectives, the programme must:
support sustainable fishing, including:
fisheries for the sustainable production and/or modernisation of fish stocks;
foster the sustainable diversification of fishing activities;
increase the fishing capacity of EU countries;
support the sustainable fishing of fish species;
provide technical assistance to the fishing industry;
facilitate cooperation between EU countries to improve the management and management of fishery resources.
In addition, the EU countries’ authorities must ensure that:
the sustainable fishing practices are implemented;
of fish stocks are maintained and maintained;
there is a high level of environmental protection;
the fishing sector is managed and managed in a sustainable way.
EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure that fish stocks continue to grow.
Fishing capacity
The programme aims to improve fishing capacity by:
increasing the number of fishing vessels registered in the EU;
developing and modernising fishing infrastructure;
increasing fish stocks.
Aquaculture
The EU countries must develop and modernise fishing infrastructure, such as:
aquaculture enterprises;
enhancing the knowledge and skills of fishers;
establishing a sustainable fishing industry.
Investment
The European Commission is responsible for:
providing financial support to EU countries that have not already done so;
monitoring the implementation and results of the programmes;
assisting the development and modernisation activities of the EU fisheries sector, including the establishment of sustainable fishing fleets;
investing in research and development;
exchanging information and best practice on sustainable fishing techniques and practices;
raising awareness of the importance of the fishery sector and of the need to diversify fishing fleets.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS AND REGULATIONS APPLY?
Decisions (EU, EU countries and the European Commission have to incorporate them into national law by 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
The common fisheries strategy was adopted by the European Parliament and the Council in 2013. The CFP was reformed in 2014.
For more information, see:
‘Fishery policy’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 138:

SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/65/EC on the establishment of common rules on the key information document for investment products for retail investors
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common rules for the drafting of key information documents (KIDs) for the sale of investment products to retail investors.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that KIDs are produced in a way that is easily understood by retail investors and that they are comprehensible to them.
The regulation applies to:
investment products designed to provide specific investment solutions tailored to the needs of retail investors;
transferable securities designed to be combined with insurance coverage or can be complex and difficult to understand.
It does not apply to investment products that are not covered by the EU’s general rules on investment products, such as pension plans, insurance plans, mutual funds and life insurance plans.
EU Member States must:
ensure that the KID is produced in such a way as to ensure that it is comprehensible and understandable to the retail investor;
make it available to all investors, irrespective of their nationality;
provide the information that retail investors need;
be clearly expressed and written in language and a style that facilitates the understanding of the information, in particular in a language that is clear, succinct and comprehensible.
inform the European Commission of the contents of the document.
In addition, the Commission must publish an annual report on the implementation of the regulation. This report must be made available to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions, as well as the European Investment Bank (EIB).
The Commission must also publish a list of the products covered by this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
  KEY TERMS

* Investment product: any financial product that can be transferred between two or more investors, including insurance plans and mutual funds.

investments in infrastructure, research and development;

finance for economic growth and jobs.

Summary 140:
Airport noise management: EU rules on aircraft noise management
Airport Noise Management: EU policies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2012 on aircraft-noise management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the management of aircraft noise at European Union (EU) airports.
It aims to ensure the effective functioning of the EU’s air transport systems and the protection of the environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all airports in the EU. It does not apply to airports in non-EU countries.
EU countries may impose noise-related operating restrictions (noise-related restrictions) on airports if they consider that there is a risk to aviation safety, airport capacity or competition. These restrictions may be temporary or permanent.
The European Commission may review the regulation if it finds that the process followed for the introduction of an operating restriction does not meet the requirements of this regulation.
Noise impact assessment and management
The noise impact assessment must be carried out by the European Commission. It must include:
a description of the effect on noise climate without further measures, and of those measures already planned to ameliorate the noise impact over the same period.
An assessment of the impact of the noise on the environment, including the impact on air traffic management.
A cost-benefit analysis must be conducted by the Commission to determine whether the measures to be taken should be implemented.
In addition, the Commission must take into account:
cost-benefit analyses
the impact on noise impact on aviation safety
the effect on airport capacity and competition
the effects on airport users
measures to reduce noise impact.
If the Commission considers that an airport operator or airport user has not taken the necessary measures, it may impose a noise restriction. The Commission must notify the relevant authorities of this decision within 3 months of receiving the request.
Where the Commission finds that an operator has not complied with the regulation, it must review the decision and inform the airport user.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE INTO FORCE?
 
   
It entered into force on 13 June 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of rules on noise management, including:
—
Directive 2014/36/EU on the assessment and control of environmental noise (see summary);
—  Â  х 
Commission Decision 2014/34/EU establishing a European Union Aviation Safety Agency (EASA) and repealing Directive 2002/49/EC on the monitoring and management of noise (See summary).
KEY TERMS
* Airport noise: the noise emitted by aircraft operating at an airport.
* Noise impact assessment: the number of aircraft flying in and out of an airport at any given time and the number and duration of flights.

Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings: EU rules on the administration of cross-border proceedings
Cross-Border insolvencies: EU rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 on the application of the European Union rules on cross-Border proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to improve the application by EU countries’ courts of the rules governing the administration and administration of insolvenic proceedings.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out rules on:
the jurisdiction of the courts of EU countries in the case of insolvent companies;
the procedures for the group coordination proceedings of a group of companies; and
the rights and obligations of the creditor.
It applies to the following types of proceedings:
group coordination proceedings: the proceedings of the members of a collective of companies whose assets and liabilities are divided between them.
Cooperation and communication: the regulation requires EU countries to cooperate and communicate with each other in order to ensure the effective administration of these proceedings. This cooperation may take any form, including the conclusion of agreements or protocols.
Directive (EU) 2019/852: the European Commission must present a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the implementation of the regulation by 27 June 2022.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 26 June 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 26 June 2017.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is one of the EU’s most important legal instruments for the fight against insolveny. It aims to ensure that the judicial authorities in EU countries can act effectively and efficiently in the event of a failure of an insolvent company.
For more information, see:
European Union rules governing cross-bankruptcy proceedings (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Group coordination proceedings. These are the proceedings which involve the financial creditors of a debtor.
Group coordination proceedings are those which are carried out by an undertaking which has been declared insolvent and whose assets are divided among its members. The undertaking must be approved by the known local creditors.
* Directive: the court of an EU country decides whether to open proceedings on behalf of the debtor. The decision is binding on the court in the country in which the debtor has its headquarters.

Summary 142:
Limitation of liability for damage caused to passengers in the event of a maritime accident (AthENS Convention)
Limitation on liability for damages caused in the case of an accident
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 on the limitation of liability of passengers involved in maritime accidents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) Convention on the Limitation of Liability for Damage Caused to Passengers in the Event of a Maritime Accident is implemented.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all domestic sea-going voyages. It does not apply to international voyages, such as those between the European Union (EU) and non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that insurance or other financial security is in force in respect of any ship that is licensed to carry more than twelve passengers, wherever registered, entering or leaving a port in their territory in so far as this Convention applies.
The insurance cover must be split in one war insurance part and one non-war insurance part, pursuant to the implementation guidelines adopted by the IMO in October 2006.
Insurance cover
Each EU country must ensure, under its national law, that the insurance or financial security, to the extent specified in paragraph 1 of the regulation’s annexes, applies to any ship carrying more than 12 passengers, regardless of whether it is registered in its territory or not.
Where the insurance cover is not in force, it must be provided at the latest on departure. In all other cases, it should be provided as soon after departure.
Liability
The liability of the carrier and the performing carrier must be joint and several and several. Where the carrier is liable, their liability must be shared and several, including:
the performing carrier;
the owner of the vessel;
any person or body responsible for the damage caused by the accident.
In addition, the carrier or performing carrier may be held liable for damage to passengers caused by any chemical, biological, bio chemical, or electromagnetic weapon.
If the accident is caused by a natural or manmade phenomenon, the liability of both carriers and performing carriers must be jointly and several (i.e. if the accident was caused by an intentional act).
The performing carrier or the owner of a vessel must be liable for any damage caused.
Damage to passengers
The convention sets out the liability rules applicable to:
damage caused by:
an intentional act or omission of a person from the ship;
damage to a vessel or body caused by negligence on the part of the ship or by a person responsible for its maintenance or repair;
damages caused by accidents caused by human error or negligence;
accidents caused by intentional acts or negligence caused by persons or bodies acting in a way that is likely to cause damage to a ship or body.
Implementation of the convention
Each country must implement the convention with due regard to the guidelines for implementing the convention.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The Convention on Liability of Passengers is one of a series of measures adopted to improve the protection of passengers who are the main victims of maritime accidents. The other measures include:
measures to prevent and reduce the risk of accidents;
measures for the prevention and reduction of damage to ships; and
measures aimed at improving the safety and security of passengers.
For more information, see:
International Maritime Organisation Convention on liability (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2006 of 21 December 2006 on the liability limitation of passengers in relation to damage caused on board ships (OJ L 348, 21.12.2006, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 550/06 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  ARTICLE

Summary 143:
Railway infrastructure capacity — European freight corridors
Railways infrastructure capacity – European freight networks
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2010 on the allocation of railway infrastructure capacity
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework for the establishment of freight corridors*.
It aims to improve the efficiency of the internal rail market and to ensure the efficient operation of the EU’s railway infrastructure.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the Rotterdam-Antwerp-Luxembourg-Metz-Dijon-Lyon/Basel freight corridor (Rotterdam) as well as to the Amsterdam-Ludwigsburg-Lithuania freight corridor.
It does not apply to the rail infrastructure of the Schengen area.
Establishment of a freight corridor
A freight corridor must be proposed by the EU country in which it is located.
The EU country which proposes the freight corridor is responsible for setting up the management board of the corridor and appointing the infrastructure managers. The management board is assisted by an advisory group. The advisory group is composed of representatives from the EU countries concerned. It may also issue own-initiative opinions.
Infrastructure managers
The infrastructure managers of the freight corridors and the advisory group must put in place procedures to ensure optimal coordination between the organisation of the railway infrastructure and the terminals.
A register of applications for infrastructure capacity must be kept for a period of 5 years. This register must be made freely available to all interested parties.
An executive board must define the framework for allocation of the infrastructure capacity on the corridor in accordance with Article 14(1) of Directive 2001/14/EC. The executive board may decide to levy charges for the use of rail infrastructure for the transport of freight.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 10 November 2010 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 10 November 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to encourage the development of rail transport networks in the European Union. These include:
TEN-T, research and development programmes;
Marco Polo, the European Regional Development Fund and the European Investment Bank.

European rail network (European Commission).

Summary 144:
Road transport operators: professional qualification
Road transport users: professional qualifications
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the pursuit of the occupation of road transport operator
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that road transport operators have a high level of professional qualification.
It sets out the rules and procedures for the qualification of professionals in this field.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all road transport users in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to:
road haulage operators;
vehicle maintenance management;
assignments of loads;
the assignment of loads effective and continuous basis by road haulage companies;
road transport managers.
Requirements for road transport professionals
EU countries must ensure that:
professional qualifications are obtained by:
passing a written examination;
participating in the organisation and organisation of a driving test;
taking part in a training course;
being familiar with the various forms of credit (bank credit, documentary credit, guarantee deposits, mortgages, leasing, renting, factoring, etc.) in accordance with the needs of the undertaking;
drawing up periodic maintenance plans for the vehicles and their equipment;
developing and implementing a periodic maintenance plan for road haulages.
Drivers must have a valid driving licence and be able to drive a vehicle with at least one of the following characteristics:
a driving licence;
a vehicle with a minimum weighting of marks of 40% and a maximum of 60% of the total number of marks to be given.
the driving licence must be valid for at least 3 years.
The driving licence is valid for 3 years and must be accompanied by a certificate of fitness. The driving licence may be suspended if the driving licence holder is found to be unfit for the job.
Road Transport Manager
The road transport manager must be a person with a driving licence valid for a minimum of 3 years, and must have the following qualifications:
an adequate knowledge of vehicle maintenance management, verification of transport contracts and documents, basic accounting, the assignment of tasks effective, continuous and continuous by that person, and indicates his or her responsibilities as transport manager.
An undertaking must designate a transport manager or managers to carry out the tasks specified in the regulation. The tasks to be specified must comprise, in particular, those relating to vehicle maintenance and vehicle accounting, verification and verification of the transport contract and documents and basic accounting.
A person designated as the transport manager is responsible for:
managing the organisation of the road transport company;
organising and implementing road transport operations;
planning and executing road transport activities;
providing road transport services;
ensuring the safety of road users;
carrying out road safety inspections.
Exemptions
The European Commission may, on the basis of a proposal from the European Parliament and the Council, exclude from the scope of the regulation undertakings which have not been authorised to engage in the occupational occupation of transport operator. The Commission may also exclude undertakings that have not fulfilled the requirements laid down in Article 3.
Directive 96/26/EC should be repealed and replaced by Directive (EU) 2019/852.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 4 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s road transport policy aims to improve the efficiency of the market and the quality of service provided by road transport companies. It also aims to reduce road transport costs and improve road safety.
For more information, see:
‘Road transport operator’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 145:
Beneficiaries’ access to social security — rules on eligibility and recovery
Benefics’ application for social security – rules on identity and recoverability
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 883/2004 on the identification and recovery of social security benefits
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that beneficiaries of the European Union (EU) social security schemes are able to access their rights as quickly as possible and under optimum conditions.
It sets out rules for the identification, recovery and verification of benefits.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU citizens who are covered by the social security scheme. It does not apply to those who have left the EU or who have moved to a non-EU country.
EU countries’ social security institutions must:
identify and verify the identity of the insured person;
establish a liaison body to deal with claims;
provide the person with advance payments;
ensure that the person who has left the country has the right to appeal against their rejection.
Where an insured person does not reside in the competent EU country, he must request authorisation from the institution of his place of residence, which forwards it to the competent institution without delay.
The institution of the person’s place of origin must inform the institution and employment services of the EU country in which he or she lives.
If an unemployed person decides, in accordance with Article 65(2) of the basic regulation, to make him/herself also available to the employment services in the Member State where he pursued his last activity as an employed or self-employed person by registering there as a person seeking work, he shall inform them of this decision and inform them, as well as the institution, employment services and social security services of that Member State, of the place where he/she intends to pursue his/her last activity.
Employers’ obligations
The employer must ensure that:
the insured person has a valid social security number;
the person who is claiming benefits has been employed for at least 6 months;
there is no discrimination between employers and insured persons;
employers are required to provide information on the benefits they provide to the person claiming benefits, including the nature of the activities carried out.
In addition, the employer must provide information about the persons who are entitled to supplementary pension rights. This information must be kept confidential.
Exchange of information between institutions and advance payments
In the event of a dispute between an institution and a claimant, it must be possible to:
exchange information between the two institutions;
make a joint decision to accept or reject the claim; or
make an advance payment to the claimant.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Social security’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 146:
Statistical system for occupational safety and health at work (SUMMARY OF:
Decision No 1350/2007/EC establishing a European statistical system in the area of occupational safety at work
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) statistical system to collect and disseminate information on the health and safety of workers at work.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The aim of the decision is to establish a European Statistical System (ESS) to collect, disseminate and analyse data on the state of the health of workers in the EU.
It aims to:
improve the quality of health information and knowledge in the field of work;
ensure the comparability and comparability of national statistical systems;
contribute to the development of a sustainable European health monitoring system;
provide comparable data and information, with appropriate indicators, and to develop, with the Community Statistical Programme, a statistical element of this system.
Objectives
The decision sets out the objectives of the ESS, including:
to improve the quality and reliability of the data collected;
to contribute to the sustainable development of the EU’s statistical system; and
to develop, in particular, a system of comparable, comparable and comparable health monitoring systems.
The ESS will be based on the results of national surveys and the results from the European Monitoring System for Occupational Safety and Health at Work (EMSH).
It will also include:
the results of pilot studies and cost-benefit analyses;
cost-benefit analysis;
analysis of the cost-effectiveness of implementing the ESHSWaste management;
the analysis of the costs and benefits of implementing ESSH;
data collection and dissemination of the results;
measures to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the system of monitoring and analysis.
Implementation
EU countries’ national authorities must ensure that:
data are collected and disseminated in accordance with the requirements laid down in Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the statistical system of the European Union;
national authorities must submit data on:
work-related health problems and illnesses;
social and psychosocial health problems
health-related work-related illnesses.
Data collection
The data collected must be collected in the form of surveys, surveys and analyses.
National authorities must:
use the methods and methods laid down by the European Commission for the collection of data;
use statistical methods and analyses to assess the quality, relevance and relevance of the information they collect;
take account of the different national systems and avoid imposing additional administrative burdens.
Quality of health care
The quality of healthcare must also be considered in the data collection.
In addition, EU countries must take into account:
quality of health and social care;
access to and use of health services;
availability of information on health-related diseases and conditions;
quality and availability of preventive and curative measures;
preventing and treating diseases;
health and social determinants of health;
information on the impact of social exclusion and social exclusion on health and well-being of workers.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
From 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a second programme of Community action in health (2008-2013) which aims to develop a sustainable, reliable and comparable European system of health monitoring and information.
For more information, see:
‘Sustainable European system for monitoring and disseminating health’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Occupational safety: any health problem or illness which can be caused, worsened or jointly caused by working conditions.
* Social determinants: any social determinant of health.

Summary 147:
Statistical indicators for job vacancies
Statistics for job vacancy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2007 on the statistical indicators for employment vacancies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a legal basis for EU-wide employment statistics.
It aims to improve the quality of the EU’s employment statistics and to monitor the European Employment Strategy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It covers the period from 1 January 2007 to 31 December 2020.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on:
the number of vacancies in their labour market;
the average number of jobs created and vacancies in the labour market over that period;
total employment in the EU as a whole.
The Commission must transmit the data to Eurostat within 3 months of the end of the quarter for which the data are to be provided. The data must be sent to the Commission on the first data delivery of the first quarter of the following year.
Eurostat must send the Commission a report on the quality and quantity of the data it receives every 2 years. The report must include a description of the methodology used to compile the data and the results of the surveys it has carried out. It must also include a summary of the findings of the feasibility studies carried out on the implementation of the regulation.
Each year, the Commission must send Eurostat a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on Employment and Social Policy Committee on the basis of the results it has received.
. The Commission must then present its conclusions to the Employment Committee.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
The Employment Committee established by Council Decision 2000/98/EC, which was endorsed by the Council on 29 September 2000, and subsequent progress reports on the development of the legal basis covering job vacancy statistics, identified as a priority the need for an indicator to monitor EU employment.
For more information, see:
‘EU employment statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Job vacancy: a paid post that is newly created, unoccupied, or about to become vacant:
for which the employer is taking active steps and is prepared to take further steps to find a suitable candidate from outside the enterprise concerned;
which the employer intends to fill either immediately or within a specific period of time.

Summary 148:
EU type-approval of hydrogen containers designed to use liquid hydrogen (2008/43/EC)
EU type–approval requirements for hydrogen containers for motor vehicles
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 — type–applicable test procedures for type–conformity requirements for the type–conditions for the use of liquid hydrogen
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the application of EU type‑approval procedures for the design, manufacture, testing and certification of type–consumption requirements for certain hydrogen containers.
It aims to facilitate the introduction of hydrogen-powered vehicles in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
hydrogen mixtures* designed for use as a transition fuel;
hybrid vehicles* designed to make use of pure hydrogen;
vehicles designed to operate on natural gas infrastructure;
transport vehicles designed to run on hydrogen.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
they have carried out the necessary tests to ensure that their products comply with the regulation’s requirements;
they carry out the required conformity assessment procedures;
the test procedures are carried out by the Technical Committee — Motor Vehicles (TCMV) established by Article 40(1) of Directive 2007/46/EC.
They must also ensure that the vehicles meet the requirements for:
the safety of the environment;
environmental considerations;
safety of the vehicle;
availability of the hydrogen storage system.
the availability of hydrogen mixtures for use;
inform the European Commission of the results of the tests carried out.
The manufacturer must also inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions and the European Transport Safety Agency of the conditions under which they can apply the regulation.
Conditions for acceptance
The regulations apply to the following a number of conditions:
manufacturers must:
make sure that the vehicle complies with the requirements laid down in Annex I of the regulation;
keep a record of the test results;
ensure that the manufacturer has carried out a conformity assessment procedure;
have carried out conformity assessment tests on their products;
check that their vehicles comply with all the requirements set out in Annex II.
If a vehicle does not comply with these conditions, the manufacturer must inform the competent authority of the EU country in which it is registered.
EU countries must also take the necessary precautionary measures to prevent the possibility of hydrogen leakage during refilling and to make sure that any removal of removable hydrogen storage systems is done safely.
In addition, manufacturers must ensure:
that the vehicle meets the requirements of Annex III to the regulation, including the requirements on the type of test;
that their products are:
safe and environmentally sound;
competent;
compliant with the relevant EU legislation;
not likely to endanger human health or safety;
safe for the environment and the environment in general;
designed to operate in a manner that minimises the risk of accidents.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
It applies from 1 July 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Hybrid vehicles’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work’ website.
* KEY TERMS
* Hydrogen: a mixture of natural gas and hydrogen that can be used as a transport fuel. It is a natural gas-based fuel that has the same energy density as electricity. It can also be used for hydrogen storage and transport.

Summary 150:
Judicial cooperation in civil matters between EU countries and non-EU countries (Civil Justice Regulation (CivilJustice Regulation) (PDF)
Judicial Cooperation in Civil Matters Between EU States and Non-EU Countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on judicial cooperation between Member States and third countries on matters of civil justice
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the legal framework for judicial cooperation* between the European Union (EU) and its Member States in the field of civil law.
It sets out the conditions for the conclusion of new agreements between the EU and a third country on matters relating to civil justice.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the areas covered by the EU’s acquis (Article 307 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU) and Article 5(2) of the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY);
the fields covered by Title IV of Part Three of the European Convention on the Law of the Member States (Title IV of Title III of Part 3 of the TFEU); and
the matters covered by Article 5 of the United Nations Convention against Transnational Organized Crime (Article 5(1) of Part 4 of the ICCTY).
It does not apply to the areas of criminal law, which are covered by Articles 1, 2, 3, 4 and 6 of the UN Convention on Criminal Justice.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCELEGAL FORCE
It entered into force
It has applied since 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
The EU countries’ legal systems are governed by agreements governed by international treaties and international agreements.
For more information, see:
‘Civil Justice’ on the European Commission's website.
In order to provide an adequate legal framework to meet specific needs of a given Member State in its relations with a third party, there may also be a manifest need for new agreements with third countries relating to areas of the civil justice that come within the purview of Title IV. This may involve the need for the re-negotiation of such agreements. This regulation aims to ensure that these agreements are concluded in line with this need.
The European Commission must be kept fully informed throughout the different stages of the negotiations as far as matters falling within the scope of this regulation are concerned and should be allowed to participate as an observer in the negotiations. It may propose negotiating guidelines or request the inclusion of particular clauses, as referred to in the second subparagraph of Article 5.
Notification to the Commission
Member States must notify the Commission of ongoing or already announced negotiations with a view to obtaining an authorisation to open formal negotiations. The notification must include, as appropriate, a copy of the existing agreement, the draft agreement or the draft proposal, and any other relevant documentation. The Member State may provide any other additional information.
If the Commission confirms the temporary nature of these negotiations, the Member State must submit a report to the European Parliament and to the Council on the results of the discussions within 30 days of the closure of these discussions. The Commission must then give a reasoned decision on the application of the decision.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition into force in Member States
Official Journal
Council Regulation (EC).
1.7.2009
-
OJ L 127 of 7.7, 2009

Summary 151:
EU statistics on migration: data collection and dissemination (2007-2008)
EU data on migration statistics: 2007-2008
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 311/76 on Community Statistics on Migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and analysis of Community Statistics in the field of migration.
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ statistics on the number of non-EU nationals entering and leaving the EU are collected and disseminated to the European Commission (Eurostat).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
third-country nationals refused entry to the EU country’s territory at the external border or illegally present in its territory under national laws relating to immigration.
EU countries must provide Eurostat with statistics on:
non-EU citizens refused entry into the EU;
third country nationals found to be illegally present within their territory under the national laws concerning immigration. These statistics must relate to reference periods of one calendar year and must be supplied to Eurostat within 3 months of the end of the reference year. They must be disaggregated by age and sex, and by the citizenship of the persons concerned.
Statistics on residence permits and residence of third-country nationalities
The EU countries must supply Eurostat data on the numbers of residence permits issued to non-European Union (EU) citizens. These data relate to the residence permits granted to third country nationals and the residence of those who are not nationals of the EU.
The data must be provided within three months of their expiry date of expiry.
Eurostatistical confidentiality
The European Commission must ensure the confidentiality of the data collected by the EU countries and Eurostat. The data may be used only for the purposes of statistical analysis.
Data on non-nationalities
EuroStat must collect statistics on non–EU nationals who are:
refused entry to EU countries;
illegal present in the country of origin;
unlawfully present in an EU country under national law relating to migration;
remaining in the EU illegally.
These statistics must be collected within the 3 months following the expiry of a residence permit or residence permit.
Non-EU countries may provide data on non nationals who:
have not been granted residence permits or residence permits;
are not citizens of an EU Member State;
have entered the country illegally;
do not have the right to stay in the Member State.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
EU migration statistics (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 310/76 of 21 December 2006 on Community statistics on Migration (OJ L 183, 31.12.2006, pp. 1-8)

Statutory audits of public interest entities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on statutory audits of statutory entities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that statutory audit* is carried out in the public interest.
It sets out the responsibilities of statutory auditors and audit firms.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
statutory auditors* carrying out statutory audits;
auditing firms carrying out such audits; and
public-interest organisations.
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts to align the regulation to the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union and, in particular, to Article 290 and 291 of Directive 2006/43/EC (see summary).
It does not apply to the provision of non-audit services (i.e. audit services that are not statutory audits) or to audit firms that do not perform statutory audits.
EU countries’ national authorities must designate competent authorities to carry out the statutory audit tasks of statutory audit. The competent authorities are appointed by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC).
The CEAOB, the European Committee of Auditors and the Committee of the Audit Bodies (CEAOB), is the body responsible for appointing the competent authorities. It is composed of representatives of national audit firms and statutory audit firms from the EU countries. It meets at least twice a year. The European Parliament and the Council of Europe have the power to approve delegated acts adopted by the Commission on the basis of a proposal from the European Commission. The Commission adopts delegated acts.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
The statutory audit is a key element of the EU’s financial transparency policy. It adds credibility to published financial information and provides better protection for shareholders, investors, creditors and other interested parties.

assessing the impact of the measures.
EU countries’ fisheries policies on marine biodiversity.

Summary 154:
Fishery and aquaculture products — common organisation of markets
Fisheries and aquatic products — Common organisation of the markets
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 854/2004 establishing a common market for fishery products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common framework for the organisation of European Union (EU) Member States’ markets in fishery* products.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for the establishment of fishery producer organisations (FPOs).
It aims to:
facilitate the development of a single market for products intended for human consumption;
improve the transparency of the EU’s fisheries sector;
promote the sustainable management of fisheries;
ensure a high level of protection of the marine environment.
It does not apply to products of animal origin.
Fishers’ organisations
FMOs must:
be based on a production and marketing plan;
have a set of objectives and measures to achieve these objectives;
meet the requirements of the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA);
have an annual report to the European Commission on their activities and submit it to the competent national authorities for approval.
Member States must ensure that FPOs have the necessary financial resources to meet these objectives.
They must also ensure that their activities are coordinated with those of the other Member States and that they cooperate with the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA).
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
Aquaculture producer organisations
Each Member State must set up a producer organisation (PIO) which:
is responsible for:
establishing and managing the fishery sector and its markets;
providing information to the Commission on its activities;
preparing and disseminating information on the production, marketing and distribution of aquacultural products;
coordinating the activities of its members;
organising the activities and activities of the Member States' competent authorities;
contributing to the development and management of the fisheries sector.
The Member States must also:
encourage the participation of the private sector in the activities that contribute to achieving the objectives of the CFP;
take steps to ensure that the PIOs are represented in the decision-making process of the Commission;
support the work of the EFSA, the European Fisheries and Aquaculture Authority and other relevant bodies;
cooperate with the Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee (EASC) on the coordination of fisheries sector policies, programmes and measures;
exchange information on fishery fishery production and market information;
participate in the establishment, management and operation of the common market in fisheries, as well as in the development, management, organisation and dissemination of information on products intended to be sold on the market.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2005. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:

KEY TERMS
* Fishery products: products of fish, crustaceans and shellfish, such as oysters, mussels, clams and clams.

Summary 155:
Securities settlement systems (CSDs) — rules on their technical standards and user rights
Securities settlements systems (SSS) — systems on their technological standards and users rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1095/2010 on the technical standards for securities settlement systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the securities settlement system (CSD) operating in the EU has the same technical standards as other financial market infrastructures.
It sets out the rules for the development and operation of these systems.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all CSDs operating securities markets. It does not apply to:
non-EU countries’ national securities markets;
the European Securities and Markets Authority’s (ESMA) national securities market infrastructure;
other market infrastructure.
Requirements for CSDs
EU countries must:
ensure that:
the CSD has the necessary technical standards to meet the requirements laid down in the regulation;
have a user committee to ensure the proper functioning of the system;
establish and maintain a code of conduct for the user committee.
The user committee is composed of representatives of CSDs and other market infrastructure users. It is chaired by an ESMA representative. It has the power to adopt technical standards. It may also adopt delegated acts.
Rules on user committees
A user committee must be composed of at least one representative from each EU country. It must be chaired by a representative of a CSD and have at least two members from the EU. It can:
representatives of the EU countries;
represent the interests of the CSD;
meet at least once a year.
the user committee may decide on a number of issues, such as:
rules on the organisation of user committees;
membership of users;
rules governing the use of user committee members’ voting rights.
Disclosure of conflicts of interest
Member CSDs must disclose to the European Commission the names of any members of their user committee who have an actual or a potential conflict of interest in relation to a particular matter. This information must be made available to the Commission and to the public. The Commission must then inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee on Payments and Settlement Systems (CPSS) of the Bank of International Settlements of the International Organisation of Securities Commissions (IOSCO).
The Commission must ensure that CSDs comply with the rules laid down by the user committees.
Exemptions
A CSD may withdraw from the regulation if it:
does not fulfil the requirements of the regulation,
has not complied with the requirements set out in its technical standards;
has failed to comply with its obligations under the regulation.
A decision to withdraw from a regulation may be made by the Commission or by the European Banking Authority.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve the functioning of EU financial markets.
For more information, see:
‘Securities Settlement Systems’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Securities settlement system: a financial market infrastructure that provides the settlement of securities.
* Financial market infrastructure: a system of financial markets in which securities are traded on a regulated market.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1024/2010 of 21 December 2010 on the regulatory technical standards applicable to securities settlements systems and implementing Regulations (EC) No 552/2009, (EC), (EU), (Euratom), and (EU).
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

Summary 157:
European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) — the outermost regions (Northernmost regions)
European Regional development Fund (Northwestern regions) (European Union)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the EU’s regional development fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules governing the EU's European Regional Development fund.
It aims to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the regions of the north-eastern European Union (EEU) which are excluded from the scope of the European Social Fund (ESF), the Cohesion Fund, the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development and the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMF);
the easternmost regions, such as Finland, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland, Norway and Sweden;
the north-western regions of Norway, Sweden, Finland and Switzerland, as well as the Baltic countries, Iceland and Switzerland.
Funding
The ERDF is funded by the European Union's general budget. It has a budget of €2.5 billion.
The funding is divided into 4 main categories:
1.1 billion for the northern regions;
2.
3.8 billion for each of the southern regions; and
4 billion for all other regions, including the Baltic States.
4.7 billion for Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, Hungary, Poland and Sweden.
5.3 billion for Sweden and Denmark.
6.7 trillion for the eastern regions and the south-western ones.
7.8 trillion for each other.
8.5 trillion for Denmark and Iceland.
9.8% for the outerest regions. This is to be used to offset the additional costs, linked to the special characteristics and constraints referred to in Article 349 TFEU, incurred in supporting:
—
the thematic objectives set out in the first paragraph of Article 9 of the first regulation;
freight transport services and start-up aid for transport services;
operations linked to storage constraints, the excessive size and maintenance of production tools, and the lack of human capital in the local market.
10.9 billion for rural development.
11.9 trillion for maritime and fisheries.
12.8tn for inland water resources.
13.9tn for the western part of the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘The European Regional development fund’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EC) No 1301/2013 of 21 December 2013 establishing the European RegionalDevelopment Fund (European External Action Service).
