Summary 158:
Interregional cooperation programmes — European Regional Development Fund (ERDF)
Interregual cooperation programmes – European Regional development Fund (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated acts on the EU’s Interregional Development Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DELEGATED ACTS?
The delegated acts set out the rules for implementing the EU's interregional development fund.
They aim to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ERDF is the main instrument for the development of regional cooperation in the European Union (EU). It is funded by the EU budget for the period 2014-2020.
The EU budget is divided into 4 main categories:
technical assistance;
research and innovation;
economic and social development;
agriculture;
environmental and rural development.
Programmes
Each programme must be designed and implemented in accordance with the specific character of the specific programme.
Cooperation programmes must:
contribute to:
improving the quality of life of the most disadvantaged regions;
promoting economic and social cohesion in rural areas;
developing rural areas and areas of industrial transition;
contributing to the sustainable development of mountain regions and islands;
providing technical assistance to the most in need;
encouraging the growth and diversification of economic activities in mountain regions, coastal regions and coastal regions, as well-integrated regions, particularly in the south-eastern part of the EU.
Specific priorities
The specific priorities for each programme are set out in Annex II of the delegated acts.
EU countries and third countries or territories must ensure that:
the programmes are implemented in line with the priorities set out by the managing authorities of the participating countries and territories;
there are specific investment priorities to reflect their specific activities;
programmes are adapted to the specific needs of the programme;
projects are designed and executed according to the needs of each participating country or territory.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES APPLY?
Directive 2014/20/EU has applied since 22 April 2014.
Delegate acts
The following delegated acts have applied since 21 April 2014:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1303/2013 on the European regional development fund
Deployment of the European territorial cooperation fund
Council Delegated Act (EU), Euratom and Euratom Implementing Decision 2014/22/EU
Decision 2014/23/EU on the implementation of the Interregualtion of Regulation (EU/Euratom).
BACKGROUND
The European territorial development fund was established under Article 174 TFEU (see summary). It was established to support the efforts of the Member States and the EU countries to develop the regions of the Union.
For more information, see:
European territorial cooperation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Regional development fund: a European Union funding instrument.

Summary 159:
Projects of common interest: EU-wide environmental protection — common rules for energy projects (Regulation (EC) No 714/2009)
Projects OF COMMON INTEREST: European Union (EU) 2020-2020 - common rules on environmental protection
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 — common EU rules on the environmental protection of energy projects
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for environmental protection* of energy-related projects in the EU.
It aims to promote the sustainable development of the EU’s energy sector.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
energy-related energy projects, such as:
transport infrastructure;
agriculture and forestry;
aquaculture and aquaculture;
municipal water resources;
industrial parks.
The rules apply to all energy projects that are eligible for EU funding, including:
electricity projects;
railways;
biomagnetic energy projects.
EU countries’ national authorities must:
ensure that projects are designed and built in a way that:
is environmentally sound;
is compatible with EU rules and regulations;
does not threaten the environment or public health;
has a high environmental impact;
provides a high degree of environmental protection.
They must also ensure that projects comply with:
environmental objectives;
have a high level of protection for the environment and human health and the environment;
are compatible with the objectives of the European Pillar of Social Innovation (PSI) and the European Green Deal;
contribute to the creation of jobs and growth of the labour market;
promote sustainable growth and jobs;
encourage investment in energy-intensive sectors such as transport, agriculture and forestry.
Regional lists
The European Commission must draw up regional lists of projects that meet the criteria set out in the regulation. These regional lists must be submitted to the European Environment Agency (EEA) for approval by the EU countries concerned. These lists must include:
a timetable for each of the following:
feasibility and design studies;
approval by the national regulatory authority or by any other authority concerned;
construction and commissioning;
the permit granting schedule;
cost allocation decision.
Evaluation
The EEA assesses the projects’ environmental impact on the environment, public health and social aspects of the economy. It then publishes a report on the results of its evaluation of the projects. This report is sent to the Commission, which then forwards it to the national authorities. The Commission then forwards the report to the EEA for final approval.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to promote energy efficiency and sustainable growth. These include the EU 2020 strategy, which aims to create a more resource-efficient, more sustainable and more competitive economy.
For more information, see:
EU energy strategy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Energy-related project: a project that aims to improve the efficiency and environmental performance of the energy sector by:
improving energy efficiency;
increasing energy efficiency in the transport sector;
enhancing the environmental impact of energy infrastructure.
* Environmental protection: the protection of the environment.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU), Euratom — Common EU rules for the environmentally sound development of energy resources (Codified version) (OJ L 136, 1.7.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 160:
Dispute resolution mechanism: online dispute resolution (ODR) for consumer disputes
Disputes between consumers and sellers
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/865 on online dispute settlement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the resolution of disputes between consumers* and sellers*.
It sets up a dispute resolution mechanism for disputes over the sale of goods and services to consumers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all disputes concerning the sale or supply of goods or services to a consumer.
It does not apply to:
the sale of products and services directly or indirectly to consumers;
the supply or sale of services directly to consumers or to persons acting outside their trade, business, craft or profession.
EU countries’ implementing powers
The European Commission has the power to adopt implementing powers. Those powers are exercised in accordance with the rules and general principles concerning mechanisms for control by Member States of the Commission’s exercise of implementing powers in the field of consumer protection.
The Commission must ensure that the regulation is implemented in line with the principles of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union (CFR) and that it does not infringe on the rights of consumers. It must also take account of the fact that EU countries have different rules on the interpretation and translation of EU law.
Rules and principles concerning the ODR platform
The EU countries must:
ensure that the platform for resolving disputes is open to all interested parties;
provide an electronic translation function for all disputes;
as soon as possible;
set up to the end of the dispute;
establish a network of ODR contact points to facilitate the exchange of information between the parties.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
Directive 2014/33/EU on consumer protection (see summary) sets out rules and principles for the implementation of the CFR.
For more information, see:
‘ADR’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Dispute Resolution mechanism: a dispute settlement procedure in which a dispute between a consumer and a seller is settled by means of an online platform.

Summary 161:
Reform of the European Union’s Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EUAFRICOM Regulation (EUFROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
It applies from 1 January 2014.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) No 582/2013.
ACT
Commission Regulation (CFSP) 2015/856 of 20 December 2015 on the European Community’re Agricultural Fund Regulation (CAP).
SUMMARY
The European Union (EU) is a multiannual financial instrument for the production and distribution of agricultural products and services. It aims to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the agricultural sector and to promote the sustainable development of rural areas.
The regulation repeals and repeals Council Regulation (EC) no 581/2013, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2016.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The Regulation applies to the following sectors and productions:
crop production: cereals, oilseeds, protein crops, grain legumes, flax, hemp, rice, nuts, starch potato, milk and milk products, seeds, sheepmeat and goatmeat, beef and veal, olive oil, silkworms, dried fodder, hops, sugar beet, cane and chicory, fruit and vegetables and short rotation coppice.
Rural areas:
seeds and cereal crops;
grain legumes;
pesticides;
nuts and rice;
nutritional protein crops.
Sustainable production:
rice, rice and maize;
milk and milk products;
sugar beet;
livestock and beef;
dairy milk products.
Eligible hectares:
1.
2.5 million hectares of land are eligible for the basic payment scheme;
3 million hectares for the single-area payment scheme.
4.6 million hectares are eligible in the single area payment scheme for the agriculture sector.
5.8 million hectares (1.5 %) of land is eligible for combined payments.
EU countries must adopt implementing acts fixing the corresponding ceilings for the redistributive payment on a yearly basis. These ceilings are set out in implementing acts adopted by the European Commission. The Commission adopts implementing acts setting these ceilings on the basis of the percentage of the national ceiling to be used by EU countries pursuant to this regulation.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
a minimum level of support is provided to each sector and production;
a maximum level of combined payments is provided for each production sector and each production area;
the amount of the combined payments for each sector is calculated on a per hectare basis;
each year, each EU country must publish a list of the sectors and production sectors that are eligible and the total amount of payments to be made.

It has applied since 31 December 2015.

Summary 162:
EU support for the smaller Aegean islands
EU support to the smaller islands
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2011 — support measures for the development of small and medium-sized islands in the eastern Mediterranean
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the conditions for the supply of agricultural products to smaller and coastal islands of the eastern and central Mediterranean (Aegean) in order to contribute to the sustainable development of these islands.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to specific supply arrangements for the production, processing and marketing of certain agricultural products.
It does not apply to:
the general supply of food and agricultural products;
the supply of goods and services to the EU’s external borders;
supply of services and goods to non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts, in accordance with Article 21 of the regulation, determining conditions for recording operators in the register and providing for the full exercise by operators of their rights to participate in the specific supply arrangement.
EU countries must ensure that:
all EU countries are informed of the conditions under which they can participate in these specific arrangements;
all relevant information is made available to the Commission, including the names, addresses and contact details of the operators;
each EU country is given the opportunity to submit a proposal for participation in the programme.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that the Commission adopts a list of the names and addresses of operators participating in the supply arrangements.
identify the operators who have been granted the right to take part in these arrangements and the conditions governing their participation in those arrangements
provide the Commission with information on the operators’ rights and obligations.
Each EU country must ensure the proper functioning of the register of operators in its territory. The Commission must also ensure that the information it receives is accurate and up-to-date.
Operators’ obligations
The Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the operation of the specific arrangements
ensuring that the operators comply with the conditions laid down in the regulation;
assisting the Commission in the implementation of the measures;
providing information to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the implementation and functioning of these specific supply agreements.

Summary 163:
Statistical results on the carriage of goods by road
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the dissemination of statistical results in respect of the carriage by road (recast)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on the distribution of statistics on the movement of goods and their movements by road in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the carriage of products by road;
the movement of persons, goods and capital by road.
It covers the first quarter of the first year after the entry into force of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (1 January 1999).
It does not apply to goods moving between EU countries.
EU countries’ responsibilities
The European Commission has the power to adopt implementing acts to disseminate statistical results to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) on the basis of the results it receives from the European Statistical System (ESS).
The Commission’s powers include:
collecting, analysing and disseminating statistical results;
monitoring and analysing the results, including the structure and content;
assessing the quality of the statistical results.
Reporting and dissemination of results
Each EU country must submit a report to the Commission every 2 years on the transmission of results. The Commission then forwards the results to other EU countries and the EEC.
The results are transmitted to the ESIEC, the EEAC and the Commission.
the Commission then publishes the results in the Official Journal of the European Statistics.

Summary 164:
Transmission and service of judicial and extrajudicial documents in civil or commercial matters: EU rules on judicial and commercial services in civil and commercial matters (Regulation (EC) No 1348/2000)
Transmission, delivery and delivery of judicial or commercial documents
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2006/852/EC on the service in the Member States of judicial documents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes rules for the transmission, delivery, and delivery in the European Union (EU) of judicial, extrajudicial and commercial documents.
It aims to improve judicial cooperation between the EU countries’ authorities in the field of judicial matters.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
judicial documents
extrajudicial documents (i.e. documents issued by a court in another EU country to a person who has been convicted of a criminal offence).
It does not apply to the following types of documents:
transmission of documents to a third party (e.g. a court of an EU country).
The law of the EU country in which the document is to be served is the same as the law of that country.
The sending agency must serve the document or have it served, either by itself or by a particular method requested by the transmitting agency, unless that method is incompatible with the law in the country of origin of the document. The sending agency may also refuse to serve a document if the addressee has exercised his or her right of refusal.
In additionressee’s’ rights of refusal
the sending agency has the right to refuse the service of a judicial or extrajudicial document. This rule applies to the subsequent service once a person has exercised this right.
EU countries must designate a central point of contact between the sending agency and the receiving agency. The central point is designated by the European Commission. The designation is valid for a period of 5 years and may be renewed at five-year intervals.
Each EU country must designate one or more central points of contact with the sending and receiving agencies. The Central Point of Contact must be located in the territory of the country where the document was to be sent or received.
A central point must be established in each EU country for each type of judicial document to which it is to send or receive. It must also be established for every type of extrajudicial or commercial document which has been issued by the court in that country to the person to whom it has been delivered.
FROM WHEN DOES THE CONVENTION APPLY?
The convention on judicial cooperation in civil matters has not entered into force. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2006.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the regulation.
For more information, see:
EU judicial cooperation (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Judicial cooperation: the cooperation between EU countries in the area of judicial cooperation.
* Extrajudicial document: a document issued by an EU court to an individual who is a defendant in a criminal case.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2006/884/EC of 30 November 2006 on the regulation on the transmission and service in Member States concerning judicial documents (OJ L 136, 30.11.2006, pp. 1-4)

Summary 165:
Combined advanced therapy medicinal products — EU rules on their marketing and labelling (European Commission Regulation (EC) No 726/2004)
Combined-advanced-therapy medicinal products – European Union rules on its marketing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 768/2008 on the marketing of combined advanced therapies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules governing the marketing, labelling and labeling of combined therapies* intended for the treatment of diseases and dysfunctions of the human body.
It aims to encourage the development of new and innovative medicines to improve the quality of life of patients.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
medicines intended for use in combination with other medicines;
combined advanced therapies intended for specific diseases and disorders;
therapeutic devices* intended to be used as part of a combined advanced therapy;
medical devices designed to be placed on the market for the use of a combination of advanced therapies.
EU countries’ national authorities must ensure that:
the products comply with the essential requirements laid down in Annex I to Directive 93/42/EEC (see summary);
they comply with Annex II to Directive 92/36/EC on the labelling of medicines intended for human use;
they are accompanied by a declaration of conformity assessment.
The European Commission must publish a list of the products covered by the regulation.
In addition, the Commission must take the necessary measures to ensure that these products are available to the public.
to be marketed in the European Union (EU).
The Commission must:
ensure that the products are marketed in accordance with the EU’s general rules on medical devices;
monitor the quality and safety of these products;
take the necessary steps to ensure the safety and efficacy of the combined products.
Each EU country must ensure the following measures:
that the combined medicinal product meets all essential requirements;
that it complies with the quality assurance requirements laid out in Annex II of Directive 93.
If the Commission considers that a product does not comply with these essential requirements, it must inform the Committee for Advanced Therapies of the reasons for its exclusion from the scope of this regulation. The Commission must also inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
Combining advanced therapies are one of the most promising areas of biomedicine, with the potential to transform the way we live, work and live in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Combined therapies’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Combined advanced therapy: a medical device designed to combine two or more medicines. It may also contain additional substances, such as cellular products, bio-molecules, bi-materials, chemical substances, scaffolds or matrices.

Summary 166:
Emissions and fuel consumption of motor vehicles: type-approval requirements and emission limit values (2007-2013)
Emissions & fuel consumption requirements and emissions limit values
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 652/2007 — type-approval requirements for motor vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out EU-wide rules on the type approval requirements for vehicles and their trailers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to motor vehicles, their trailers and light commercial vehicles. It does not apply to:
passenger cars;
cargo vehicles;
trucks;
motor coaches;
bicyclers;
light commercial vehicles; and
light goods vehicles.
It applies to all vehicles registered in the European Union (EU).
EU countries must ensure that the vehicles comply with the regulation’s requirements.
Manufacturers must:
ensure that the vehicle complies with the regulations;
make available to the public and authorised dealers and repairers the technical specifications and information on their vehicles’ emission control systems;
provide information on fuel consumption and carbon dioxide emissions to the European Commission and the European Agency for Harmonised Harmonised Instrumentation for Standardisation of Motor Vehicles (Harmonisation of Vehicle Emissions and Fuel Control Systems (Ecodesign).
The European Commission must also:
monitor the fuel consumption* and CO2 emissions* of vehicles and trailers;
assess the fuel efficiency* of the vehicles concerned;
inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions and the Committee on the Functioning of the Internal Market, and the Commission on the harmonisation of vehicle emissions and fuel efficiency.
The Commission must publish a report on the results of these tests every 5 years. It must also publish a list of vehicles that have not complied with this regulation and its implementing measures.
National authorities may not, on grounds relating to emissions or fuel consumption, refuse to grant EC type approval or national type approval for a new type of vehicle, or prohibit the registration, sale or entry into service of a new vehicle, where the vehicle concerned complies:
with the Euro 5 limit values set out in Table 1 of Annex I or with the Euro 6 limit values in Table 2 of Annex I.
If a manufacturer so requests, the national authorities may:
prohibit the registration or sale of a vehicle where it does not comply with these rules; or
ban the registration of a motor vehicle if it is found to be in breach of these rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 2 July 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the efficiency of its motor vehicle market. These include:
the harmonised method of measuring fuel consumption (Euro 5) and the carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions (Emissions/Access to Information System (EASIS);
rules on fuel efficiency (Euro 6).
For more information, see:
‘Emissions’ on the EC website.
* KEY TERMS
* Motor vehicle: a vehicle designed and built to carry passengers, trailers and heavy goods vehicles, such as trucks, buses and coaches.

Summary 167:
EU common procedure for the assessment and authorisation of substances in foodstuffs and food categories [Official Journal C 168 of 20.11.2008]
EU common process for the application of substances
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 178/2002 establishing a common EU procedure for assessing and authorising substances in the food and feed sectors
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common European Union (EU) procedure to assess, authorise and monitor the safe use of certain substances* in food and food products.
It aims to ensure the free movement of these substances within the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following categories of substances:
food and feedstuffs (e.g. meat, poultry, fish, eggs, dairy products, meat-processing products, eggs and milk);
animal feed (eggs, milk, poultry and eggs);
foods and feed (meat, milk and eggs).
Risk assessment
The European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) carries out a risk assessment of a substance’s safety. This assessment, which must be carried out under the responsibility of the EFSA, must be followed by a risk management decision taken by the European Commission under a regulatory procedure that ensures close cooperation between the Commission and the EU countries.
The Commission then adopts a decision to authorise or prohibit the use of the substance. The decision must be published in the Official Journal of the European Union.
EU countries’ authorities must ensure that the decision is publicly available to the public and that the substance has been authorised.
In addition, the Commission must publish a list of substances that have been authorised to be used on the EU market. This list is updated by the Commission every 3 years.
Authorisation procedure
The EU countries must notify the European Authority of the substances on the list within 3 months of receiving a request for authorisation. The Commission decides after consulting with the applicants which information can remain confidential and must notify applicants and the Member States accordingly.
Applicants must provide the Commission with the following information:
the name and address of the applicant;
the product name and a clear description of the product.
If the Commission considers that the applicant has not provided sufficient information on the substance, it must inform the applicant of the reasons for not authorising or prohibiting its use in or on specific food or food categories;
information that is relevant to the assessment of the safety and the method of analysis used to determine the safety of the chemical;
where applicable, the analysis method(s) used must be made public.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2008/48/EC has applied since 20 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The common procedure is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and streamline EU food safety rules.
For more information, see:
EU rules on food safety (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Food and feed: food, feed, animal feed and animal feed.
* Animal feed: animal feed, meat, eggs or milk.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 179/2002 of 20 December 2002 establishing a European Union common procedure on the assessment, authorisation and monitoring of substances (OJ C 168, 20.10.2002, pp. 1-9)
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 168:
Accreditation, market surveillance, controls and conformity assessment of products from non-EU countries and the CE marking (until 31 December 2014)
Accreditation and market surveillance: accreditation, surveillance, checks and conformity assessments of products originating outside the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the accreditation of products entering the Community market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on accreditation*, market surveillance*, controls of products coming into the EU from third countries, as well as the European Commission’s role in monitoring the safety of these products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to products that are subject to EU harmonisation legislation (known as the ‘CE’ directive). It does not apply to products which are not covered by this directive.
It applies to the following sectors:
drug precursors, medical devices, medicinal products for human and veterinary use, motor vehicles and aviation;
motor vehicles;
electrical products;
bicyclic devices;
personal protective equipment (PPE);
electromagnetic compatibility devices (e.g. mobile phones, tablets, tablets and mobile phones);
medical devices.
EU countries must ensure that the products covered by the regulation are:
accredited by an accreditation body recognised by the Commission;
registered in the EU;
obliged to carry a CE marking.
The European Commission is responsible for monitoring the compliance of the products with the regulation.
National authorities must:
monitor the safety and quality of products on sale on the market;
monitor accidents and harm to health which are suspected to have been caused by products subject to the legislation;
ensure that corrective action has been taken;
follow up scientific and technical knowledge concerning safety issues.
In addition, EU countries must establish adequate procedures in order to:
report to the Commission any issues relating to risks arising in connection with the products that fall within the scope of the regulation;
check that corrective actions have been taken by the manufacturer or importer;
verify the corrective action taken.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
From 1 January 2010.

Summary 169:
EU-Iceland agreement on extradition for the purpose of prosecution or execution of sentence
EU-Ireland agreement on extraditable offences
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement between the European Union and the Kingdom of Norway and the Republic of Iceland on extradition
Decision 2006/852/EC on the conclusion of the Agreement between the EU and Iceland on the extradition for prosecution and execution of sentences
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement establishes a formal framework for the extradition of suspected or convicted criminals between the Member States of the European Economic Area (EEA) and Iceland.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The EU and Norway agree to cooperate in the field of extradition, taking account of, as minimum standards, the terms of the Convention of 27 September 1996 relating to extradition between EU countries.
Arrest warrant
An arrest warrant is issued by the judicial authorities of an EU country to a non-EU country for a person who has been convicted of a criminal offence. It must be transmitted to the competent judicial authority of the country of the issuing country. The country of origin of the arrest warrant must transmit the request for the person’s surrender to the country in which the person is being held. The executing country must then transmit the warrant to the requesting country.
In the case of an arrest warrant, the requesting the surrender of a person, the executing country sends a copy of the warrant and the requested person's name, address, date of birth and date of arrest. The requested. The requesting country then transmits the warrant. The issuing the warrant in the form of a declaration or notification to the issuing EU country. In the case where the country transmitting the warrant does not have the power to execute the warrant, it must inform the country sending the warrant of the reasons for the delay.
EU countries must ensure that:
the requested person has the right to legal counsel;
the person concerned is informed of the conditions under which the warrant can be executed.
If the requesting EU country does not transmit the execution warrant within the time limits set out in the agreement, the requested EU country must notify the country that issued it.
Where the executing EU country fails to transmit a warrant in accordance with the conditions laid down by the EU country issuing it, the request must be sent to that country. If the request is not transmitted within the specified time limits, the country which issued the warrant must immediately inform the issuing judicial authority thereof, giving reasons for its delay. In this case, the issuing party must inform Iceland and Norway of the reason for its decision.
Iceland and Norway must cooperate in order to ensure that the warrant is executed and that the person who is being surrendered is handed over to the authorities of their country of residence.
Requests for the surrender must be made in writing and must be accompanied by:
a description of the offence;
a list of the offences for which the request has been made;
an estimate of the period of detention to be served in the country where the person has been detained;
details of the duration of the detention.
When a request is refused, the person concerned must be informed of all the rights and obligations of the requesting party, including the possibility to appeal against the decision. The request must also include details of the procedure to be followed in the event of the refusal of the request.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
The Agreement entered into force on 28 June 2006. It has provisionally applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
 
  Iceland and the EU (European External Action Service).
For more information, see:
EU–Iceland extradition agreement (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS

Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 of 28 May 2006 on the signature and provisional application of an Agreement between Member States and the Government of the Kingdom on the Extradition of Persons Arrested for Prosecutions and the Execution of Judgments and on the Protection of the Rights and Freedoms of Persons Subject to Arrest Warrant of the Court of Justice of the State of Norway, and repealing Council Regulation (EU) No 852/2004 (OJC) No 1082/2004) (See amending act(s)

Summary 170:
Railway carriage charge: passenger rights and carriage charges (2007-2013)
Railway carriage charge: passengers’ rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2007 on the carriage charge
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on carriage charges applicable to passengers travelling by rail in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the consumer rights of railway passengers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
passengers travelling by railway to, from or from a railway station in an EU Member State;
travelers travelling between railway stations in a Member State other than the one in which they are registered.
It does not apply to the carriage of goods.
Carriage charge
The carriage charge applies to all passengers travelling on railway services between the railway stations of the Member State in which the passenger is registered. The carriage charge is calculated on the basis of:
the total cost of the ticket;
the ticket price; and
the carriage charge applied by the railway company to the passenger.
Exclusion of waiver and stipulation of limits
Excluded from the regulation are:
any waiver or stipulation on the amount of carriage charge applicable to a passenger’s baggage;
any limitation on the number of items of luggage that can be carried by the carrier.
A carriage charge may be waived or stipulated on a case-by-case basis if the item of luggage is:
deliberately lost or stolen;
delayed by more than 30 minutes;
unclaimed by the passenger;
not recovered within the specified time limit; or
delivered by the same carrier more than 1 year after the request for delivery.
If a passenger has not been claimed within 30 days of the date of delivery of the item, the carrier must dispose of it in accordance with the laws and prescriptions in force at the place where the item is situated.
The carrier must also dispose of the items of baggage recovered if it is not claimed within the period stated in paragraph 3 or if the carrier has not disposed of it more than one year after it is recovered.
Disputes between railway undertakings
A dispute between a railway company and a passenger concerning carriage charges may lead to a dispute between the company and the passenger concerning the carriage charges.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Carriage charges’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Passenger: any person travelling by train on a railway.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барканска блака  гракова   нашка
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2007 of 26 September 2007 on the payment of carriage charges relating to passenger journeys by railway (OJ L 127, 1.1.2008, pp. 1-8)

LIFE statistics

Summary 172:
EU energy statistics
EU Energy statistics
SUMMEMBER REFERENCES
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 on the transmission of energy statistics to the European Commission
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU energy statistics.
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) has reliable, comparable and up-to-date energy statistics for the EU’s energy sector.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
energy supply and consumption;
energy production and consumption of fossil fuels and manufactured gases used by autoproducers;
transmission of energy data to the Commission and the European Energy Market Observatory (EEMO).
It does not apply to the production of electricity.
EU countries’ energy statistics must be transmitted with the following frequencies:
annual, for the energy statistics in Annex A;
monthly or short-term monthly;
short-term quarterly or monthly for the information on energy consumption in Annex B.
Transmission format and method
The transmission format must conform to an appropriate interchange standard specified by Eurostat. It must also conform to the transmission format specified by the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom).
The EEMO must transmit the data in accordance with the format set out in Annex C.
The Commission must ensure that:
the transmission format conforms to the format specified in Annex D;
the data are transmitted to the following formats:
annex A, B, C, D, E and E;
Annex B, E, C and D are transmitted on the basis of the transmission frequency specified by Euratom.
EURatom must transmit data to Eurostat on a regular basis.
Imports and exports
Imported and exported energy must be reported by country of origin, and exports to the EU country of destination.
Exports and imports of energy from non-EU countries must also be reported.
In addition, EU countries must report on the imports and exports of:
transport and exports by country;
exports of energy products;
imports of electricity;
electricity production;
gas production; and
energy consumption of power plants.
Export and import statistics
The import statistics must cover the following sectors:
telecommunications and information technology (telecommunications, information technology, energy storage, energy efficiency and energy efficiency);
transportation and energy production (transport, energy production, energy consumption and energy consumption);
industrial and non-ferrous metals industries;
other sectors (energy sector, transport sector, energy sector);
energy efficiency (energy efficiency, energy recovery and energy recovery);
non-fossil fuels (energy production, electricity generation, electricity consumption, energy generation and energy distribution);
manufactured gases (manufacturing, transport and energy storage);
nuclear energy (nuclear, thermal, geothermal, photovoltaic and geothermal);
other energy sectors (transportation, energy distribution, energy supply, and energy generation).
Export statistics must include:
total energy imports;
total exports;
net imports of goods and services;
insurance premiums paid by EU countries;
inbound and outbound movements of goods;
revenue from energy imports.
Supply and consumption statistics
Energy statistics must also include the following categories of energy consumption:
gas and electricity (gas, electricity, heat, transport, etc.).
Electricity (electricity, gas and heat): electricity is supplied by:
power plants, including: nuclear power plants, hydroelectric power plants and hydro-electric plants;
power generation;
hydro-electricity (gas and heat);
gas;
nuclear power (gas);
steam (steam and heat).
Other energy sectors: electricity generation (gas) and heat (steam, electricity production).
Transport: electricity is transported by road, rail, inland waterways, inland lakes and waterways, and in coastal waters.
Other sectors: transport (railways, airports, ports, seaports, seagoing vessels) and inland waterways.
Energy production: energy is supplied to: industrial and commercial and public services, transport sectors, transport industries, transport operators, transport companies, energy producers, energy suppliers, energy consumers, energy traders, energy distributors, energy users.

It does not affect the rights under Directive 90/314/EEC.
Rights of passengers

Compensation in respect of wheelchairs and other mobility equipment
They may also benefit from:
accessibility and information;
compensation for wheelchairs;
access to accommodation, accommodation, food, clothes and transport;
refreshments;
medical assistance;
costs of travel;
the right to return to the Member State if the tour operator cancels a regular service.
In addition, passengers may benefit from the following:
refundishment of travel expenses;
reasonable funeral expenses
reasonable personal injury
loss of luggage
damage to luggage.
Directive 90/313/EC

Summary 174:
EU statistics on aquaculture and fisheries (2008-2020)
EU statistics (2008 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on EU statistics on the EU’s aquacultural sector
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to provide the European Union (EU) with reliable, comparable and up-to-date data on the contribution of the EU fisheries sector (aquaculture) to total fisheries production.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the total production of fish, crustaceans, molluscs and shellfish by EU countries;
the output from aquaciculture at first sale, including production from hatcheries and nurseries offered for sale.
It does not cover:
capture-based fishing;
production of aquacultures;
participation of non-EU countries’ fishing fleets in EU fisheries;
fishing areas for statistical purposes.
EU countries must submit a report to the European Commission every 2 years on the data they have collected.
The Commission publishes the report on the basis of the data it receives from each EU country.
Each EU country must send the Commission a detailed methodological report on how the data have been collected. The report must be sent to the Commission within 3 months of the end of the reference calendar year.
Aquaculture statistics
Each year, EU countries must provide the Commission with:
a list of the number of fish they have caught;
a description of the species they have captured;
an estimate of the value of the production by volume of each species.
Annex II of the regulation sets out the inputs and outputs for each species of fish.
In addition, each country must provide data on:
total production of shellfish;
output of crustacean species;
total volume of mollusk production;
number of shell fish caught.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE?
It applies from 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality of EU fisheries statistics. These include:
rules on the collection and processing of data;
rules for the use of the European Fisheries Fund (EFS);
rules governing the collection, processing and dissemination of data on EU fisheries.
For more information, see:
EU fisheries statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Aquaculture: the practice of collecting specimens from the wild and their subsequent use in aquacry.
* Production: the output of fish and mollusc species that are caught by fishing operations.

Summary 175:
Conservation and management of fishery resources in the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation (NATO) Convention Area (NATO Convention Area)
Convention Area Conservation and Management of Fishery Resources
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the implementation of the Convention Area Conservation & Management Scheme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the convention’s conservation and management measures are implemented in accordance with the UN Convention on the Law of the Sea (Nato Convention).
It sets out the rules and procedures for implementing the convention.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels flying the flag of a contracting party to the convention area. It does not apply to:
non-contracting parties, such as non-EU countries and non-European Union (EU) countries;
fishing vessels flying a flag of an EU country;
ships flying a non-Nato signatory.
EU participation
EU countries are required to participate in the convention by:
participating in the monitoring and enforcement of measures adopted by the convention;
carrying out inspections and joint fishing operations;
ensuring compliance with the convention rules and measures;
providing technical assistance and assistance to other contracting parties.
Ensuring compliance of non-Contracting parties with the regulation.
Rules and procedures
Each contracting party must:
ensure that:
its vessels comply with all the rules, regulations and procedures laid down in the regulation;
participate in the control and enforcement measures adopted under the convention, including:
monitoring and reporting on catches;
keeping a register of vessels’ movements and quantities of fish caught;
cooperate with other parties to ensure compliance;
take measures to prevent, detect and deter illegal fishing;
keep records of catches and quantities held on board.
Monitoring and enforcement
The convention sets out:
rules and procedures to be followed by the contracting parties;
measures to be taken by non-contractors to ensure the compliance of vessels flying their flag;
rules to be applied to vessels flying an EU flag.
Cooperation between contracting parties
The contracting parties must cooperate in order to ensure:
the implementation of measures to conserve and manage fisheries;
the protection of the marine environment;
reciprocal access to fishing grounds;
protection of marine ecosystems; and
cooperation with non-member countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 May 2008.
BACKGROUND
The Convention provides for an appropriate framework for multilateral cooperation on the rational conservation, management and sustainable use of fisheries in the area defined by the Convention (the Convention Area). The EU is a member of the convention and participates in its implementation.
For more information, see:
North Atlantic Convention (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Convention Area: the area covered by the Nato Convention.

Summary 176:
EU Ecolabel for food and feed — rules on ecolabel classification
EU EColabel for foods and feed – rules on eco-label classification
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the European Union ecolabels
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the European Commission’s (EC) eColabel scheme.
It aims to improve the quality and transparency of products and to ensure that they are labelled as organic.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
food and feed products intended for human consumption;
animal feed and feed intended for animal feed;
eggs, egg products, milk products, poultry and eggs;
seaweed and seaweed intended for aquaculture.
Ecolabel criteria
The European Commission sets out rules on the development and revision of EU Ecolabel criteria. These are based on the results of a study carried out by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA). The results of the study are published in the Official Journal of the EU.
The Commission decides, taking into account the outcome of this study and the opinion of the European Economic and Social Committee (EFSC), which advises the Commission on developing and revising the criteria, on the basis of the findings of the EFSA study. The Commission then forwards the results to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Environment Agency (Euratom).
Eligible products
Products eligible for award of an EU EColourity
Products that are certified as organic must comply with the following a conformity assessment carried out in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation. Products that do not comply with these rules are not eligible for the award of the ecolabilabel. However, the Commission may consider the option that only those products that meet the requirements of the regulation may be awarded the EU colabel.
Consultation procedure
A European Commission working group meets at least twice a year to develop and update the EU Eligibility criteria. The working group is chaired by the Commission and comprises representatives from the Commission, the European Board of Food Safety Authorities (EEA) and representatives of producers, importers, distributors and retailers.
EU conformity assessment procedure
Each EU country must designate a competent body to carry out the conformity assessment of products. The competent body must be independent of the organisation or the product it assesses. It must have at its disposal the necessary technical knowledge and sufficient and appropriate experience to perform the tasks it performs.
At all times and for each conformity assessment procedures and each kind or category of products in relation to which it has been designated, the competent body is responsible for ensuring the transparency and the ability of reproduction of those procedures.
In addition, it must ensure that:
the competent body has the necessary:
technical know-how and sufficient experience;
descriptions of the procedures it carries out;
the ability to reproduce those procedures;
transparency and reproducibility of the results;
records of the information it has produced.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU ecolabus has been in place since 2000.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Successive amendments to Regulation (EURatom) no 550/13 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 177:
Protection of patents and related patents in the European Union (EU) — codification of Regulation (EC) No 552/2009
Protection and related patent certificates in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/33/EC on the protection of patents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It codifies and repeals Directive 2001/83/EC (see summary).
It aims to ensure that patents granted by the European Patent Office (EPO) are protected by a European patent.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to patents granted in accordance with the EU’s general patent law. It does not apply to:
patents granted by EU countries’ national patent offices;
powers conferred on the EPO by international organisations, such as the World Intellectual Property Organisation (WTO) or the European Research Council (ERC);
presents patents to the European Commission.
It does not cover:
the protection granted to the holder of the basic patent or his successor in title;
the patent covered by the certificate.
The duration of the protection granted by a patent must be such as to provide adequate effective protection. It must not exceed 5 years.
Duration of the certificate
The certificate must be valid for a period of 5 years from the date on which it is granted. It may be renewed if the patent holder surrenders it or if the product covered by it no longer meets the conditions laid down in the regulation.
Application for a patent law
An application must be submitted to the EPO for a certificate by a research centre located in one of the EU countries. The applicant must be able to demonstrate that:
a patent is essential for the development of a new product;
a product is likely to have a significant impact on the environment or human health;
an application for the certificate concerns a product that could have a major impact on human health or the environment.
Applying for a CE marking
The applicant must submit an application to the authority responsible for issuing the CE marking. The authority decides whether to grant the certificate or to reject it if the application or the product to which it relates does not meet the conditions set out in this regulation. The decision is binding on all EU countries and applies to certificates granted before 1 January 1995.
Procedure for granting a certificate
A certificate is granted to a patent holder for the purpose of:
protecting the intellectual property rights of a person, organisation or business;
providing a legal basis for research, development and testing of products;
promoting the development and commercialisation of products covered by a certificate;
ensuring that the certificate is valid for at least 5 years after the end of the 5-year period of protection.
Expiry of the patent
The validity of a patent may be terminated at any time by the holder if:
it is no longer in use;
it has not been commercially viable; or
the product no longer satisfies the conditions of the regulation;
or
the holder has not paid the annual fee.

Summary 178:
Convention on the enforcement of judgments in civil and commercial matters
Convention of the Lugano Convention
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2016/852 on the conclusion of the Convention on the Enforcement of Judgments in Civil and Commercial Matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE CONVENTION AND OF THE DECISION?
The convention aims to ensure that judgments are enforceable in all matters of law in the European Union (EU).
The decision concludes the convention on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The Convention applies to all disputes arising from:
contractual obligations arising from a contract or agreement between a party and a third party;
the rights and obligations of a party in relation to the property, goods or services of another party.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Convention entered into force on 1 July 2016.
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of legal instruments to protect the rights and interests of consumers and businesses.
For more information, see:
‘The EU’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
  
Council Decision (EC) No 852/2016 of 25 June 2016 on the signing, on behalf the European Community, of the Conventions on the enforceability of judgments and judgments in Civil & Commercial Matters (Lugano Convention) (OJ L 136, 1.7.2016, pp. 1-8)
Conventions on enforcement of judgements in civil & commercial matters (Ludano convention)
Council decision (EU-Ludon Convention of the Council of the European Confederation of the Nations of the Regions, Euratom Decision (EU/EU) of 26 June 2016 concerning the conclusion, on the basis of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union, of Article 79 of the convention.
